The Four Additional Moods of the First Figure
Lists additional moods and notes scholarly disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the additional moods accepted by later logicians and explain why early logicians rejected them.

Core Idea
The first figure's moods expanded from five to eight across logical tradition.

The passage presents the remaining four productive moods of the first figure according to later scholars, and then contrasts with the earlier restriction to five. Understanding this dispute helps you see how logical systems evolve through critique and refinement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four moods
The text lists: (1) particular affirmative minor with universal negative major, (2) both premises negative (i.e., minor negative, major negative?) with universal affirmative major, (3) universal negative minor with particular affirmative major. Actually, the commentator clarifies these produce three moods plus one more (given first). Count them as four.
The first mood listed is 'Al-juz'iyya ma'a al-saliba al-kulliyya'.
2
Analyse the conditions for each mood
For a mood to be productive, it must satisfy certain conditions about quantity and quality. The commentator explains: if minor is universal negative, major must be universal or particular affirmative; if minor is particular negative, major must be universal affirmative only.
These conditions ensure the middle term is distributed at least once.
3
Contrast early and later views
Early logicians (al-mutaqaddimun) held only five moods are productive. Later logicians (al-muta'akhkhirun) accepted eight. The passage mentions Ibn al-Hajib as adhering to the earlier view.
The text says early scholars 'dropped the productivity of the minor' (isqat intaj al-sughra), possibly referring to moods with negative minor.
4
Apply the condition to a real example
Take an Islamic example: 'Some animal is not human (particular negative), Every human is rational (universal affirmative).' Could this yield a valid conclusion? According to later scholars, yes: 'Therefore some animal is not rational'? Actually, we need to check the mood: minor particular negative, major universal affirmative is one of the additional moods.
The conclusion would be 'Some animal is not rational', but that is false. However, logical validity does not require truth of premises; it only concerns form. This shows the importance of distinguishing validity from truth.

(قَوْلُهُ: كُلُّ ب ج ، وَبَعْضُ أ لَيْسَ (ب) أي كلُّ إنسان حيوان وبعضُ الحيوان ليس بإنسان . (قَوْلُهُ : فَبَعْضُ ج لَيْسَ (أ) أي : فبعض الحيوان ليس بحجر (قَوْلُهُ : وَالسَّالِبَةُ الْكُلِّيَّةُ مَعَ الْمُوْجِبَةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ) أي: على العبارةِ الصَّحيحةِ الَّتي قالَها الشَّارِحُ لإصلاح المتن.

As for the remaining productive moods, they are also four, understood from his saying: 'And the particular with the universal negative, and the two negatives with the universal affirmative, and the universal negative with the particular affirmative.' And its detail: (His saying: 'Every B is J, and some A is not B') i.e., every human is an animal and some animal is not human. (His saying: 'Therefore some J is not A') i.e., therefore some animal is not stone. (His saying: 'The universal negative with the particular affirmative') i.e., according to the correct expression which the commentator stated to correct the text. (His saying: 'And the two negatives with the universal affirmative, and the universal negative with the particular affirmative') means: if the minor is universal negative, then the major must be universal affirmative or particular affirmative, and cannot be universal or particular negative, because both conditions are violated. And if it is particular negative, then the major must be universal affirmative only, and cannot be particular negative, or universal negative, or particular affirmative, because both or one condition is violated. And it should be known that this detail is only according to the later scholars. As for the earlier scholars, the productive moods for this figure are five, and according to Ibn al-Hajib he dropped eleven? Indeed, the earlier scholars also dropped the productivity of the minor.

Commentary
This passage presents the remaining four productive moods of the first figure according to later scholars, and then contrasts their view with that of earlier scholars. The text first lists the moods in a condensed form: (1) particular affirmative minor with universal negative major, (2) both premises negative (minor negative, major negative?) with universal affirmative major, (3) universal negative minor with particular affirmative major. Actually, the phrasing 'والسالبتان مع الموجبة الكلية' is ambiguous: it may mean two moods where the minor is negative and the major is either universal or particular affirmative? The commentator explains: if the minor is universal negative, then the major must be universal affirmative or particular affirmative (so two moods). If the minor is particular negative, the major must be universal affirmative only (one mood). That totals three, but they said four? Possibly the fourth is the one with particular minor and universal negative major mentioned first. However, the main point is the critical refinement: later scholars accept these eight moods, but earlier scholars (like Ibn al-Hajib) held that only five moods are productive. The exact number dropped is unclear due to textual corruption ('فا إحدى عشرة' might mean 'dropped eleven' from some larger set). The passage highlights an ongoing scholarly debate within the logical tradition about the valid moods of the first figure. The commentary by al-Dasuqi and the footnote by al-`Attar (correcting an example) show the meticulous attention to detail in these discussions.

Key Takeaway
Later scholars expanded the first figure's productive moods from five to eight by accepting moods with a negative minor premise, showing that logical rules can be refined over time.

Eight Productive Moods of the Fourth Figure
Enumerates eight productive moods of the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight productive moods of the fourth figure and identify the quantity and quality of their premises.

Core Idea
The fourth figure yields eight valid moods, each with a specific combination of premise quantities and qualities.

In this passage, the author explains the eight productive moods (ḍurūb muntijah) of the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. These moods are derived from two general rules: the universal affirmative minor with four types of major, and three additional combinations involving negatives. By mastering these, you will be able to recognize valid syllogisms in Islamic logical texts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the fourth figure structure
Recall that in the fourth figure, the middle term is the predicate of the minor premise and the subject of the major premise. This determines the position of terms in the conclusion.
The minor premise contains the subject of the conclusion; the major contains the predicate.
2
Identify the first four moods from the rule 'universal affirmative with the four'
The text states that when the minor is universal affirmative, four moods are productive depending on the major: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative (though only some of these are valid as per the details).
The author compresses this as 'المنتجة الموجبة الكلية مع الأربع'.
3
Extract the three additional moods from the second rule
The second rule mentions 'the particular with the universal negative, both negatives with the universal affirmative, and the universal negative with the particular affirmative.' This yields three distinct moods: (1) minor particular affirmative + major universal negative → particular negative; (2) minor universal negative + major universal affirmative → universal negative; (3) minor particular negative + major universal affirmative → particular negative.
The fourth mood from this set is actually already covered in the first four? The text says 'four' but lists three? It says 'the remaining four' and then gives three? Actually it says 'the remaining four' but then details three? Possibly the fourth is the minor universal affirmative with particular negative already mentioned. The passage should be read carefully. For this lesson, we focus on the total of eight: the first four implied and the next four explicitly, but the latter are three? Let's reconcile: The author says 'four from the first rule' and 'four from the second rule' making eight. The second rule actually lists three combinations? But it says 'the particular with the universal negative, the two negatives with the universal affirmative, and the universal negative with the particular affirmative' — that's three, but the fourth is the minor universal affirmative with particular negative from the first set? Actually it says 'فهذه أربعة' from the first statement, and 'فأربعة أيضاً' from the second statement, but the second statement gives three? Possibly the second statement includes four because 'السالبتان مع الموجبة الكلية' counts as one (two negatives with one affirmative), and 'السالبة الكلية مع الموجبة الجزئية' is another, and 'الجزئية مع السالبة الكلية' is another, and then maybe one more? We'll follow the text's explicit enumeration: it then gives three examples: minor particular aff. + major univ. neg., minor univ. neg. + major univ. aff., minor part. neg. + major univ. aff. That is three, but the commentary by al-Attar says 'إذا ضُمَّتْ إلى الثمانية' implying eight total. So we accept eight. For this lesson, we list them as per the standard later count: (1) AOO-4, (2) EAE-4, (3) IEO-4? Actually need to check standard moods. For simplicity, we present the examples given.
4
Verify the conclusions using conversion
Some moods require conversion of the conclusion or premises. For example, the mood minor universal affirmative + major particular negative yields a particular negative conclusion directly. Check that the conclusion follows from the premises by applying the rules of categorical syllogism.
Refer to the examples: 'Every B is C, some A is not B, so some C is not A.'

العطار
السالبة الجزئية معَ الكُبرى الموجبة الكُلِّيَّةِ ، والصُّغرى الموجبة الكُلِّيَّةِ مع الكُبرى السالبة الجزئيَّةِ ، والصُّغرى السَّالبةِ الكُلِّيَّةِ معَ الكُبرى الموجبة الجزئية، فإذا صُمَّتْ إلى الثَّمانيةِ ؛ يكونُ مَجموعُ السَّاقط : إحدى عشرة ؛ لأنَّ بيان هذهِ الثَّلاثةِ على انعكاس السالبة الجزئية، والمتقدّمونَ لَمَّا اعتقدوا عدم انعكاسها ؛ حصرُوا الضُّروب المنتجة في الشكل الرابع في الخمسة، وبيَّنُوا عُقْمَ هذهِ الثَّلاثة بالاختلافِ في النتيجة، وأجابَ المتأخّرُونَ : بأنَّ بيان الاختلافِ في هذهِ القُروبِ إِنَّما يتمُّ إذا كانَ القياسُ مُركَّباً مِنَ المقدِّماتِ البسيطة، لكنَّه يشترط في إنتاجها أن تكونَ السَّالبة المستعملة فيها من إحدى الخاصَّتين، وقد تقدَّمَ أنَّها تنعكسُ فَلَا تَنْهَضُ تِلْكَ النُّقُوضُ فِيهَا .
Chapter: On Syllogism. The minor universal affirmative with the major particular negative produces a particular negative, as we say: Every (B) is (C), and some (A) is not (B), so some (C) is not (A). So these are four types understood from his statement: 'The universal affirmative produces with the four.' As for the remaining productive types, they are also four, understood from his statement: 'The particular with the universal negative, the two negatives with the universal affirmative, and the universal negative with the particular affirmative.' And their detail: Al-Dasuqi: His saying: 'Every B is C, and some A is not B' means every human is an animal and some animal is not human. His saying: 'So some C is not A' means some animal is not stone. His saying: 'The universal negative with the particular affirmative' means according to the correct expression that the commentator stated to correct the text. Al-Attar: His saying: 'Both negatives with the universal affirmative and the universal negative with the particular affirmative' means: If the minor is universal negative, then the major is universal affirmative or particular affirmative, and it cannot be universal or particular negative because both conditions are absent in them. If it is particular negative, then the major is only universal affirmative, and it cannot be particular negative, universal negative, or particular affirmative because both or one of the conditions is absent. And it should be known that this detail is according to the opinion of the later scholars. As for the opinion of the earlier scholars, the productive types for this figure are five, and according to him is Ibn al-Hajib, so the fallacies are eleven. So the earlier scholars also dropped the production of the minor particular affirmative with the major universal negative, producing a particular negative, as we say: Some (B) is (C), and nothing of (A) is (B), so some (C) is not (A). And the minor universal negative with the major universal affirmative produces a universal negative, as we say: Nothing of (B) is (C), and every (A) is (B), so nothing of (C) is (A). And the minor particular negative with the major universal affirmative produces a particular negative, as we say: Some (B) is not (C), and every (A) is (B), so some (C) is not (A). Al-Dasuqi: His saying: 'Some B is C, and nothing of A is B' means some human is animal, and nothing of stone is human. His saying: 'So some C is not A' means some animal is not stone. His saying: 'Nothing of B is C, and every A is B' means nothing of human is stone, and every rational is human. His saying: 'So nothing of C is A' means nothing of stone is rational. His saying: 'Some B is not C, and every A is B' means some human is not stone, and every rational is human. His saying: 'So some C is not A' means some stone is not rational. Al-Attar: The particular negative with the universal affirmative, and the universal affirmative with the particular negative, and the universal negative with the particular affirmative. So if they are added to the eight, the total dropped becomes eleven; because the demonstration of these three depends on the conversion of the particular negative. The earlier scholars, when they believed it does not convert, limited the productive types in the fourth figure to five, and showed the sterility of these three by difference in the conclusion. The later scholars answered: That the demonstration of difference in these types is only valid if the syllogism is composed of simple premises, but it is required for their production that the negative used in them be from the two special [premises], and it has been established that it converts, so those objections are not valid against them.
Source pages p. 833

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the productive moods (al-ḍurūb al-muntijah) of the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. It opens with a chapter heading 'On Syllogism'. The author first states that when the minor premise is universal affirmative and the major is particular negative, the conclusion is particular negative, giving the example: 'Every B is C, and some A is not B, therefore some C is not A.' He then notes that this is one of four types understood from a compressed statement: 'The universal affirmative produces with the four' — meaning the universal affirmative minor combined with each of the four types of major (though not all are productive). Then he mentions that the remaining four productive types are also understood from another compressed statement: 'The particular with the universal negative, the two negatives with the universal affirmative, and the universal negative with the particular affirmative.' The commentary by al-Dasuqi and al-Attar clarifies these. Al-Dasuqi explains the example: 'Every human is an animal, and some animal is not human, therefore some animal is not stone' (with a note correcting the example: the major should be 'some stone is not human' but the text has 'some animal is not human' — a likely OCR/transmission error). Al-Attar then analyses the conditions: if the minor is universal negative, the major can be universal or particular affirmative; if the minor is particular negative, the major can only be universal affirmative. He stresses that this is according to the later scholars (al-mutaʼakhkhirūn), while the earlier scholars (al-mutaqaddimūn) held that the fourth figure has only five productive moods. Following this, the text enumerates three additional productive moods recognized by the later scholars: (1) minor particular affirmative with major universal negative → particular negative; (2) minor universal negative with major universal affirmative → universal negative; (3) minor particular negative with major universal affirmative → particular negative. Al-Dasuqi gives examples for each: (1) 'Some human is animal, nothing of stone is human, therefore some animal is not stone'; (2) 'Nothing of human is stone, every rational is human, therefore nothing of stone is rational'; (3) 'Some human is not stone, every rational is human, therefore some stone is not rational.' Finally, al-Attar explains the dispute: the earlier scholars rejected these three moods because they involve the particular negative premise, which they believed did not convert (as required for the syllogism to produce a conclusion). The later scholars responded that the particular negative does convert, so the objection fails. Thus, the total number of productive moods in the fourth figure is eight according to the later scholars, while the earlier count was five, dropping eleven invalid moods.

Key Takeaway

The Syllogism

The Dispute over the Number of Fourth Figure Moods
Discusses the dispute on the number of productive moods in the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why earlier logicians counted only five productive moods for the fourth figure and how the later logicians expanded the count to eight by accepting the conversion of the particular negative.

Core Idea
The disagreement hinges on the convertibility of the particular negative proposition.

The passage reveals a methodological dispute in the history of Islamic logic: earlier logicians, relying on a stricter rule that the particular negative (O) does not convert, recognized only five productive moods in the fourth figure. Later logicians, accepting the conversion of O, added three more moods, bringing the total to eight. Understanding this debate clarifies how logical doctrine develops through problematizing basic operations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the earlier logicians' position
The text states that earlier logicians (المتقدّمون) considered only five moods productive in the fourth figure. They rejected three additional moods that involve the particular negative premise because they believed the particular negative does not convert.
Ibn al-Hajib is mentioned as holding this view.
2
Understand the role of conversion of the particular negative
The disputed moods require the conversion of the particular negative premise to derive the conclusion. If conversion is not allowed, the syllogism fails to produce a valid conclusion. The earlier logicians argued that the particular negative does not convert because it would change the quantity.
Conversion of a particular negative (O) yields a particular negative (O) – actually O converts to itself? The standard Aristotelian view is that O does not convert; later logicians argued it does convert.
3
Examine the three additional moods
The later logicians added: (1) minor particular affirmative + major universal negative; (2) minor universal negative + major universal affirmative; (3) minor particular negative + major universal affirmative. These three involve a negative premise that is either major or minor.
The text gives examples with human, animal, stone, rational.
4
Analyze the later logicians' response
The later logicians (المتأخّرون) responded by arguing that the demonstration of difference (نقوض) only holds if the syllogism uses simple premises, but these moods require the negative to be from the خاصّتين (special premises? Or the two special [converted] forms?). They claimed that the particular negative does convert, so the objections are invalid.
The phrase 'من إحدى الخاصّتين' likely refers to the two special types of conversion? Possibly meaning the negative is used in a converted form.
5
Assess the significance of the dispute
This dispute shows how logical principles are refined over time. The acceptance of conversion of the particular negative by later logicians allowed a more comprehensive account of valid syllogisms, leading to eight moods in the fourth figure.
The total number of dropped moods (ساقط) is eleven if we count all invalid combinations.

Chapter: On Syllogism. The minor universal affirmative with the major particular negative produces a particular negative, as we say: Every (B) is (C), and some (A) is not (B), so some (C) is not (A). So these are four types understood from his statement: 'The universal affirmative produces with the four.' As for the remaining productive types, they are also four, understood from his statement: 'The particular with the universal negative, the two negatives with the universal affirmative, and the universal negative with the particular affirmative.' And their detail: Al-Dasuqi: His saying: 'Every B is C, and some A is not B' means every human is an animal and some animal is not human. His saying: 'So some C is not A' means some animal is not stone. His saying: 'The universal negative with the particular affirmative' means according to the correct expression that the commentator stated to correct the text. Al-Attar: His saying: 'Both negatives with the universal affirmative and the universal negative with the particular affirmative' means: If the minor is universal negative, then the major is universal affirmative or particular affirmative, and it cannot be universal or particular negative because both conditions are absent in them. If it is particular negative, then the major is only universal affirmative, and it cannot be particular negative, universal negative, or particular affirmative because both or one of the conditions is absent. And it should be known that this detail is according to the opinion of the later scholars. As for the opinion of the earlier scholars, the productive types for this figure are five, and according to him is Ibn al-Hajib, so the fallacies are eleven. So the earlier scholars also dropped the production of the minor particular affirmative with the major universal negative, producing a particular negative, as we say: Some (B) is (C), and nothing of (A) is (B), so some (C) is not (A). And the minor universal negative with the major universal affirmative produces a universal negative, as we say: Nothing of (B) is (C), and every (A) is (B), so nothing of (C) is (A). And the minor particular negative with the major universal affirmative produces a particular negative, as we say: Some (B) is not (C), and every (A) is (B), so some (C) is not (A). Al-Dasuqi: His saying: 'Some B is C, and nothing of A is B' means some human is animal, and nothing of stone is human. His saying: 'So some C is not A' means some animal is not stone. His saying: 'Nothing of B is C, and every A is B' means nothing of human is stone, and every rational is human. His saying: 'So nothing of C is A' means nothing of stone is rational. His saying: 'Some B is not C, and every A is B' means some human is not stone, and every rational is human. His saying: 'So some C is not A' means some stone is not rational. Al-Attar: The particular negative with the universal affirmative, and the universal affirmative with the particular negative, and the universal negative with the particular affirmative. So if they are added to the eight, the total dropped becomes eleven; because the demonstration of these three depends on the conversion of the particular negative. The earlier scholars, when they believed it does not convert, limited the productive types in the fourth figure to five, and showed the sterility of these three by difference in the conclusion. The later scholars answered: That the demonstration of difference in these types is only valid if the syllogism is composed of simple premises, but it is required for their production that the negative used in them be from the two special [premises], and it has been established that it converts, so those objections are not valid against them.
Source pages p. 833

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the productive moods (al-ḍurūb al-muntijah) of the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. It opens with a chapter heading 'On Syllogism'. The author first states that when the minor premise is universal affirmative and the major is particular negative, the conclusion is particular negative, giving the example: 'Every B is C, and some A is not B, therefore some C is not A.' He then notes that this is one of four types understood from a compressed statement: 'The universal affirmative produces with the four' — meaning the universal affirmative minor combined with each of the four types of major (though not all are productive). Then he mentions that the remaining four productive types are also understood from another compressed statement: 'The particular with the universal negative, the two negatives with the universal affirmative, and the universal negative with the particular affirmative.' The commentary by al-Dasuqi and al-Attar clarifies these. Al-Dasuqi explains the example: 'Every human is an animal, and some animal is not human, therefore some animal is not stone' (with a note correcting the example: the major should be 'some stone is not human' but the text has 'some animal is not human' — a likely OCR/transmission error). Al-Attar then analyses the conditions: if the minor is universal negative, the major can be universal or particular affirmative; if the minor is particular negative, the major can only be universal affirmative. He stresses that this is according to the later scholars (al-mutaʼakhkhirūn), while the earlier scholars (al-mutaqaddimūn) held that the fourth figure has only five productive moods. Following this, the text enumerates three additional productive moods recognized by the later scholars: (1) minor particular affirmative with major universal negative → particular negative; (2) minor universal negative with major universal affirmative → universal negative; (3) minor particular negative with major universal affirmative → particular negative. Al-Dasuqi gives examples for each: (1) 'Some human is animal, nothing of stone is human, therefore some animal is not stone'; (2) 'Nothing of human is stone, every rational is human, therefore nothing of stone is rational'; (3) 'Some human is not stone, every rational is human, therefore some stone is not rational.' Finally, al-Attar explains the dispute: the earlier scholars rejected these three moods because they involve the particular negative premise, which they believed did not convert (as required for the syllogism to produce a conclusion). The later scholars responded that the particular negative does convert, so the objection fails. Thus, the total number of productive moods in the fourth figure is eight according to the later scholars, while the earlier count was five, dropping eleven invalid moods.

Key Takeaway
The number of productive moods in the fourth figure depends on whether the conversion of the particular negative is accepted, illustrating how logical doctrines evolve through debate.

Moods of the Fourth Figure and Proof by Contradiction
Four productive moods of the fourth figure and al-khalf method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid fourth figure syllogism and apply the method of al-khalf to prove its conclusion.

Core Idea
The fourth figure's moods require al-khalf because direct reduction fails without conversion of the particular negative.

This passage from Tadhheeb al-Mantiq enumerates eight productive moods of the fourth figure and introduces the proof method of al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum). The author explicitly lists four moods and then discusses a historical debate about the validity of three additional moods. Finally, he explains how al-khalf works by taking the contradictory of the conclusion and combining it with one premise to derive a contradiction with the other premise. You will learn to identify these moods and apply proof by contradiction to establish conclusions in the fourth figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
In a fourth figure syllogism, the middle term is the predicate in the minor premise and the subject in the major. For the first mood: minor 'Some B is C', major 'No A is B', conclusion 'Some C is not A'. Note the arrangement of terms.
Use the Arabic variables (أ، ب، ج) as placeholders to track the terms.
2
Determine the mood
Specify the quantity and quality of each premise. For the first mood: minor is particular affirmative (I), major is universal negative (E), conclusion is particular negative (O). This is mood IEO.
The remaining moods follow similar patterns: EAE, IOE, and EAO.
3
Understand the controversy
Earlier scholars rejected three moods because they thought the particular negative does not convert. Later scholars argued that the necessary premises are already converted, so the objections fail.
This shows how logical disputes hinge on the validity of immediate inferences like conversion.
4
Apply al-khalf
Take the contradictory of the conclusion and combine it with one of the premises to produce a contradiction with the other premise. For the first mood, the contradictory of 'Some C is not A' is 'All C is A'. Combine with the minor 'Some B is C' to get 'Some B is A' (by first figure), which contradicts the major 'No A is B'.
The contradiction demonstrates the original conclusion must be true.
5
Verify the deduction
Check that the derived contradiction is indeed the contradictory of the other premise. In the example, 'Some B is A' contradicts 'No A is B' (I vs. E). The proof is complete.
Ensure the middle term is properly placed in the auxiliary syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنَّ الصُّغرى الموجبة الجزئية مع الكبرى السالبة الكلية، تنتج سالبة جزئيَّةٌ، كَقَولِنَا : بعضُ (ب) (ج) ، ولا شيء من (أ) (ب)، فبعضُ (ج) ليس (أ)) . والصُّغرى السَّالبة الكُلِّيَّةَ مع الكُبرى الموجبة الكلية، تنتج سالبة كلّيَّةٌ ، كَقولنا : لا شيء من (ب) (ج ) وكلَّ (أ) (ب)، فلا شيء من (ج) (أ)) والصغرى السالبة الجزئية مع الكُبرى الموجبة الكلية، تنتج سالبة جزئيةً، كَقولنا : بعض (ب) ليس (ج) ، وكلَّ (أ) (ب)، فبعض (ج) ليس (أ)) السالبة الجزئية معَ الكُبرى الموجبة الكُلِّيَّةِ ، والصُّغرى الموجبة الكُلِّيَّةِ مع الكُبرى السالبة الجزئيَّةِ ، والصُّغرى السَّالبةِ الكُلِّيَّةِ معَ الكُبرى الموجبة الجزئية، فإذا صُمَّتْ إلى الثَّمانيةِ ؛ يكونُ مَجموعُ السَّاقط : إحدى عشرة ؛ لأنَّ بيان هذهِ الثَّلاثةِ على انعكاس السالبة الجزئية، والمتقدّمونَ لَمَّا اعتقدوا عدم انعكاسها ؛ حصرُوا الضُّروب المنتجة في الشكل الرّابع في الخمسة، وبيَّنُوا عُقْمَ هذهِ الثَّلاثة بالاختلافِ في النتيجة، وأجابَ المتأخّرُونَ : بأنَّ بيان الاختلافِ في هذهِ القُروبِ إِنَّما يتمُّ إذا كانَ القياسُ مُركَّباً مِنَ المقدِّماتِ البسيطة، لكنَّه يشترط في إنتاجها أن تكونَ السَّالبة المستعملة فيها من إحدى الخاصَّتين، وقد تقدَّمَ أنَّها تنعكسُ فَلَا تنهضُ تلكَ النُّقوضُ فيها . والصُّغرى السالبة الكلّيّة مع الكبرى الموجبة الجزئية، تنتج سالبة جزئيَّةٌ ، كقولنا : «لا شيء من (ب) (ج) ، وبعضُ (أ) (ب)، فبعض (ج) ليس (أ)) . ثمَّ هذه الصُّروبُ الثَّمانية، إنَّما تنتج بِالخُلْفِ)، وهو في هذا الشكل أن يُؤخذ نقيضُ النَّتيجة، ويُضَمَّ إلى إحدى المقدمتين؛ لينتج ما ينعكس إلى نقيض المقدّمة الأخرى . فَفِي بعض الضُّروبِ يُجعَلُ نقيضُ النَّتيجة كُبرى وصغرى القياس؛ لينتج ما يُنافي الكُبرى، وفي بعضها يُجعَلُ نقيضُ النَّتيجة صُغرى، وكُبرى القياس كُبرى، لينتج ما يُنافي الصُّغرى.
That the particular affirmative minor premise with the universal negative major premise produces a particular negative conclusion, as our saying: Some B is C, and no A is B, so some C is not A. And the universal negative minor premise with the universal affirmative major premise produces a universal negative conclusion, as our saying: No B is C, and every A is B, so no C is A. And the particular negative minor premise with the universal affirmative major premise produces a particular negative conclusion, as our saying: Some B is not C, and every A is B, so some C is not A. The particular negative with the universal affirmative major, and the universal affirmative minor with the particular negative major, and the universal negative minor with the particular affirmative major – if these are added to the eight, then the total of the invalid (moods) becomes eleven; because the demonstration of these three depends on the conversion of the particular negative. The earlier scholars, since they believed it does not convert, restricted the productive moods in the fourth figure to five, and demonstrated the sterility of these three by difference in the result. The later scholars replied: the demonstration of difference in these moods is only complete if the syllogism is composed of simple premises, but it is required for their productivity that the negative used in them be one of the two particular (propositions). It has preceded that it converts, so those objections are not valid. And the universal negative minor premise with the particular affirmative major premise produces a particular negative conclusion, as our saying: No B is C, and some A is B, so some C is not A. Then these eight moods only produce by al-khalf (reductio). It is in this figure that the contradictory of the conclusion is taken and joined to one of the two premises so that something is produced that converts to the contradictory of the other premise. In some moods, the contradictory of the conclusion is made the major premise and the minor of the syllogism to produce something that contradicts the major. In some moods, the contradictory of the conclusion is made the minor premise and the major of the syllogism is made the major, to produce something that contradicts the minor.
Source pages p. 834

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the productive moods of the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. It first enumerates three explicit moods: (1) particular affirmative minor with universal negative major yields a particular negative; (2) universal negative minor with universal affirmative major yields a universal negative; (3) particular negative minor with universal affirmative major yields a particular negative. After mentioning these, the author notes that when combined with three other moods (particular negative with universal affirmative major, universal affirmative minor with particular negative major, universal negative minor with particular affirmative major), together with two more to make eight, the total of alleged unproductive moods according to earlier scholars would be eleven. The earlier scholars believed the particular negative does not convert, so they rejected these three moods and accepted only five. The later scholars rebut this by noting that the argument against these moods relies on the premise that the syllogism is composed of simple (unconverted) premises, but in these moods the negative used is one of the two special propositions (which are particular), and it has been shown that the particular negative does convert (to a particular negative), so the objections are invalid. The author then gives a fourth explicit mood: universal negative minor with particular affirmative major yields a particular negative. Finally, the author explains that all eight moods of the fourth figure produce their conclusions by al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum). The method is to take the contradictory of the conclusion and combine it with one of the premises to produce a conclusion that contradicts the other premise. In some moods, the contradictory of the conclusion serves as the major premise along with the minor of the original syllogism; in others, it serves as the minor premise with the original major as the major. For example, in the first mood, the contradictory of the conclusion 'some C is not A' is 'all C is A'. Joining it with the minor 'some B is C' yields a middle that, by first figure, contradicts the major premise. Thus the original conclusion is proven. This passage illustrates the technical depth of classical Islamic logic in analyzing the syllogism.

Key Terms

خلف
ضرب
نقيض

The fourth figure's eight moods are proven valid by al-khalf, which uses the contradictory of the conclusion to derive a contradiction with one of the premises.

Productive Mood and Khulf in the Second Figure
Author defines a productive mood of the second figure and explains khulf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a productive mood of the second figure and construct a proof by contradiction (khulf) for its validity.

Core Idea
In the second figure, khulf uses the contradictory of the conclusion to derive a contradiction with one premise.

In this passage, the author defines a specific productive mood of the second figure: universal negative minor with particular affirmative major yields particular negative. He then introduces the method of proof by contradiction (khulf), which is essential for demonstrating the validity of moods that do not produce direct conclusions. You will learn how to construct such proofs by assuming the opposite of the conclusion and combining it with one premise to contradict the other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Locate the minor and major premises in the example: minor is universal negative ('No B is J'), major is particular affirmative ('Some A is B'), conclusion is particular negative ('Some J is not A').
The author uses Arabic letters as variables.
2
Understand the mood
This mood (universal negative + particular affirmative) is productive in the second figure, yielding a particular negative conclusion according to the rule.
This mood corresponds to 'Festino' in traditional terminology.
3
Comprehend khulf
Khulf (reductio ad absurdum) takes the contradictory of the conclusion (naqid al-natija) and combines it with one of the original premises to produce a new conclusion that, after conversion, contradicts the other premise.
The contradictory of 'Some J is not A' is 'All J is A' (universal affirmative).
4
Determine which premise to use
The choice of which premise to combine with the contradictory depends on the quality of the contradictory: if it is affirmative, make it the minor premise; if negative, make it the major. Here the contradictory is affirmative (All J is A), so it becomes the minor.
This is clarified in the commentary by al-Dasuqi.
5
Trace the derivation
Combine the contradictory (minor: All J is A) with the original major (Some A is B) to derive 'Some J is B'. Then, by conversion (partial?), this contradicts the original minor (No B is J). If the contradictory is assumed true, the premises would be inconsistent, proving the original conclusion valid.
The derivation assumes standard conversion rules (e.g., particular affirmative converts simply).

Commentary
This passage defines a productive mood of the second figure of the categorical syllogism and introduces the method of proof by contradiction (khulf). The author first states that when the minor premise is universal negative and the major is particular affirmative, the conclusion is particular negative. An abstract example is given using placeholders. Then the general method of khulf is described: take the contradictory of the conclusion, combine it with one of the original premises, and produce a new conclusion that, after conversion, contradicts the other original premise. The commentary by al-Dasuqi then clarifies the abstract example with concrete terms: 'Nothing of man is stone' (the minor), 'some rational is man' (the major), so 'some stone is not rational' (conclusion). He then notes that the choice of which premise to combine with the contradictory is not arbitrary: if the contradictory is affirmative, it becomes the minor; if negative, the major. This prepares for the detailed discussion of specific moods.

Key Terms
قياس (qiyas): Categorical syllogism, specifically the second figure (shakl) in this passage. [Note: In earlier texts, qiyas may include analogical reasoning; here it is strictly categorical.]
صغرى (sughra): The minor premise of the syllogism. [Note: No significant shift; stable term.]
كبرى (kubra): The major premise of the syllogism. [Note: No significant shift; stable term.]
خلف (khulf): Proof by contradiction (reductio ad absurdum): assume the contradictory of the conclusion and derive a contradiction with one premise. [Note: In earlier logic, khulf is a specific method; here it is applied to the second figure.]
نقيض (naqid): The contradictory (opposite truth-value) of a proposition, used as the assumption in khulf. [Note: Stable term; sometimes confused with contrary (didd).]
ضرب (darb): A mood of the syllogism, i.e., a valid combination of premises with a specific quantity and quality. [Note: No significant shift; stable term.]

Applying Khulf to Second-Figure Moods
Commentators specify which moods admit khulf and the two procedures, with a critique of the exclusion of the fourth mood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply proof by contradiction (khulf) to any second-figure categorical syllogism and determine which moods admit khulf.

Core Idea
Khulf reduces second-figure moods to first-figure syllogisms by assuming the contradictory of the conclusion.

The second figure has eight moods, but not all can be proved directly. Scholars developed a method using khulf (proof by contradiction) to establish those that resist direct proof. In this passage, al-‘Attār and al-Dasūqī explain two procedures for applying khulf and challenge the received view that the fourth mood cannot be proved this way. Let's walk through the steps.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood and its conclusion type
Determine whether the conclusion of the original second-figure syllogism is universal (A or E) or particular (I or O). This decides which khulf procedure to use.
First and second moods have universal negative conclusions; third through seventh have particular negative; eighth has particular negative but its minor is negative.
2
Select the appropriate khulf procedure
If the conclusion is universal (first or second mood), take its contradictory (which is particular affirmative) and make it the major premise of a first-figure syllogism, with the original minor as its minor. If the conclusion is particular negative (third through seventh), take its contradictory (universal affirmative) and make it the minor premise of a first-figure syllogism, with the original major as its major.
The choice ensures the first-figure premises are valid (major universal, minor affirmative).
3
Construct the first-figure syllogism
Form a first-figure syllogism using the contradictory of the conclusion and one of the original premises (the minor for universal conclusion, the major for particular conclusion). Use the original remaining premise as the other premise.
The original premises are from the second figure: e.g., 'Every horse is animal' (major), 'Every neighing thing is horse' (minor) for the first mood.
4
Derive a contradiction with the original premise
The first-figure syllogism yields a conclusion that, after conversion if needed, directly contradicts the other original premise (the one not used in the khulf syllogism).
For the first mood, the khulf yields 'Nothing of horse is neighing' which converts to 'Nothing of neighing is horse', contradicting the original major 'Every neighing thing is horse'.
5
Conclude the original conclusion is true
Since the assumption of the contradictory led to a contradiction with a true premise, the assumption is false, so the original conclusion must be true. This validates the mood.
Khulf is a valid proof method; it does not require direct syllogistic inference.

(۲) (قَوْلُهُ : لأن الكبرى ... إلخ) أي : وهي لا تصلح لكبرى الشكل الأوَّل إذ جعل نقيض النتيجة صغراه، فإن جعل النقيض كبراه وصغرى الأصل صغراه منع أيضاً في الثامن؛ لأن صغراه سالبة لا تصلح كما يمنع في الرابع وإن كانت صغراه تصلح؛ لأنه ينتج ما لا ينافي كبرى الأصل هذا ما قالوه ونحن لا نسلم في الرابع فإن المنافاة ظاهرة مثلاً : كل إنسان حيوان وبعض الحجر ليس بإنسان، ينتج بعض الحيوان ليس بحجر، والدليل لو لم يصدق هذا لصدق نقيضه وهو كل حيوان ،حجر، ثم نجعله كبرى لصغرى الأصل هكذا كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان حجر ينتج كل إنسان حجر وينعكس إلى بعض الحجر إنسان وهو ينافي كبرى الأصل المفروضة الصدق والفساد إنما جاء من نقيض النتيجة فهي حق وبذلك يعلم جريان الخلف فيما عدا الثامن.
(His statement: 'by khulf') It proceeds in the first, second, third, fifth, sixth, and seventh moods, and does not proceed in the fourth and eighth because the major of the first figure becomes particular. (His statement: 'for in some of the moods' up to his statement: 'to produce what contradicts the minor') As for the first and second moods: he makes the contradictory of the conclusion, because of its universality, the major, and the minor of the syllogism [the original?] because its affirmative nature makes it a minor of the first figure, so it is determined that its major and the minor of the original [premise] is its minor. For if we say: Every horse is an animal and every neighing thing is a horse, it produces: Some animal is neighing. The proof: if this were not true, its contradictory would be true, namely: Nothing of animal is neighing. And it is made major for the minor of the original as follows: Every horse is an animal and nothing of animal is neighing; it produces: Nothing of horse is neighing, and by conversion: Nothing of neighing is horse, which contradicts the supposed-true major [of the original]. The falsity came only from the contradictory of the conclusion, so it [the conclusion] is true. And the like is said for the second mood. (1) (His statement: 'the third... etc.') The secret is that the conclusion in these four moods is negative, and its contradictory is affirmative, and it is suitable as a minor of the first figure. So the major [of the first figure] is joined to it to produce something which, if converted, contradicts the minor of the original. We say for the third mood, by analogy with the rest: Every horse is an animal and nothing of stone is a horse, it produces: Some animal is not a stone. For if this were not true, its contradictory would be true, namely: Every animal is a stone. And it is made a minor for the major of the original as follows: Every animal is a stone and nothing of stone is a horse; it produces: Nothing of animal is a horse, and by conversion: Nothing of horse is an animal, which contradicts the supposed-true minor of the original. The falsity came only from the contradictory of the conclusion, so it is true. (2) (His statement: 'because the major... etc.') Meaning: it is not suitable as a major of the first figure when the contradictory of the conclusion is made its minor, for if the contradictory is made its major and the minor of the original is its minor, it is also prevented in the eighth because its minor is negative and not suitable; similarly it is prevented in the fourth even though its minor is suitable because it produces something that does not contradict the major of the original. This is what they said, but we do not concede regarding the fourth, for the contradiction is apparent. For example: Every human is an animal and some stone is not a human, it produces: Some animal is not a stone. The proof: if this were not true, its contradictory would be true, namely: Every animal is a stone. Then we make it a major for the minor of the original as follows: Every human is an animal and every animal is a stone; it produces: Every human is a stone, and by conversion: Some stone is a human, which contradicts the supposed-true major of the original. The falsity came only from the contradictory of the conclusion, so it is true. And by that it is known that khulf proceeds in all except the eighth.
Source pages p. 835

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by al-‘Attār and al-Dasūqī that expands on the application of khulf (proof by contradiction) to the eight moods of the second figure. The commentator first lists the moods where khulf works: first, second, third, fifth, sixth, seventh. It fails in the fourth and eighth because the major of the first figure becomes particular (i.e., the contradictory of the conclusion, when used as a premise, would yield a particular major in the first figure, which is not allowed). Then al-Dasūqī explains the two procedures: in the first and second moods, the contradictory of the conclusion is universal negative and is made the major premise of a first-figure syllogism, with the original minor as the minor, to derive a conclusion that contradicts the original major. He gives an example: the conclusion 'some animal is neighing' is proved by assuming its contradictory 'nothing of animal is neighing' and combining with the original minor 'every horse is an animal' to get 'nothing of horse is neighing', which converts to 'nothing of neighing is horse', contradicting the original major 'every neighing thing is horse'. Similarly for the second mood. For the third through seventh moods, the conclusion is negative, so its contradictory is affirmative and becomes the minor of the first figure, with the original major as the major, to derive a conclusion that contradicts the original minor. An example is given for the third mood. Finally, al-‘Attār challenges the received view that khulf does not work in the fourth mood. He shows that if the fourth mood's contradictory is assumed, it does lead to a contradiction with the original major. For instance, from 'every human is animal' and 'some stone is not human', the conclusion 'some animal is not stone' is proven by assuming its contradictory 'every animal is stone', making it the major with the original minor 'every human is animal' to get 'every human is stone', which converts to 'some stone is human', contradicting the original major 'some stone is not human'. Thus khulf works in the fourth mood as well. The only mood excluded is the eighth, because its minor is negative and cannot serve as a premise in the first figure.

Key Terms
خلف
ضرب
نقيض
شكل أول
عكس

Key Takeaway

Burhan al-Khalf in Certain Syllogistic Moods: Procedure and Dispute
Al-Dasuqi and al-‘Attar explain burhan al-khalf in certain moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the burhan al-khalf method to reduce categorical syllogisms in certain moods to the first figure, and evaluate its applicability in the fourth mood based on the cited demonstration.

Core Idea
Burhan al-khalf proves a conclusion by showing its negation leads to a contradiction with an original premise.

This passage from al-Dasuqi and al-‘Attar specifies which moods of the categorical syllogism admit proof by contradiction (burhan al-khalf). This method is a powerful tool in demonstration, allowing us to prove conclusions by reductio ad absurdum. Understanding its conditions and the dispute over the fourth mood helps us apply it correctly in theological and juristic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism
Determine the mood (shakl and ḍarb) of the syllogism. The passage focuses on moods where the conclusion is negative (third, fifth, sixth, seventh in the first figure? Actually the text refers to moods of the second figure). Note that the method is initially said to apply only to these.
The original syllogism must be valid.
2
Form the contradictory of the conclusion
Take the contradictory of the conclusion. Since the original conclusion is negative, its contradictory is affirmative, which is suitable as a premise in the first figure.
The contradictory must be a categorical proposition.
3
Construct a first-figure syllogism
Combine the contradictory (as the major premise) with the original minor premise (as the minor premise) to form a new categorical syllogism in the first figure.
The original minor becomes the minor of the new syllogism.
4
Derive and convert the new conclusion
From the new syllogism, derive a conclusion. Then convert this conclusion using standard conversion rules. The converted proposition should directly contradict one of the original premises (usually the major).
If the contradiction is achieved, the original conclusion is proven.
5
Evaluate the fourth mood dispute
The passage records an objection: some scholars say burhan al-khalf does not work in the fourth mood because the derived conclusion does not contradict the original major. Al-‘Attar refutes this by providing an example (human, animal, stone) and showing that after conversion, a contradiction does arise.
This demonstrates that the method is valid in the fourth mood as well, with only the eighth mood remaining excluded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (وفي بعضها)؛ أي: وهو الضَّربُ الثَّالثُ (۱) ، والخامس، والسادس، (۲) والسَّابِعُ، وَلَا يجري في الرابع ، والثَّامن؛ لأنَّ الكُبرى (٢) فيهما جزئية.
العطار.
صغرى، لينتج ما ينعكس إلى ما يُنافي الكُبرى، مثلاً إذا صدقَ كُلُّ «ب» «ج» وكُلُّ «أ» «ب»؛ صدقَ بعضُ (ج)» «أ»، وإلا ؛ فَلا شيء مِن «ج» «أ» نجعلُها كُبرى لِقَولِنَا : كُلُّ «ب» «ج» ينتج : لا شيء من «ب» «أ»، وينعكس إلى : لا شيء من (أ) (ب) وقد كانَتِ الكُبرى : كُلُّ «أ» «ب» هف، وَقِش عليهِ .
لصغروية الشَّكل الأوّل فتعين أن تكون كبراه وصغرى الأصل صغراه فإذا قلنا : كل فرس حيوان وكل صاهل ،فرس أنتج بعض الحيوان صاهل والدليل أنه لو لم يصدق هذا الصدق نقيضه وهو لا شيء من الحيوان بصاهل ويجعل كبرى لصغرى الأصل هكذا كل فرس حيوان ولا شيء من الحيوان بصاهل ؛ ينتج : لا شيء من الفرس بصاهل، وينعكس إلى لا شيء من الصاهل بفرس وهو منافٍ لكبرى الأصل المفروضة ،الصدق، والفساد إنما جاء من نقيض النتيجة فهي حق ومثل ذلك يقال في الضرب الثاني . (۱) (قَوْلُهُ : الثَّالث ... إلخ ) السِّرُّ في ذلك أن النتيجة في هذه الضروب الأربعة سالبة ونقيضها موجبة وهي تصلح لصغرى الشكل الأوَّل فيضم إليها كبراه لينتج ما لو عكس لنا في صغرى الأصل وتأتي لنا بالضرب الثَّالث ليقاس عليه الباقي فنقول : كل فرس حيوان ولا شيء من الحجر بفرس ينتج بعض الحيوان ليس بحجر، فإنه لو لم يصدق هذا لصدق نقيضه وهو : كل حيوان حجر ويجعل صغرى لكبرى الأصل هكذا : كل حيوان حجر ولا شيء من الحجر بفرس، ينتج : لا شيء من الحيوان بفرس، وينعكس إلى : لا شيء من الفرس بحيوان، هو يخالف صغرى الأصل المفروضة الصدق والفساد إنما جاء من نقيض النتيجة فهي حق . (۲) (قَوْلُهُ : لأن الكبرى ... إلخ) أي : وهي لا تصلح لكبرى الشكل الأوَّل إذ جعل نقيض النتيجة صغراه، فإن جعل النقيض كبراه وصغرى الأصل صغراه منع أيضاً في الثامن؛ لأن صغراه سالبة لا تصلح كما يمنع في الرابع وإن كانت صغراه تصلح؛ لأنه ينتج ما لا ينافي كبرى الأصل هذا ما قالوه ونحن لا نسلم في الرابع فإن المنافاة ظاهرة مثلاً : كل إنسان حيوان وبعض الحجر ليس بإنسان، ينتج بعض الحيوان ليس بحجر، والدليل لو لم يصدق هذا لصدق نقيضه وهو كل حيوان ،حجر، ثم نجعله كبرى لصغرى الأصل هكذا كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان حجر ينتج كل إنسان حجر وينعكس إلى بعض الحجر إنسان وهو ينافي كبرى الأصل المفروضة الصدق والفساد إنما جاء من نقيض النتيجة فهي حق وبذلك يعلم جريان الخلف فيما عدا الثامن.
And his saying: 'And in some of them' – meaning: the third mood (1), the fifth, the sixth, (2) and the seventh, and it does not apply in the fourth and the eighth because the major (2) in them is particular.
Al-‘Attar.
Minor, to produce what, when converted, contradicts the major. For example: if 'Every B is J' and 'Every A is B' are true, then 'Some J is A' is true. Otherwise, if 'No J is A', we make it the major for our saying 'Every B is J' – it produces 'No B is A', and it converts to 'No A is B', whereas the major was 'Every A is B', and it is criticized.
Because the minor of the first figure is determined, so its major and the original minor become its minor. So if we say: 'Every horse is an animal' and 'Every neighing thing is a horse', it produces 'Some animal is neighing'. The proof: if this were not true, its contradictory 'No animal is neighing' would be true, and we make it the major for the original minor thus: 'Every horse is an animal' and 'No animal is neighing' produces 'No horse is neighing', and it converts to 'No neighing thing is a horse', which contradicts the supposed truth of the original major. The falsity came only from the contradictory of the conclusion, so it is true. And the like is said in the second mood. (1) (His saying: 'the third... etc.') The reason for that is that the conclusion in these four moods is negative, and its contradictory is affirmative, and it is suitable as the minor of the first figure, so it is combined with its major to produce what, if converted, opposes the original minor. And we bring the third mood to measure the rest against it. We say: 'Every horse is an animal' and 'No stone is a horse' produces 'Some animal is not a stone'. For if this were not true, its contradictory 'Every animal is a stone' would be true, and we make it the minor for the original major thus: 'Every animal is a stone' and 'No stone is a horse' produces 'No animal is a horse', and it converts to 'No horse is an animal', which contradicts the supposed truth of the original minor. The falsity came only from the contradictory of the conclusion, so it is true. (2) (His saying: 'because the major... etc.') That is: it is not suitable as the major of the first figure when the contradictory of the conclusion is made its minor. For if we make the contradictory the major and the original minor the minor, it is also prevented in the eighth because its minor is negative and not suitable, as it is prevented in the fourth even though its minor is suitable, because it produces that which does not contradict the original major. This is what they said, but we do not accept it in the fourth, for the contradiction is apparent. For example: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some stone is not a human' produces 'Some animal is not a stone'. The proof: if this were not true, its contradictory 'Every animal is a stone' would be true, then we make it the major for the original minor thus: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every animal is a stone' produces 'Every human is a stone', and it converts to 'Some stone is a human', which contradicts the supposed truth of the original major. The falsity came only from the contradictory of the conclusion, so it is true. And by that it is known that [the method of] burhan al-khalf applies except in the eighth.
Source pages p. 836

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on a brief matn statement 'And in some of them' referring to the moods in which the method of proof by contradiction (burhan al-khalf) is applicable. The commentators, al-Dasuqi and al-‘Attar, clarify which moods are included (third, fifth, sixth, seventh) and excluded (fourth, eighth) and explain the underlying reasoning.

Al-Dasuqi first states that the method does not apply in the fourth and eighth because the major premise is particular. Then al-‘Attar elaborates with a systematic explanation: we take the contradictory of the conclusion (which must be negative for these moods) and use it as a premise in a first-figure syllogism together with the original minor premise, such that the result, after conversion, contradicts the original major premise. This proves the original conclusion by reductio ad absurdum.

He provides a concrete example using terms: 'Every horse is an animal' (major of original? careful: the example has original premises: Every B is J (major) and Every A is B (minor) yielding Some J is A. The contradictory 'No J is A' is used as major with 'Every B is J' to get 'No B is A', which converts to 'No A is B', contradicting the original major 'Every A is B'. The method is then illustrated with the horse/neighing/animal example, and also with the second mood analogously.

Footnote (1) explains the rationale: the conclusion in these four moods (third, fifth, sixth, seventh) is negative, so its contradictory is affirmative and serves as a valid minor in the first figure. The commentator then shows the procedure for the third mood with the example of horse, stone, animal.

Footnote (2) addresses the objection about the fourth mood. Some scholars said burhan al-khalf does not apply because the produced result does not contradict the original major. But the commentator (al-‘Attar?) argues that it does contradict, providing an example with human, animal, stone. He shows that the contradictory of the conclusion, when used as major, produces a result that after conversion contradicts the original major. Therefore, the method is valid in the fourth as well, and only the eighth remains excluded because its minor is negative.

The lesson thus clarifies the precise conditions and procedure for burhan al-khalf in categorical syllogisms, emphasizing that it requires the contradictory to be a suitable premise, and that the contradiction with an original premise must be achieved after conversion.

Key Terms

الضرب

Key Takeaway
Burhan al-khalf is a valid method for proving conclusions in categorical syllogisms provided the contradictory premise is suitable and the contradiction is achieved after conversion, with the eighth mood being the exception.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Reducing Syllogisms by Swapping Premises and Converting the Conclusion
Author introduces the method of 'swapping premises' then 'converting the conclusion' to reduce other syllogistic figures to the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce any valid categorical syllogism to the first figure by applying the technique of swapping premises (ʿaks al-tartīb) and converting the conclusion (ʿaks al-natīja), and identify which moods permit this reduction.

Core Idea
Reducing to the first figure is the key to certitude in syllogistic reasoning.

In this lesson, we learn a practical technique to transform any categorical syllogism into the first figure—the most natural and certain form. The author presents two steps: swapping the order of premises and then converting the conclusion. We will work through the example given and understand the conditions for this method to succeed.

Guided Path
1
Extract the syllogism's original form
Write the two premises and conclusion using the variables A, B, C. Identify the major and minor terms.
In the example, the original form is: Every A is B, every B is C; therefore every A is C.
2
Swap the premises
Exchange the positions of the major and minor premises so that the original minor becomes the major and vice versa.
This is called ʿaks al-tartīb. It may rearrange the premises to fit the first figure's requirement.
3
Identify the new figure
After swapping, check if the middle term is now the subject of the major and the predicate of the minor. If so, the syllogism is in the first figure.
If not, the method fails; other reductions may be needed.
4
Convert the conclusion
Apply the appropriate conversion (e.g., universal affirmative to particular affirmative) to obtain the desired conclusion.
This is ʿaks al-natīja. In the example, 'Every A is C' converts to 'Some C is A'.
5
Verify the conditions
Ensure the major premise is affirmative. As al-Sharnubi notes, the method only works for moods where the major is affirmative (first, second, and sixth moods).
If the major is not affirmative, the method cannot be applied.

Chapter: On the Syllogism
Or by swapping the order (of premises) so that it returns to the first figure, then converting the conclusion, as is said in the first example for instance: Every A is B, and every B is C, so every A is C, and it converts to the desired conclusion, which is Some C is A.
Al-Dasuqi
(His saying: 'for in some of the moods... etc.') Its summary: that one combines the contradictory of the conclusion with a premise such that a syllogism from the first figure is arranged, productive, fulfilling the conditions, and that that conclusion is contradictory. Not if a syllogism is not arranged because a condition is violated, as if the major is particular, or it comes in the form of a productive syllogism but does not produce contradiction, as if it produces a particular negative, it is not contradictory to a particular affirmative. (His saying: 'to produce that which contradicts... etc.) That is: and the conversion is necessary, and likewise is said thereafter. (His saying: 'in the first example') — it is the compound (1) of two universal affirmative premises, major for example: Every B is C, and every A is B; i.e., every human is an animal, and every rational being is a human. (His saying: 'Every A is B' i.e., every rational being is a human, and this is the original major. (His saying: 'and every B is C') i.e., every animal is a human, this is the original minor. (His saying: 'so every A is C') i.e., every rational being is an animal, and this conclusion is universal affirmative, and it converts to particular affirmative. (His saying: 'Some C is A') i.e., some animal is rational.
Al-‘Attar
(His saying: 'or by swapping the order') — it is also called 'al-tabdīl' and 'al-qalb', and it is that you make the minor the major and the major the minor so that this figure returns to the first figure, then convert the conclusion. (His saying: 'Every A is B' means: if we say in the first example: every rational being is a human and every human is an animal, then every rational being is an animal; then if we convert it to our saying: some animal is rational, we obtain the desired goal.
(1) (His saying: 'and it is the compound... etc.) — it is corrupted, and its correct form is: 'and it is the compound of two universal affirmatives.' And know that swapping the order then the conclusion applies in the first, second, and sixth because their major is affirmative, so it is suitable as the minor of the first figure, but not in the remaining moods due to the absence of the conditions for productivity. End quote. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 837

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter ('Fasl fi al-Qiyas') and presents an alternative method to reduce any syllogism to the first figure: by swapping the order of premises (making the minor premise the major and vice versa) and then converting the conclusion. The example given is a standard first-figure syllogism with terms A, B, C: 'Every A is B, every B is C, therefore every A is C'. After swapping the premises, we get 'Every B is C, every A is B' which is also first figure but with different order, then the conclusion 'Every A is C' is converted by accident (from universal affirmative to particular affirmative) to obtain 'Some C is A'.

Al-Dasuqi then provides a commentary clarifying that this method is part of the broader technique of proof by contradiction, but here it is presented separately. He explains that the procedure requires that the contradictory of the conclusion be combined with a premise to form a valid first-figure syllogism that yields a contradiction. He notes that if the premises are not suitable (e.g., particular major), the method fails.

Al-‘Attar adds that this method is also called 'al-tabdīl' (exchange) and 'al-qalb' (inversion), and he rephrases the example using concrete terms (rational, human, animal).

A footnote by al-Sharnubi corrects a misprint in the text: the phrase 'al-murakkab' should be 'al-murakkab min mūjibatayn kulliyyatayn' (compound of two universal affirmatives). He further states that this method of swapping premises then converting the conclusion applies only to the first, second, and sixth figures because their major premises are affirmative and thus can serve as minor of the first figure; it does not apply to other moods because conditions for productivity are absent.

The lesson thus teaches a specific conversion procedure used in the art of logic to unify all syllogistic arguments under the first figure, which is the most intuitive and productive.

Key Terms
عكس الترتيب (ʿaks al-tartīb): Swapping the order of premises: making the original minor premise the major and the original major premise the minor. This is a method to reduce other syllogistic figures to the first figure. Also called 'al-tabdīl' and 'al-qalb'. [Note: This term is used consistently in later logic texts; no significant shift.]
عكس النتيجة (ʿaks al-natīja): Conversion of the conclusion: after reducing to the first figure, the conclusion is converted by simple conversion (e.g., universal affirmative to particular affirmative) to obtain the desired proposition. [Note: Conversion rules vary by type; here it is specifically the conversion of a universal affirmative to a particular affirmative.]
الشكل الأول (al-shakl al-awwal): The first figure of the categorical syllogism: characterized by the middle term being the subject in the major and predicate in the minor. It is the only figure that yields all four types of conclusions without conversion. [Note: No shift; this is standard throughout the tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The method of swapping premises and converting the conclusion is a systematic technique to reduce any valid syllogism to the first figure, but only when the major premise is affirmative.

The Syllogism

Reducing Syllogisms to the First and Second Figures
Commentary explains three methods for reducing syllogisms to first figure and reduction to second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the three methods of reducing categorical syllogisms to the first figure (reversal of order with conversion of conclusion, conversion of both premises) and the method of reduction to the second figure by converting the minor premise.

Core Idea
All valid syllogisms can be reduced to the self-evident first figure using simple conversion.

The passage presents three methods for reducing syllogisms that are not in the most evident first figure into that form, plus an alternative reduction to the second figure. These techniques are essential for verifying syllogistic validity in Islamic logic, as they allow any valid argument to be transformed into the clearest figure. The commentary by Dusuqi and 'Attar explains each method with concrete examples using Arabic variables (أ, ب, ج) and then interprets them with terms from everyday knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism's figure and mood
Examine the given syllogism to determine its figure based on the position of the middle term. If it is not in the first figure, note which method might apply. For example, a syllogism with an affirmative universal minor and a negative universal major may be reduced by converting both premises.
The first figure has the middle term as subject of the minor and predicate of the major.
2
Apply reversal of order (عكس الترتيب) and conversion of conclusion (عكس النتيجة)
Swap the minor and major premises so that the middle term becomes subject in one and predicate in the other (first figure). Then, if the resulting conclusion is not the desired proposition, convert it using simple conversion (e.g., universal affirmative becomes particular affirmative). Example: All A is B, all B is C becomes all A is C; convert to 'some C is A'.
This method works when the premises after reversal satisfy first-figure conditions.
3
Apply conversion of both premises (عكس المقدمتين)
Convert both premises individually using simple conversion (e.g., 'all B is C' becomes 'some C is B'; 'no A is B' becomes 'no B is A'). Ensure the resulting syllogism is in the first figure and produces the desired conclusion. Example: From 'all human is animal' and 'no stone is human', after conversion we get 'some animal is human' and 'no human is stone', yielding 'some animal is not stone'.
This method applies only to certain moods (e.g., third and fifth) where the first-figure conditions are met after conversion.
4
Apply reduction to the second figure by converting the minor (الرد إلى الشكل الثاني بعكس الصغرى)
Convert only the minor premise to obtain a second-figure syllogism (where the predicate of the minor is also predicate of the major). This is an alternative when first-figure reduction is not convenient. Note restrictions: the second figure requires a particular conclusion and works only when the premises differ in quality.
It does not apply when premises have the same quality (first and second moods) or when the conclusion would be universal.

(قَوْلُهُ : لِيُنْتِجَ مَا يُنَافِي... إلخ) أي : وَلَا بدَّ من العكس، وكذا يُقالُ فيما بعده. (قَوْلُهُ: كُلُّ (أ ب) أي : كلُّ ناطق إنسان، وهذا هو كبرى الأصل. (قَوْلُهُ : وَكُلُّ ب (ج) أي : كُلُّ حيوان إنسان، هذا هو صُغرى الأصل. (قَوْلُهُ: فَكُلُّ أ ج ) أي : كلُّ ناطق حيوان، وهذه النتيجة وهي موجبة كلية، وهي تنعكس موجبة جزئية.
