(قَوْلُهُ: كُلُّ أب يعني : إذا قُلْنَا في المثالِ الأَوَّلِ : كُلُّ ناطق إنسانٌ وَكُلُّ إنسانٍ حيوان؛ فَكُلُّ ناطق حيوان، فإذا عكسنَاهُ إلى قولنا : بعضُ الحيوانِ ناطق؛ يحصلُ المطلوب. (أو بعكس المقدمتين وهو أن تنعكس الصُّغرى، ثمَّ الكُبرى بالعكس المستوي؛ ليرتد إلى الشكل الأوَّلِ، وينتج المطلوب كما يُقالُ في المثالِ الثَّالِثِ مثلاً : «بعض (ج) (ب) ، ولا شيء من (ب)(أ)، فبعض (ج) ليس (أ)) . الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : فِي الْمِثَالِ الثَّالِثِ) وهو المركَّبُ مِن موجبةٍ كُلِّيَّةٍ صُغرى، وسالبة كُلّيَّةٍ كُبرى نحو : كلُّ ((ب)) ((ج))، وَلَا شيء مِن «أ» «ب» ، أي : كلُّ إنسان حيوانٌ، وَلَا شيء مِنَ الحجر بإنسان. (قَوْلُهُ : بَعْضُ ج ب ) أي : بعضُ الحيوان إنسان، وهذا هو عكس الصُّغرى في الأصل. (قَوْلُهُ : وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنْ ب (أ) أي من الإنسان بحجر، وهذا هو عكس الكُبرى في الأصل. (قَوْلُهُ : فَبَعْضُ ج لَيْسَ (أ) أي : فبعض الحيوان ليس بحجر (قَوْلُهُ : إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الثَّانِي وهو أنْ يكونَ المحمول في الصغرى محمولاً في الكُبرى. العطار: (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ بِعَكْسِ الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ) وهذا يجري في الثَّالث والخامس، وَلَا يجري في غيرِهِمَا لانتفاء شرائطِ إنتاج الشكل الأوَّلِ. (قَوْلُهُ : بَعْضُ ... إِلَخَ) فإذا قُلْنَا : كُلُّ حيوان إنسانٌ وَلَا شَيءٍ مِنَ الإِنسانِ بحمار ينتجُ : بعضُ الحيوانِ ليس بحمارٍ، وَقِش عليه الخامس. (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ بِالرَّدْ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الثَّانِي بِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى) وهو يجري في السَّابعِ والثَّالث والخامس أيضاً ، لكنْ لَمَّا أمكنهم البيانُ بالشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ؛ تركُوا ذلكَ، وَلَا يَجري في الأوَّلِ والثَّاني ؛ لعدم الاختلافِ في الكيفِ، وَلَا في السَّادس؛ لأنَّ الشَّكلَ الثَّاني لا ينتج إلا جزئيَّةٌ ، وَلَا في الرابع والثَّالث؛ لأنَّ الجزئية لا تصلحُ لِكبرويَّةِ الشَّكلِ الثَّاني
Section: On Syllogism. Or by reversal of order, so that it reverts to the first figure, then conversion of the conclusion, as is said in the first example: All A is B, and all B is C, so all A is C, and it converts to the desired conclusion, which is some C is A. Al-Dusuqi: (His saying: 'so in some moods...etc.') Its summary: He adds the contradictory of the conclusion to a premise such that a syllogism of the first figure is formed, productive, fulfilling conditions, and that conclusion is contradictory; but if a syllogism is not formed because a condition is violated, e.g., the major premise is particular, or it comes in the form of a productive syllogism except that it does not produce contradiction, e.g., it produces a particular negative, then it is not contradictory to the particular affirmative. (His saying: 'to produce what contradicts...etc.') i.e., conversion is necessary, and similarly is said afterward. (His saying: 'in the first example') It is composed of an affirmative universal major, like: All B is C, and all A is B; i.e., every human is an animal, and every rational is a human. (His saying: 'All A is B') i.e., every rational is human, and this is the major of the original. (His saying: 'and all B is C') i.e., every animal is human, this is the minor of the original. (His saying: 'so all A is C') i.e., every rational is animal, and this conclusion is a universal affirmative, and it converts to a particular affirmative. (His saying: 'some C is A') i.e., some animal is rational. Al-'Attar: (His saying: 'or by reversal of order') It is also called substitution and inversion, and it is that you make the minor the major, and the major the minor, so that this figure returns to the first figure, then convert the conclusion. (His saying: 'All A is B' means: when we say in the first example: every rational is human and every human is animal; so every rational is animal, and if we convert it to our statement: some animal is rational; the desired conclusion is obtained. (Or by converting both premises: that the minor is converted, then the major by simple conversion, so that it reverts to the first figure, and produces the desired conclusion, as is said in the third example: 'Some C is B, and no B is A, so some C is not A'.) Al-Dusuqi: (His saying: 'in the third example') It is composed of an affirmative universal minor and a negative universal major, like: All B is C, and no A is B, i.e., every human is animal, and no stone is human. (His saying: 'some C is B') i.e., some animal is human, and this is the conversion of the minor in the original. (His saying: 'and no B is A') i.e., no human is stone, and this is the conversion of the major in the original. (His saying: 'so some C is not A') i.e., some animal is not stone. (His saying: 'to the second figure') It is that the predicate in the minor is a predicate in the major. Al-'Attar: (His saying: 'or by converting both premises') This applies to the third and fifth, and does not apply to others due to the absence of conditions for production of the first figure. (His saying: 'some...etc.') If we say: every animal is human and no human is donkey, it produces: some animal is not donkey, and similarly for the fifth. (His saying: 'or by reduction to the second figure by converting the minor') It applies to the seventh, third, and fifth also, but since they could explain by the first figure, they left that; and it does not apply to the first and second due to lack of difference in quality, nor to the sixth because the second figure only produces a particular, nor to the fourth and third because the particular is not suitable for being the major of the second figure.
Source pages p. 837

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of methods for reducing syllogisms that are not in the first figure into the first figure (or sometimes into the second figure) in order to obtain the desired conclusion. The commentary, drawing from Dusuqi and 'Attar, elaborates on each method. The author first describes the method of 'reversal of order' (عكس الترتيب) followed by conversion of the conclusion. This means swapping the minor and major premises so that the figure becomes the first figure, then converting the conclusion (عكس النتيجة) via simple or accidental conversion to produce the desired proposition. An example is given using variables (أ, ب, ج) and then interpreted with concrete terms: all rational (أ) is human (ب), all human (ب) is animal (ج), therefore all rational is animal, and converting gives 'some animal is rational'. Next, the method of 'converting both premises' (عكس المقدمتين) is explained: both the minor and major are converted using simple conversion (العكس المستوي) to yield a first-figure syllogism. This is illustrated with the third mood: an affirmative universal minor and a negative universal major. The example: all human (ب) is animal (ج), no stone (أ) is human (ب) becomes after conversion: some animal (ج) is human (ب), no human (ب) is stone (أ), concluding some animal (ج) is not stone (أ). Finally, the method of 'reduction to the second figure by converting the minor' (الرد إلى الشكل الثاني بعكس الصغرى) is presented. The second figure is defined as having the predicate of the minor also as predicate of the major. This method is said to apply to certain moods (seventh, third, fifth) but is avoided when possible by using the first figure. It does not apply when quality is the same (first and second moods) or when the conclusion is particular. The commentary thus provides a systematic classification of conversion-based reduction techniques, essential for proving that all valid syllogisms can be reduced to the first figure.

عكس النتيجة
عكس المقدمتين
الرد إلى الشكل الثاني
الشكل الأول
الشكل الثاني

Checkpoint
According to the passage, reduction to the second figure by converting the minor premise does NOT apply to which moods?
A) First and second moods
B) Third and fifth moods
C) Fourth and sixth moods
D) Seventh and third moods

Key Takeaway
A syllogism not in the first figure can be reduced to it by reversal of order plus conversion of the conclusion, or by converting both premises; alternatively, it can be reduced to the second figure by converting the minor only, though this is less common.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reduce the following syllogism to the first figure using the method of converting both premises: 'All humans are animals, no stones are humans, therefore some animals are not stones.' (Use the example from the passage.)
?
Given the syllogism 'Every believer is righteous, some scholars are believers, therefore some scholars are righteous', identify whether it is in the first figure. If not, choose a method to reduce it and perform the reduction.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Reducing Syllogisms to the Second Figure by Converting the Minor Premise
Author demonstrates reduction of the seventh mood to the second figure by converting the minor premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a valid syllogism from the seventh mood to the second figure by applying conversion to the minor premise.

Core Idea
Converting the minor premise transforms some moods into valid second-figure syllogisms.

In this lesson, we examine a technique used by logicians to reduce certain syllogisms to the second figure. By converting only the minor premise, the mood changes while preserving the conclusion. The author demonstrates this with the seventh mood and addresses a common objection about particular negatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original mood
Find the minor premise (particular negative) and major premise (universal affirmative). The seventh mood has a particular negative minor and universal affirmative major.
Example: 'Some J is not B' (minor) and 'Every A is B' (major).
2
Apply conversion to the minor premise
Convert the particular negative minor premise by swapping subject and predicate: 'Some J is not B' becomes 'Some B is not J'.
This follows the special rule that certain particular negatives can be converted.
3
Check the resulting figure
After conversion, both premises have the middle term B as predicate: minor 'Some B is not J' and major 'Every A is B'. This is the second figure.
The second figure requires the middle to be predicate in both premises.
4
Verify the conclusion matches
The original conclusion 'Some J is not A' should still follow from the new premises. In the second figure, the valid mood is Cesare or Camestres; check that the conclusion is identical.
The reduction preserves the original conclusion.
5
Address the objection
Some may object that particular negatives do not convert. The author clarifies that this minor is a special case that does convert, as previously established.
This exception is taught in immediate inference lessons.

فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ
وهو أن تُعكَسَ الصُّغرى فقط بالعكس المستوي؛ ليرتد إلى الشكل الثاني، وينتج المطلوبُ، كَمَا يُقالُ في المثالِ السَّابع مثلاً : «بعضُ (ج) ليس هو (ب)، وكلُّ (أ) (ب)، فبعضُ (ج) ليس (أ)) .
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: كَمَا يُقَالُ فِي الْمِثَالِ السَّابع وهو المركَّبُ من سالبة جزئية صغرى، وموجبةٍ كُلِّيَّةٍ كُبرى كقولنا : بعضُ ب ليسَ ،ج، وكلُّ أب؛ أي: بعضُ الإنسان ليس بحجر، وكل ناطق إنسان، فالصُّغرى تنعكش كنفسها كما يُقالُ في السَّابع. فإِنْ قِيْلَ : الصُّغرى سالبةٌ جزئيَّة، وهي لا تنعكسُ ؛ قُلْتُ : تحملُ الصُّغرى السالبة المذكورة على إحدى الخاصَّتين ؛ لأنَّهُ تقدَّمَ أنَّها تنعكس، هكذا صرَّحوا به. (قَوْلُهُ : كَمَا يُقَالُ: فِي الْمِثَالِ السَّابع) أي: وفي الثَّالث)، والخامس، والسادس دون البقية.
A section on the syllogism. It is that you convert the minor premise only by simple conversion, so that it returns to the second figure, and the desired conclusion is produced, as is said in the seventh example, for instance: 'Some J is not B, and every A is B, therefore some J is not A.' Al-Dussuqi: His statement: 'as is said in the seventh example' – and it is composed of a particular negative minor premise and a universal affirmative major premise, like saying: Some B is not C, and every A is B; i.e., 'Some man is not a stone, and every speaking being is a man', so the minor premise is converted as it is, as is said in the seventh. If it is said: The minor premise is particular negative, and it does not convert; I say: This particular negative premise is one of the two special cases because it was previously mentioned that it does convert; thus they explicitly stated. His statement: 'as is said in the seventh example' – i.e., also in the third, fifth, and sixth, not the rest.
Source pages p. 839

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the method of reducing syllogisms to the second figure by converting the minor premise. The author states the rule: convert the minor premise only (using simple conversion) so that the syllogism becomes a second-figure syllogism. The seventh mood is given as an example: originally it has a particular negative minor ('Some J is not B') and a universal affirmative major ('Every A is B'), which in the second figure requires the minor to be the middle term. By converting the minor premise, the mood is transformed into a valid second-figure syllogism. Al-Dussuqi clarifies that the minor in this case is 'some man is not a stone' and the major is 'every speaking being is a man'; after conversion the minor becomes 'some stone is not man' (if read carefully). He addresses an objection: particular negative premises normally do not convert. He responds that this particular negative does convert because it falls under the special case of negative propositions that convert, as explained earlier. He then notes that this method works for the seventh, third, fifth, and sixth moods, but not others. The teacher should stress that the conversion rule for particular negatives is an exception taught in immediate inferences.

Reduction to the Third Figure by Converting the Major Premise
Author explains reduction to the third figure via conversion of major premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which moods of categorical syllogisms can be reduced to the third figure by converting the major premise, and explain why the sixth, seventh, and eighth moods cannot.

Core Idea
Reduction to the third figure requires a convertible major premise and an affirmative minor premise.

This passage introduces a specific reduction technique: converting the major premise to transform a syllogism into the third figure. We will explore which moods this method applies to and the logical constraints that prevent it from working on all moods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reduction method
The method is to convert the major premise (عكس الكبرى) while leaving the minor premise unchanged, so that the resulting syllogism fits the third figure (middle term subject in both premises).
Conversion is an immediate inference that swaps subject and predicate, preserving truth only for universal negatives and particular affirmatives.
2
Review the moods mentioned by Al-Dussuqi
Al-Dussuqi states that this reduction applies to the first five moods (of the standard eight) and excludes the sixth, seventh, and eighth. Identify which moods these are in the standard list.
The first five moods are likely Barbara, Celarent, Darii, Ferio (first figure) and Cesare, Camestres, Festino, Baroco (second figure) but the classification varies; here they refer to the set of eight moods across all three figures.
3
Understand the condition for the third figure
In the third figure, the minor premise must be affirmative because the middle term is the subject in both premises and the conclusion is often particular. A negative minor premise would violate the figure's structural rules.
The sixth, seventh, and eighth moods have negative minor premises, so they cannot be reduced to the third figure by any method.
4
Check Al-Attar's elaboration
Al-Attar adds that the method actually applies to the fourth, first, second, third, and fifth moods as well, but logicians did not resort to it because they preferred reduction to the first figure. He also notes it applies to the second figure (possibly a different context).
The consistent restriction is that moods with negative minors (sixth, seventh, eighth) are excluded.
5
Verify with an example
Take a valid mood like Barbara (AAA-1): 'Every A is B, every C is A, therefore every C is B.' Convert the major premise 'every A is B' to its converse 'some B is A' (since conversion of A is particular). The result is a third figure syllogism with premises 'every C is A' and 'some B is A', giving conclusion 'some C is B' (Disamis).
The reduction is valid but the conclusion may weaken from universal to particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو بالرد إلى الشكل الثالث بعكس الكبرى فقط؛ ليرتد إلى الشكل الثالث.
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: أَوْ بِالرَّدْ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الثَّالِثِ ... إلخ) يأتي في الخمسِ الأُوَلِ، دونَ السَّادس، والسابع، والثَّامن.
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ: بِعَكْسِ الْكُبْرَى وهوَ يَجري في الرابع والأَوَّلِ والثَّاني والثَّالِثِ والخامس أيضاً، لكنَّهم لم يلتقُوا إليهِ لمثل ما مرَّ ، ولا يجري في السادس والسابع والثَّامنِ ؛ لامتناعِ سَلْبِ الصُّغرى في الشَّكلِ الثَّالث، وأيضاً يجري في الثاني.
Or by reduction to the third figure by converting the major premise only, so that it returns to the third figure. Al-Dussuqi: His statement: 'or by reduction to the third figure... etc.' applies to the first five (moods), excluding the sixth, seventh, and eighth. Al-Attar: His statement: 'by converting the major premise' and it applies to the fourth, first, second, third, and fifth as well, but they did not resort to it for the same reason as before, and it does not apply to the sixth, seventh, and eighth because negating the minor premise is not allowed in the third figure, and also it applies to the second.
Source pages p. 839

Commentary
This passage presents the method of reducing syllogisms to the third figure by converting the major premise only. Al-Dussuqi comments that this reduction applies to the first five moods (of the original list) but not to the sixth, seventh, or eighth. Al-Attar elaborates that this method actually applies to the fourth, first, second, third, and fifth moods, but logicians did not use it because they preferred the first-figure reduction. It does not apply to the sixth, seventh, and eighth because in the third figure the minor premise must be affirmative, and those moods have a negative minor. The teacher should note that the third figure requires the minor premise affirmative, so a negative minor would not work. Al-Attar also notes it applies to the second figure (?) but that seems out of place; it may be a reference to another context.

Key Takeaway
Reduction to the third figure by converting the major premise is only possible for moods that have an affirmative minor premise, which excludes the sixth, seventh, and eighth moods.

Reducing the Seventh Syllogism by Converting the Minor Premise
Author describes converting the minor premise to reduce the seventh syllogism to the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a particular negative minor premise in a categorical syllogism with a universal affirmative major premise to produce a valid second-figure syllogism (Festino).

Core Idea
A particular negative can convert in special cases, enabling reduction to the valid second-figure mood Festino.

Sometimes a syllogism appears invalid, but a simple conversion of its minor premise can transform it into a valid form. In this lesson, you will learn how the seventh mood—a particular negative minor and universal affirmative major—is reduced to the second figure by converting the minor premise. This technique is a key tool for evaluating and salvaging arguments in the logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism's form
The original seventh mood has a particular negative minor premise (e.g., 'Some B is not J') and a universal affirmative major premise (e.g., 'Every A is B'). This combination is not a valid mood in any figure.
The original minor is particular negative, which normally does not convert, but special cases exist.
2
Apply simple conversion to the minor premise
Swap the subject and predicate of the minor premise while preserving truth. For a particular negative, this is only allowed in two special cases: when the terms are coextensive or the subject is an individual. The conversion yields a new minor premise: 'Some J is not B'.
Simple conversion of particular negative normally fails, but here it is permitted.
3
Determine the resulting figure and mood
The new syllogism has the converted minor 'Some J is not B' and the major 'Every A is B'. The middle term (B) is the predicate of both premises, placing it in the second figure. This specific combination (particular negative minor, universal affirmative major) is the valid mood Festino.
Second figure requires one negative premise (present) and one universal (present).
4
Write the valid conclusion
From the valid Festino mood, the conclusion is 'Some J is not A' (e.g., 'Some stone is not rational'). The conclusion follows necessarily.
Festino is a standard valid mood in the second figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ وهُوَ أَنْ تُعْكَسَ الصُّغْرَى فَقَطْ بِالْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي؛ لِيَرْتَدَّ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الثَّانِي، وَيَنْتُجُ الْمَطْلُوبُ، كَمَا يُقَالُ فِي الْمِثَالِ السَّابِعِ مَثَلاً: «بَعْضُ (ج) لَيْسَ هُوَ (ب)، وَكُلُّ (أ) (ب)، فَبَعْضُ (ج) لَيْسَ (أ)» . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ: كَمَا يُقَالُ فِي الْمِثَالِ السَّابع وهو المركَّبُ من سالبة جزئية صغرى، وموجبةٍ كُلِّيَّةٍ كُبرى كقولنا : بعضُ ب ليسَ ج، وكلُّ أب؛ أي: بعضُ الإنسان ليس بحجر، وكل ناطق إنسان، فالصُّغرى تنعكس كنفسها كما يُقالُ في السَّابع. فإِنْ قِيْلَ : الصُّغرى سالبةٌ جزئيَّة، وهي لا تنعكسُ ؛ قُلْتُ : تحملُ الصُّغرى السالبة المذكورة على إحدى الخاصَّتين ؛ لأنَّهُ تقدَّمَ أنَّها تنعكس، هكذا صرَّحوا به. (قَوْلُهُ : كَمَا يُقَالُ: فِي الْمِثَالِ السَّابع) أي: وفي الثَّالث، والخامس، والسادس دون البقية. (قَوْلُهُ : بَعْضُ ج لَيْسَ هو (ب) أي : بعضُ الحجرِ ليس هو بإنسان، وهذا هو عکس الصُّغرى في الأصل. (قَوْلُهُ : وَكُلُّ (أ ب) أي : كلُّ ناطق إنسان، وهذا هو كُبرى الأصل. (قَوْلُهُ : فَبَعْضُ ج لَيْسَ (أ) أي : فبعضُ الحجرِ ليس بناطق.
Section: On the syllogism. It is that the minor premise is converted only by simple conversion, so that it returns to the second figure, and the desired conclusion is produced, as is said in the seventh example, for instance: 'Some (C) is not (B), and every (A) is (B), so some (C) is not (A).' Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'as is said in the seventh example') — it is composed of a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, as we say: 'Some B is not J' (i.e., some human is not stone) and 'Every A is B' (i.e., every rational being is human). So the minor premise converts as itself, as is said in the seventh. If it is said: The minor is a particular negative, and it does not convert; I say: That particular negative minor falls under one of the two special cases, because it has been established that it converts, thus they explicitly stated. (His saying: 'as is said in the seventh example') — i.e., and in the third, fifth, and sixth, excluding the rest. (His saying: 'Some C is not B' — i.e., some stone is not human, and this is the conversion of the original minor. (His saying: 'Every A is B' — i.e., every rational being is human, and this is the original major. (His saying: 'So some C is not A' — i.e., some stone is not rational.
Source pages p. 839

Commentary
This passage explains the method of reducing a syllogism from an invalid mood to a valid one by converting the minor premise. The seventh mood consists of a particular negative minor premise and a universal affirmative major premise. This mood is not valid as is, but by converting the minor premise (which is a particular negative) using simple conversion, we obtain a new syllogism that fits the second figure (where the middle term is the predicate of both premises). The commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies the example: 'Some human is not stone' becomes 'Some stone is not human' after conversion, and 'Every rational being is human' remains the major. The conclusion becomes 'Some stone is not rational'. The passage also addresses an objection: a particular negative normally does not convert. The response explains that this particular negative is one of the two special cases (e.g., when the subject and predicate are co-extensive or the subject is an individual) in which conversion is allowed. The commentator also notes that this method applies to the third, fifth, and sixth moods as well, but not to others.

العكس المستوي
الشكل الثاني
سالبة جزئية
الخاصَّتين

C) Contraposing the minor premise
D) There is no reduction possible

Key Takeaway
A particular negative minor premise can sometimes be converted, turning an invalid syllogism into a valid Festino mood in the second figure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following original seventh mood: 'Some B is not J' and 'Every A is B'. Convert the minor premise using simple conversion (assuming it falls under the special cases). Write the resulting valid syllogism (both premises and conclusion) and identify its figure and mood.
?
Check the validity of the following syllogism: 'Some intoxicants are not wine, all wine is fermented, therefore some intoxicants are not fermented.' If invalid, apply the conversion technique to see if it becomes valid.

Reducing the Fourth Syllogism to the Third Figure
Author explains converting the major premise to reduce the fourth syllogism to the third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a fourth-figure categorical syllogism to the third figure by converting the major premise and verify the validity of the resulting syllogism.

Core Idea
Convert the major premise to transform a fourth-figure syllogism into a valid third-figure one.

This passage teaches a specific reduction technique: converting the major premise of a fourth-figure syllogism to produce a third-figure syllogism. The method is illustrated with an example using 'human', 'animal', and 'stone'. Understanding this operation is essential for mastering syllogistic reduction, a core tool in Islamic logic for simplifying complex arguments and checking their validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism's premises.
Locate the minor and major premises in the Arabic text. The fourth mood has a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major: 'Every B is C' and 'Some A is not B'.
2
Apply conversion to the major premise.
Convert the particular negative major premise 'Some A is not B' to 'Some B is not A' using simple conversion, which is valid for particular negatives under the special cases recognized in the tradition.
This step changes the middle term's position.
3
Check the resulting figure.
After conversion, the middle term (B) becomes the subject of both premises, placing the syllogism in the third figure.
Third figure condition: middle term is subject of both premises.
4
Verify the conclusion follows.
From 'Every B is C' and 'Some B is not A', the conclusion 'Some C is not A' follows validly in the third figure (mood with universal affirmative minor and particular negative major).
The conclusion matches the original conclusion, confirming equivalence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(أَوْ) بِالرَّدِّ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الثَّالِثِ بِعَكْسِ الْكُبْرَى فَقَطْ؛ لِيَرْتَدَّ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الثَّالِثِ، كَمَا يُقَالُ فِي الْمِثَالِ الرَّابِعِ مَثَلاً: «كل (ب) (ج)، وبعض (ب) ليس هو (أ)، فبعضُ (ج) ليس هو (أ)) . العطار (قَوْلُهُ: بِعَكْسِ الْكُبْرَى) وهوَ يَجري في الرابع والأَوَّلِ والثَّاني والثَّالِثِ والخامس أيضاً، لكنَّهم لم يلتقُوا إليهِ لمثل ما مرَّ ، ولا يجري في السادس والسابع والثَّامنِ ؛ لامتناعِ سَلْبِ الصُّغرى في الشَّكلِ الثَّالث، وأيضاً يجري في الثاني الثالث، كَمَا يُقالُ في المثالِ الرَّابِعِ مثلاً : «كل (ب) (ج)، وبعض (ب) ليس هو (أ)، فبعضُ (ج) ليس هو (أ)) . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : كَمَا يُقَالُ فِي الْمِثَالِ الرَّابع وهو المركَّبُ من صغري موجبةٍ كُلِّيَّة، وَكُبرى سالبة جزئية كقولنا : كلُّ ب ج ، وبعضُ أليس ب؛ أي: كلُّ إنسان حيوان، وبعضُ الحجر ليس بإنسان. (قَوْلُهُ: كُلُّ ب (ج) أي : كُلُّ إنسانٍ .حيوان. (قَوْلُهُ : وَبَعْضُ ب ليسَ هُوَ (أ) أي: وبعضُ الإنسانِ ليس هو بحجرٍ، وهذا هو عكسُ كُبرى الرابع التي هي سالبةٌ جزئية، ويأتي فيهِ ما مرَّ في السَّابع من السُّؤالِ والجواب . (قَوْلُهُ : فَبَعْضُ ج ليسَ (أ) أي : فَبعض الحيوان ليس بحجرٍ.
(Or) by reduction to the third figure by converting the major premise only, so that it returns to the third figure, as is said in the fourth example, for instance: 'Every (B) is (C), and some (B) is not (A), so some (C) is not (A).' Al-Attar: (His saying: 'by converting the major premise') — it applies to the fourth, first, second, third, and fifth as well, but they did not resort to it for similar reasons as before, and it does not apply to the sixth, seventh, and eighth because of the impossibility of a negative minor in the third figure. Also it applies to the second and third, as is said in the fourth example, for instance: 'Every (B) is (C), and some (B) is not (A), so some (C) is not (A).' Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'as is said in the fourth example') — it is composed of a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major, as we say: 'Every B is C' (i.e., every human is animal) and 'Some A is not B' (i.e., some stone is not human). (His saying: 'Every B is C' — i.e., every human is animal. (His saying: 'And some B is not A' — i.e., some human is not stone, and this is the conversion of the major of the fourth, which is a particular negative, and the same question and answer as in the seventh apply to it. (His saying: 'So some C is not A' — i.e., some animal is not stone.
Source pages p. 839

Commentary
This passage explains the method of reducing a syllogism to the third figure by converting the major premise. The fourth mood consists of a universal affirmative minor premise and a particular negative major premise. By converting the major premise (a particular negative) via simple conversion, we obtain a new syllogism that fits the third figure (where the middle term is the subject of both premises). The example given: minor 'Every human is animal', major 'Some stone is not human' becomes 'Some human is not stone' after conversion, yielding 'Some animal is not stone'. Al-Attar notes that this method applies to the first, second, third, fourth, and fifth moods, but not to the sixth, seventh, and eighth because the third figure requires a particular minor, and those moods have a negative minor which cannot be placed in the third figure. Al-Dasuqi elaborates the example and points out that the same objection about converting a particular negative arises here as in the seventh mood, and the same answer applies (it falls under the two special cases).

Key Terms

الشكل الثالث

Key Takeaway
Reducing a fourth-figure syllogism to the third figure by converting the major premise is possible only when the major premise is convertible (under the special cases), and it allows the use of known valid moods in the third figure.

The Fifth Form (al-Iftirad) Is Not a Genuine Syllogism
Critical refinement of the fifth form's status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fifth form (al-iftirad) and explain why it is not considered a true syllogism according to later logicians.

Core Idea
Al-iftirad is not a syllogism—only a manipulation of terms to resolve confusion in particulars.

In this passage, al-Attar clarifies that the fifth form of syllogism, known as al-iftirad, was not included by the author of the matn among the four figures. Drawing on the investigation in Sharh al-Isharat, he explains that this form is not a true syllogism but merely a manipulation of the subject and predicate. Understanding this distinction helps us recognize what genuinely counts as a valid deductive argument in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Locate the fifth form in the text
Find the phrase 'الخامس الافتراض' in the Arabic. This designates the form under discussion.
2
Extract the main verdict
What does al-Attar say about this form? He states it is 'ليس بقياس' (not a syllogism). Identify the sentence containing this verdict.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Why is it not a syllogism? The text says it is 'ليس إلا تصرفاً ما في الموضوع والمحمول' (nothing but a manipulation of subject and predicate). Identify this reason.
4
Identify the function
For what purpose was it presented in syllogistic form? The text says it was 'لإزالة اشتباه يعرض لبعض الأذهان من جهة تعين الموضوع في الجزئيات' (to remove confusion about the subject in particulars).
5
Connect to the broader framework
How does this refine our understanding of a valid syllogism? A genuine syllogism requires proper structure and middle term; al-iftirad lacks this, so it is not a figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العطار والخامس الافتراض، لكنَّه لم يذكره المصنِّفُ في واحدٍ من الأشكال الأربعة، والشَّارِحُ تَبِعَهُ؛ لأنَّ التَّحقيق فيهِ على ما ذكر في شرح الإشاراتِ أنَّه ليس بقياس، فضلاً عن أن يكون شكلاً مِنَ الأشكال ؛ لأنَّه ليس إلا تصرُّفاً ما في الموضوع والمحمول، وإنَّما أُوردَ على صورة القياس؛ لإزالة اشتباه يعرضُ لبعض الأذهانِ من جهة تعين الموضوع في الجزئيَّاتِ، وَلِهذَا ؛ لم يستعملوه في الكُلِّيَّاتِ إلا عند الضَّرورةِ، قال رجب أفندي : وَفِي بعض نسخ المتن هكذا : (وضابط شرائط الأربعة أَنَّهُ لا بُدَّ إِمَّا مِنْ عمومِ مَوضوعيَّةِ الأوسَطِ مَعَ مُلاقاتِهِ لِلأصغرِ بالفعلِ أَوْ حَمْلِهِ على الأكبر، وإمَّا من عموم موضوعيَّةِ الأكبر معَ الاختلافِ في الكيف معَ مُنافاة نسبةِ الأوسط إلى ذاتِ وصفِ الأكبر لِنِسبَتِهِ إلى ذاتِ الأصغرِ) انتهى، ولم يشرحه هذا الشارح لعدم وجودِهِ في نسخته التي شرحها، وفي سبب عدم وجودِهِ فيها وجهان؛ الأوَّلُ: أنَّ المصنِّفَ لم يحرِّرْهُ حينَ ألَّفَ المتنَ، وانتشرَتِ النُّسَخ في الأطرافِ، ثمَّ ألحقَهُ إليهِ وَنسخةُ الشَّارحِ مِنَ النُّسَخِ المنتشرة قبل الإلحاق؛ يدلّ عليه وجوده في أكثر النسخ، وعدم وجودِهِ في بعضها ، والثَّاني : أَنَّهُ حرَّرَهُ حِينَ أَلَّفَهُ، وانتشرَتِ النُّسَخُ أيضاً، ثمَّ ضربَهُ وأخرجَهُ مِنَ المتنِ لِكَوْنِهِ مِمَّا لا حاجة إليه بعد ذكرِ شرائط
Al-Attar: And the fifth is al-iftirad (presupposition), but the author (of the matn) did not mention it in any of the four figures, and the commentator followed him. Because the investigation into it, as mentioned in Sharh al-Isharat, is that it is not a syllogism, let alone being a figure among the figures; because it is nothing but a certain manipulation of the subject and predicate. It was only presented in the form of a syllogism to remove a confusion that occurs to some minds regarding the determination of the subject in particulars. Hence they did not use it in universals except when necessary. Rajab Afendi said: And in some copies of the matn (text) (it is) thus: 'And the rule for the conditions of the four (figures) is that it is necessary either from the generality of being a subject of the middle term with its actual meeting of the minor, or its being predicated of the major, or from the generality of being a subject of the major with the difference in quality and the incompatibility of the relation of the middle to the essence of the description of the major with its relation to the essence of the minor.' End quote. And this commentator did not explain it because it was not present in his copy that he commented on. And concerning the reason for its absence, there are two opinions: First, that the author did not finalize it when he composed the matn, and copies spread in the regions, then he appended it later, and the commentator's copy was among the copies that spread before the appendage; this is indicated by its presence in most copies and its absence in some. Second, that he finalized it when he authored it, and copies also spread, then he struck it and removed it from the matn because it is something unnecessary after mentioning the conditions.
Source pages p. 840

Commentary
This passage discusses the status of the fifth form of syllogism, known as al-iftirad. The commentator al-Attar states that the author of the matn did not include this form among the four figures, and the commentator (presumably the author of the sharh, al-Dasuqi) followed him. The reason, based on the investigation in Sharh al-Isharat (by Nasir al-Din al-Tusi), is that this form is not a true syllogism at all, let alone a separate figure. It is merely a manipulation of the subject and predicate, presented in the form of a syllogism only to remove confusion that may arise when the subject is determined in particular judgments. Therefore, logicians did not use it for universal statements except when necessary. Then the passage quotes Rajab Afandi, who mentions a missing passage found in some copies of the matn. That passage contains a rule (ضابط) for the conditions of the four figures. The commentator (i.e., the author of this sharh) did not comment on it because it was not in his copy. Two explanations are given for its absence: either the author added it later after the initial copies had spread, or he removed it after writing because it was unnecessary after mentioning the conditions. This textual note highlights the fluidity of manuscript transmission.

Key Terms
الخامس الافتراض
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It was added later
D) It was too complex

Key Takeaway
The fifth form (al-iftirad) is not a valid syllogistic figure but a mere manipulation of terms, used only for clarifying particular judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Syllogism

Example of the Fourth Figure with Explanation
Al-Dasuqi explains an example of the fourth syllogistic figure with its mood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth figure of a categorical syllogism, extract its premises, and derive the conclusion using conversion of the particular negative major premise.

Core Idea
The fourth figure requires converting the major premise to reach a valid conclusion.

In this lesson, we examine al-Dasuqi's concrete example of a fourth figure syllogism using human, animal, and stone. By working through the steps, you will see how the major premise is converted and how the conclusion follows. Pay attention to the conversion of the particular negative—it is key to the syllogism's validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor and major premises
The minor premise is 'Every B is J' (universal affirmative), e.g., every human is an animal. The major premise is 'Some A is not B' (particular negative), e.g., some stone is not a human. Note the order: the middle term B appears as subject of the minor and predicate of the major.
This order defines the fourth figure.
2
Convert the major premise
Al-Dasuqi converts 'Some A is not B' to 'Some B is not A'. This step is necessary to align the terms for the conclusion. However, recall that this conversion is not universally valid for all particular negatives.
The reference to 'the seventh' indicates a prior discussion on this conversion.
3
Extract the conclusion
After conversion, the premises become: minor 'Every B is J' and major 'Some B is not A'. The conclusion follows as 'Some J is not A' (particular negative). In the example: some animal is not a stone.
The conclusion uses the predicate of the minor (J) and the subject of the original major (A).
4
Check validity conditions
The conversion of O (particular negative) to O is not always true. For instance, 'Some animals are not dogs' is true, but 'Some dogs are not animals' is false. Validity here depends on the specific terms; the syllogism form itself is not universally valid.
This highlights the importance of evaluating both form and content.

(قَوْلُهُ : كَمَا يُقَالُ فِي الْمِثَالِ الرَّابع وهو المركَّبُ من صغري موجبةٍ كُلِّيَّة، وَكُبرى سالبة جزئية كقولنا : كلُّ ب ج ، وبعضُ أليس ب؛ أي: كلُّ إنسان حيوان، وبعضُ الحجر ليس بإنسان. (قَوْلُهُ: كُلُّ ب (ج) أي : كُلُّ إنسانٍ .حيوان. (قَوْلُهُ : وَبَعْضُ ب ليسَ هُوَ (أ) أي: وبعضُ الإنسانِ ليس هو بحجرٍ، وهذا هو عكسُ كُبرى الرابع التي هي سالبةٌ جزئية، ويأتي فيهِ ما مرَّ في السَّابع من السُّؤالِ والجواب . (قَوْلُهُ : فَبَعْضُ ج ليسَ (أ) أي : فَبعض الحيوان ليس بحجرٍ.)
His statement: 'As is said in the fourth example' – and it is composed of a universal affirmative minor premise and a particular negative major premise, as in our saying: 'Every B is J', and 'Some A is not B'; i.e., 'Every human is an animal', and 'Some stone is not a human'. His statement: 'Every B is J' – i.e., every human is an animal. His statement: 'And some B is not A' – i.e., some human is not a stone, and this is the conversion of the major premise of the fourth, which is a particular negative, and what was mentioned in the seventh regarding the question and answer applies here. His statement: 'So some J is not A' – i.e., some animal is not a stone.
Source pages p. 840

Commentary
This passage presents an example of the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism, as explained by al-Dasuqi. The author first states the compound premise: a universal affirmative (Every B is J) and a particular negative (Some A is not B), leading to a particular negative conclusion (Some J is not A). Al-Dasuqi then substitutes concrete terms from the sciences: 'every human is an animal' (universal affirmative) and 'some stone is not a human' (particular negative), yielding 'some animal is not a stone'. He notes that the major premise 'Some A is not B' has been converted to 'Some B is not A' (i.e., 'some human is not a stone'), which is the converse of the original particular negative. He references a prior discussion (the 'seventh') regarding the validity of such conversion. The example follows the pattern of the fourth figure: minor premise (subject = middle term), major premise (middle term = predicate of subject?), and conclusion with the subject from the major premise and predicate from the minor. Here, the middle term is 'human' (B), the subject of the minor and predicate of the major. This demonstrates a valid mood of the fourth figure with a particular negative conclusion.

Key Terms
الشكل الرابع
صغرى
كبرى
عكس

A) Animal
B) Human
C) Stone
D) Not specified

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure syllogism with a particular negative major requires conversion of that premise, but the conversion is not universally valid; validity depends on the specific terms.

Convert the major premise 'Some stone is not a human' into its converse. Provide a counterexample (using different terms) showing that this conversion is not always true.

The Fifth Figure: Why It Is Not a Genuine Syllogism
Al-'Attar explains the rejection of the fifth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the hypothetical figure (al-iftiradi) is not considered a valid syllogism in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The fifth figure is not a syllogism—it is only a manipulation of subject and predicate.

In this lesson, we examine al-'Attar's commentary on al-Tadhhib. He clarifies why the so-called 'fifth figure' (al-iftiradi) is omitted from the four figures: it is not a genuine syllogism but a heuristic device for particulars. Understanding this distinction is crucial for rigorous logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about the fifth figure
Find where the passage states that the author and commentator did not include the fifth figure among the four.
2
Understand the reason for omission
The passage says verification (tahqiq) shows it is 'not a syllogism' (laysa bi-qiyas). This is the key ground.
3
Analyze its description
It is called 'a manipulation in subject and predicate' (tasarruf ma fi al-mawdu' wa al-mahmul), used only to remove confusion in particulars.
4
Note its limited use
It is not employed in universals (kulliyyat) except when necessary, implying its inferior logical status.
5
Conclude accordingly
Summarize that the fifth figure is not a genuine figure because it lacks syllogistic validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخامس الافتراض، لكنَّه لم يذكره المصنِّفُ في واحدٍ من الأشكال الأربعة، والشَّارِحُ تَبِعَهُ؛ لأنَّ التَّحقيق فيهِ على ما ذكر في شرح الإشاراتِ أنَّه ليس بقياس، فضلاً عن أن يكون شكلاً مِنَ الأشكال ؛ لأنَّه ليس إلا تصرُّفاً ما في الموضوع والمحمول، وإنَّما أُوردَ على صورة القياس؛ لإزالة اشتباه يعرضُ لبعض الأذهانِ من جهة تعين الموضوع في الجزئيَّاتِ، وَلِهذَا ؛ لم يستعملوه في الكُلِّيَّاتِ إلا عند الضَّرورةِ، قال رجب أفندي : وَفِي بعض نسخ المتن هكذا : (وضابط شرائط الأربعة أَنَّهُ لا بُدَّ إِمَّا مِنْ عمومِ مَوضوعيَّةِ الأوسَطِ مَعَ مُلاقاتِهِ لِلأصغرِ بالفعلِ أَوْ حَمْلِهِ على الأكبر، وإمَّا من عموم موضوعيَّةِ الأكبر معَ الاختلافِ في الكيف معَ مُنافاة نسبةِ الأوسط إلى ذاتِ وصفِ الأكبر لِنِسبَتِهِ إلى ذاتِ الأصغرِ) انتهى، ولم يشرحه هذا الشارح لعدم وجودِهِ في نسخته التي شرحها، وفي سبب عدم وجودِهِ فيها وجهان؛ الأوَّلُ: أنَّ المصنِّفَ لم يحرِّرْهُ حينَ ألَّفَ المتنَ، وانتشرَتِ النُّسَخ في الأطرافِ، ثمَّ ألحقَهُ إليهِ وَنسخةُ الشَّارحِ مِنَ النُّسَخِ المنتشرة قبل الإلحاق؛ يدلّ عليه وجوده في أكثر النسخ، وعدم وجودِهِ في بعضها ، والثَّاني : أَنَّهُ حرَّرَهُ حِينَ أَلَّفَهُ، وانتشرَتِ النُّسَخُ أيضاً، ثمَّ ضربَهُ وأخرجَهُ مِنَ المتنِ لِكَونِهِ مِمَّا لا حاجة إليه بعد ذكرِ شرائط الأشكال وتفصيلها، فحينئذ تكونُ نُسختُهُ مِنَ النُّسَخِ المنقَّحَةِ، والنسخ الَّتي يوجد فيها هذا الصَّابطُ مِنَ الغيرِ المنقَّحةِ لِكَونِهَا مُنتشرةً قبلَ الضَّربِ والإخراج، ويمكنُ أن يكونَ هَهُنا وجه ثالث، وهو أنْ يُقالَ: إنَّ هذا مِن ألحاق البعض لا مِنَ المصنفِ، وقد نقل هذهِ الزّيادة مير أبو الفتح في شرح المتن، وشرحها واعترضَهَا، ونقل ذلك رجب أفندي، وتركناهُ لِقِلَّةِ جَدواهُ، وَلَا يَخفى أنَّ التَّعبيرَ بِالوجه في كلام رجب أفندي مِمَّا لَا وجهَ لَهُ ، بل اللائق في مثلِهِ ذكر الاحتمال.
And the fifth is the hypothetical [figure], but the author did not mention it in any of the four figures, and the commentator followed him; because the verification concerning it, according to what is mentioned in the Commentary on the Indications, is that it is not a syllogism, let alone be a figure among the figures; because it is nothing but some manipulation in the subject and predicate, and it is only presented in the form of a syllogism to remove confusion that occurs to some minds due to the determination of the subject in particulars, and therefore they did not use it in universals except when necessary. Rajab Efendi said: 'And in some copies of the text it is as follows: (And the rule of the conditions of the four is that it is necessary either from the generality of the subjecthood of the middle term together with its meeting the minor actually, or its predication on the major; or from the generality of the subjecthood of the major together with the difference in quality, with the contradiction of the relation of the middle to the essence of the description of the major to its relation to the essence of the minor.)' End. And this commentator did not explain it because it was not present in his copy which he commented upon. Regarding the reason for its absence in his copy, there are two aspects: First, that the author did not finalize it when he composed the text, and the copies spread to the regions, then he appended it to it, and the commentator's copy was among the copies spread before the addition; this is indicated by its presence in most copies and its absence in some. Second, that he finalized it when he composed it, and the copies also spread, then he struck it out and removed it from the text because it is unnecessary after mentioning the conditions of the figures and their details. In that case, his copy is among the revised copies, and the copies that contain this rule are among the unrevised ones because they spread before the striking out and removal. And it is possible that there is a third aspect here, which is to say that this is from the additions of some [copiers] and not from the author. Mir Abu al-Fath transmitted this addition in his commentary on the text, and explained it and objected to it, and Rajab Efendi transmitted that. And we left it due to its lack of benefit. And it is not hidden that the expression 'aspect' in the speech of Rajab Efendi has no basis; rather, in such a case it is appropriate to mention 'probability'.
Source pages p. 840

Commentary
This passage discusses the so-called 'fifth figure' (al-iftiradi) and its omission from the author's text. Al-'Attar explains that the fifth figure is not a genuine syllogism but merely a manipulation of subject and predicate, used to remove confusion in particular cases. Hence, it was not included among the four figures. He then cites Rajab Efendi, who notes that some manuscripts contain an additional rule (ضابط) regarding the conditions of the four figures. Al-'Attar explores three possible reasons for the rule's absence in the commentator's copy: (1) the author appended it later after initial spread of copies; (2) the author originally included it but later removed it as unnecessary; (3) it is a spurious addition by a copyist. He concludes that the term 'aspect' (وجه) used by Rajab Efendi is inappropriate; 'probability' (احتمال) is better. This passage illustrates textual criticism in the logical tradition and underscores the historical fluidity of such texts.

Key Terms
الافتراض
القياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-'Attar, why is the fifth figure not included among the figures?
A) Because the author forgot it
B) Because it is not a genuine syllogism
C) Because it is only for particulars
D) Because it was added later

Key Takeaway
The fifth figure is not a genuine syllogism but a heuristic manipulation for particulars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Explain in your own words why the hypothetical figure is not considered a valid syllogism, using the criteria from this passage.
?
Find an argument from a kalam text that appears to use a fifth-figure structure and show why it fails to meet the conditions of a genuine syllogism.

Manuscript Variations: The ضابط Rule and Its Authenticity
Al-'Attar discusses manuscript variations of a rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the authenticity of a manuscript addition using the three aspects discussed by al-'Attar.

Core Idea
Textual criticism shows that a rule's presence or absence may indicate authorial revision or scribal addition.

This lesson analyzes a manuscript variant mentioned by al-'Attar: a rule (ضابط) summarizing the conditions of the four figures that appears in some copies but not in the commentator's. Al-'Attar presents three explanations for its absence, demonstrating the importance of textual criticism in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed text
Locate the quote from Rajab Efendi: the ضابط rule on conditions of the four figures.
2
Understand why it was not explained
The commentator did not explain it because it was absent from his copy.
3
Examine the first aspect
The author may have appended the rule later, after copies had spread; the commentator's copy was from before the addition.
4
Examine the second aspect
The author may have originally included the rule but later removed it as unnecessary; the commentator's copy is from a revised version.
5
Consider the third possibility
The rule might be a scribal addition, not from the author at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Syllogism

Definition and Division of Compound Conjunctive Syllogism
Author defines and divides compound conjunctive syllogism from conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given compound conjunctive syllogism as either categorical or conditional based on its premises.

Core Idea
The compound conjunctive syllogism includes conditionals, not just categoricals.

This passage introduces a crucial division: the compound conjunctive syllogism splits into categorical and conditional. Understanding this division is essential because conditional syllogisms have distinct rules and applications, especially in theology and geometry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis for division
The author divides the compound conjunctive syllogism based on premise composition: if all premises are categorical, it's categorical; otherwise, it's conditional.
2
Distinguish pure categorical vs conditional
Pure categorical syllogisms have only categorical premises. Conditional syllogisms have at least one conditional premise, whether pure conditionals or mixed with categoricals.
3
Note the scope of the conjunctive syllogism
The conjunctive syllogism includes both categoricals and conditionals, contrary to those who restrict it to categoricals only.
4
Recognize the need for conditional syllogisms
Even though Aristotle did not cover them, conditional syllogisms are necessary because their judgments differ from categoricals, and they appear in fields like geometry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ الِاقْتِرَانِي الْمُرَكَّبِ مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ
اعلَمْ أنَّ الاقتراني المركَّبَ على ما مرَّ ، ينقسم إلى: حملي وشرطيّ؛ لأنَّه إن تركَّبَ منَ الحمليَّاتِ المحضة فحملي، وإن لم يتركَّب منها، بل تركَّبَ منَ الشَّرطيَّاتِ المحضة، أو منَ الشَّرطيَّاتِ والحمليَّاتِ فشرطي .
(قَوْلُهُ : الْمُرَكَّب مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ) أي: فالقياسُ الاقتراني يتركَّبُ من الحمليَّاتِ والشَّرطيات، خلافاً لِمَنْ حَصَّهُ بالحمليات .
وقالَ عبد الحكيمِ : كَمَا أَنَّ الحمليَّاتِ فطريَّات ونظريات؛ كذلكَ الشَّرطيات قد تكونُ فطريَّةٌ كَقَولِنَا : كُلَّما كانَتِ الشَّمس طالعةٌ ؛ فالنَّهارُ مَوجود، وقد تكون نظريَّةٌ كَقَولِنَا : كُلَّمَا وُجِدَ الممكن ؛ وُجِدَ الواجب الوجود، فَمَسَّتِ الحاجة إلى معرفة الأقيسَةِ الشَّرطيَّةِ الاقترانيَّةِ، لا سيما في الهندسة المشتمل عليها كتاب إقليدس، وبسبب أن أرسطو لم يورد هذا الباب في التَّعليم ؛ زعم بعضُهُم أَنَّهُ لَا حاجة إليهِ؛ لأنَّ معرفةً الاقترانيَّاتِ الحمليَّةِ تُغني عن ذكرِهَا ، وليس بشيءٍ لِمَا بينَ أحكامِهَا مِنَ الاختلافِ الواضح.
Chapter: On the compound conjunctive syllogism composed of conditionals. Know that the compound conjunctive, as previously mentioned, divides into categorical and conditional. Because if it is composed solely of categoricals, it is categorical; but if it is not composed of them, rather composed solely of conditionals or of conditionals and categoricals, then it is conditional. (His saying: “composed of conditionals”) means: the conjunctive syllogism is composed of categoricals and conditionals, contrary to one who restricted it to categoricals. And Abd al-Hakim said: Just as categoricals are innate and theoretical, likewise conditionals may be innate, like our saying: Whenever the sun is rising, then daytime exists; and may be theoretical, like our saying: Whenever the contingent exists, then the Necessary Existent exists. Thus there is need for knowledge of conditional conjunctive syllogisms, especially in geometry contained in Euclid’s book. And because Aristotle did not include this chapter in the teaching, some claimed there is no need for it, since knowledge of categorical conjunctives suffices instead of mentioning it. But this is nonsense, due to the clear difference between their judgments.
Source pages p. 842

Commentary
This passage defines and divides the compound conjunctive syllogism that is composed of conditional propositions. The author begins by stating that the compound conjunctive syllogism (as previously defined) is divided into two types: categorical and conditional. The basis for this division is the nature of the premises: if all premises are pure categoricals, the syllogism is categorical; if the premises are not all categoricals but include conditionals (either all conditionals or a mix of categoricals and conditionals), then it is conditional. The commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies that the phrase “composed of conditionals” means that the conjunctive syllogism can be composed of categoricals and conditionals, contrary to those who restrict it to only categoricals. Then a marginal note from Abd al-Hakim expands on the need for conditional syllogisms. He draws an analogy: just as categorical premises can be self-evident (fitri) or theoretical (nazari), so too conditionals can be self-evident (e.g., “Whenever the sun rises, daytime exists”) or theoretical (e.g., “Whenever the contingent exists, the Necessary Existent exists”). Because of this, there is a need to study conditional conjunctive syllogisms, especially in geometry (as in Euclid’s Elements). Some have claimed that since Aristotle did not include this chapter, it is unnecessary, and that categorical conjunctives suffice. The author refutes this, stating that such a claim is baseless because the judgments of conditional and categorical syllogisms differ clearly. Thus, this passage establishes the definition and classification of the compound conjunctive syllogism from conditionals, and argues for its necessity.

Key Terms
القياس الاقتراني
شرطي
حملي
محضة

A) It contains at least one conditional premise.
B) It contains only conditional premises.
C) It contains only categorical premises.
D) It is composed of a mixture of conditional and disjunctive premises.

Key Takeaway
The compound conjunctive syllogism is either categorical (all premises categorical) or conditional (at least one conditional premise).

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
The Compound Categorical Syllogism: Categorical and Hypothetical
Author defines the compound categorical syllogism and its two main divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a compound categorical syllogism composed purely of categoricals and one that includes conditionals or disjunctives.

Core Idea
The compound categorical syllogism extends beyond categoricals to include conditionals and disjunctives.

We now explore the compound categorical syllogism (al-qiyas al-iqtirani al-murakkab), which the author divides into two main types: categorical (hamli) and hypothetical (sharti). The categorical is composed purely of categorical propositions, while the hypothetical includes conditionals or disjunctives. This lesson clarifies the classification and its importance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main divisions
The author states that the compound categorical syllogism divides into categorical and hypothetical. The categorical is purely categorical; the hypothetical is not purely categorical.
2
Understand the hypothetical definition
Hypothetical includes syllogisms composed purely of conditionals, purely of disjunctives, or a mix of conditionals/disjunctives with categoricals.
This contrasts with some logicians who restrict 'categorical' to only categoricals.
3
Recognize the need for hypothetical syllogisms
The commentator Abd al-Hakim notes that just as categoricals have innate and theoretical types, so do conditionals. Examples from geometry and kalam show their necessity.
Aristotle did not include this topic, but later scholars found it essential.
4
Note the five primary divisions
The hypothetical syllogism under the categorical has five divisions, based on combinations of conditional and disjunctive premises.
The first two are covered in this source: two conditionals, two disjunctives.

بعده .
(قَوْلُهُ : الْمُرَكَّبُ مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ) أي : وحدها أو مع الحمليَّاتِ كَمَا يدلُّ عليهِ مَا
(قَوْلُهُ : وَإِنْ لَمْ يَتَرَكَّبْ مِنْهَا ... إِلَخ) تصريح بأن ليس المراد بالقياس الشَّرطي المركَبِ مِنَ الشَّرطيَّاتِ ؛ بلْ مَا لَا يتركَّبُ مِنَ الحمليَّاتِ ؛ سواءٌ تركَّبَ مِنَ الشَّرطيَّاتِ المحضةِ أَوْ مِنَ الشَّرطيَّاتِ والحمليَّاتِ، فَلِهذا كانت أقسامُهُ الأوَّلِيَّةُ خمسةٌ . (قَوْلُهُ : الْمَحْضَةِ) المراد من كونها محضةٌ أنَّه لم ينضم إليها شَرطيَّةُ الظَّاهِرِ كَمَا هو ظاهرُ ، لَا كونها ليست شبيهة بالشَّرطيَّةِ.
والمصنِّفُ لَمَّا فرغ من الحملي ؛ شرع في الشَّرطيّ من الاقترانيّ،
(الشَّرْطِيُّ مِنَ الِاقْتِرَانِي) ينقسم إلى خمسة أقسام؛ لأنَّه :

(أَوْ) من (مُنْفَصِلَتَيْن)، وهو القسمُ الثَّاني، كَقولِنَا : كلُّ عددٍ إِمَّا زوج أو فرد، وكلُّ زوج إمَّا زوج الزوج، أو زوج الفردِ،
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: إِمَّا أَنْ يَتَرَكَّبَ مِنْ مُتَّصِلَتَيْنِ قالَ الحفيد : وشرائط إنتاج هذا القياس الشَّرطي الاقتراني ما سبق من الأشكال الأربعةِ .
(قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ مِنْ مُنْفَصِلَتَيْنِ شرطُ إنتاجه إيجاب المقدمتين، وكلية إحداهما،
وصدقُ منع الخلو عنهما .
(قَوْلُهُ: إِمَّا زَوْجُ الزَّوْجِ كَالعشرين والثمانية، فالعشرين زوج، وهي منقسمة إلى عشرة وعشرة وكلّ مِنَ القسمين زوج.
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : فَشَرْطِيٌّ ) أي : أنَّ القياسَ الشَّرطيّ لا يختصُّ بِمَا تركَّبَ مِنَ الشَّرطيَّاتِ فقط، وهذا اصطلاح لا حجر فيهِ، فلا يضرُّ أَنَّهم خصَّصُوا الحمليَّ بِمَا تركَّبَ مِنَ الحمليات فقط
(قَوْلُهُ : شَرَعَ فِي الشَّرْطِيّ مِنَ الِافْتِرَانِي الأظهرُ والأخصرُ إسقاط قَوْلِهِ : (مِنَ الاقتراني).
(قَوْلُهُ : إِمَّا زَوْجُ الزَّوْجِ أَوْ زَوْجُ الْفَرْدِ لأَنَّهُ إِمَّا أَنْ ينقسم إلى مُتساويَيْنِ أو لا ؛ الثَّاني الفردُ كَالثَّلاثةِ، والأَوَّلُ إِمَّا أَنْ ينقسم إلى المنقسم بمتساويين أو لا ؛ الأَوَّلُ زوجُ الزَّوحِ كَالثَّمانيةِ والثَّاني زوج الفردِ كَالسِّتَّةِ .
Section: On the compound categorical syllogism composed of conditionals
Know that the compound categorical, as previously, divides into: categorical and hypothetical; because if it is composed of purely categoricals, it is categorical; and if it is not composed of them, but rather composed of purely conditionals, or of conditionals and categoricals, it is hypothetical.
Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: composed of conditionals) meaning: the categorical syllogism is composed of categoricals and conditionals, contrary to whoever confined it to categoricals.
Al-Attar: And a section: on the categorical syllogism... etc.
Abd al-Hakim said: Just as categoricals are innate and theoretical, likewise conditionals may be innate, like our saying: Whenever the sun is shining, then daytime exists; and may be theoretical, like our saying: Whenever the contingent exists, the Necessary Existence exists. Thus there is a need to know hypothetical categorical syllogisms, especially in geometry included in Euclid's book. And because Aristotle did not include this chapter in his teaching, some claimed there is no need for it, since knowledge of categorical categoricals suffices without mentioning them. But this is nothing, because of the clear difference between their rulings.
(His saying: composed of conditionals) i.e., alone or with categoricals, as indicated by what follows.
(His saying: and if not composed of them... etc.) is a declaration that by the hypothetical syllogism is not meant the one composed of conditionals; rather, whatever is not composed of categoricals, whether composed of pure conditionals or of conditionals and categoricals. Thus its primary divisions are five.
(His saying: pure) intended by being pure is that no apparent conditional has joined it, as is apparent, not that it is not similar to a conditional.
And when the author finished with the categorical, he began with the hypothetical of the categorical.
(The hypothetical of the categorical) divides into five divisions:
(Either it is composed of two conditionals), which is the first division, like our saying: If the sun is shining, then daytime exists; and whenever daytime exists, the earth is illuminated. It yields: If the sun is shining, then the earth is illuminated.
(Or) of (two disjunctives), which is the second division, like our saying: Every number is either even or odd; and every even is either even of even or even of odd.
Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: either composed of two conditionals) Al-Hafid said: The conditions for production of this hypothetical categorical syllogism are what preceded among the four figures.
(His saying: or from two disjunctives) Condition for its production is affirmation of both premises and universality of one of them, and the truth of the prevention of voidness of both.
(His saying: either even of even like twenty and eight, for twenty is even and it divides into ten and ten, and each of the two parts is even.
Al-Attar: (His saying: then it is hypothetical) meaning: the hypothetical syllogism is not confined to what is composed of conditionals only, and this is a convention with no restriction; so it does no harm that they confined the categorical to what is composed of categoricals only.
(His saying: he began with the hypothetical of the categorical) the more apparent and concise is to drop his saying: (of the categorical).
(His saying: either even of even or even of odd) because it either divides into two equals or not; the second is odd like three; the first either divides into divisible by two equals or not; the first is even of even like eight; the second is even of odd like six.
Source pages p. 843

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory section on the compound hypothetical syllogism. The author begins by stating that the compound categorical syllogism (al-qiyas al-iqtirani al-murakkab) divides into two types: categorical (hamli) and hypothetical (sharti). The categorical is composed purely of categorical propositions, while the hypothetical is composed either purely of conditional propositions or a mix of conditional and categorical. The commentators then clarify that some scholars mistakenly restricted the categorical syllogism to only categorical propositions, but the author includes conditionals. A need for this study is justified by examples from Euclidean geometry and kalam (such as the necessary existent). The hypothetical syllogism is then divided into five primary divisions based on whether its premises are conditionals (muttasila) or disjunctives (munfasila). The first division is when both premises are conditionals, e.g., 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists; and if daytime exists, the earth is illuminated' yields 'If the sun is shining, then the earth is illuminated.' The second division is when both premises are disjunctives, e.g., 'Every number is either even or odd; and every even is either even of even or even of odd.' The conditions for validity of these syllogisms are noted: the first follows the four figures of categorical syllogisms, while the second requires both premises affirmative and one universal, with the condition of preventing voidness (man' al-khuluww).

Key Terms
القياس الاقتراني
شَرْطِي
حملي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the compound categorical syllogism is divided into which two main types?
A) Conditional and disjunctive
B) Categorical and hypothetical
C) Simple and compound
D) Innate and theoretical

Key Takeaway
The compound categorical syllogism includes both purely categorical and hypothetical forms, with the latter further divided into five types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the following syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Determine whether it is a compound categorical or hypothetical syllogism. Explain.
?

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
The First Type: Two Conditionals (Muttasilatayn)
Author presents the first division: a syllogism composed of two conditionals, with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid compound hypothetical syllogism of the first type (two conditionals) and verify its validity.

Core Idea
When both premises are conditionals, the conclusion is also a conditional, following the four figures of categorical syllogisms.

The first division of the hypothetical syllogism is composed of two conditional (muttasila) premises. The author provides an example: 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists; and if daytime exists, the earth is illuminated; therefore if the sun is shining, the earth is illuminated.' We will analyze this form and its validity conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the form
Recognize that the syllogism has two conditional premises of the form 'If P then Q' and 'If Q then R'.
This is a chain of implications.
2
Check the middle term
The middle term is 'daytime exists', which connects the two premises.
This follows the pattern of the first figure in categorical syllogisms.
3
Derive the conclusion
The conclusion is also a conditional: 'If P then R'.
4
Verify validity conditions
Al-Hafid (mentioned in commentary) says the conditions for production are the same as the four figures of categoricals. The example is valid because the antecedent of the first matches the consequent of the second? Actually, it's standard hypothetical syllogism.
The validity is unconditional; the conclusion follows necessarily.

(قَوْلُهُ : الْمُرَكَّبُ مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ) أي : وحدها أو مع الحمليَّاتِ كَمَا يدلُّ عليهِ مَا
(قَوْلُهُ : وَإِنْ لَمْ يَتَرَكَّبْ مِنْهَا ... إِلَخ) تصريح بأن ليس المراد بالقياس الشَّرطي المركَبِ مِنَ الشَّرطيَّاتِ ؛ بلْ مَا لَا يتركَّبُ مِنَ الحمليَّاتِ ؛ سواءٌ تركَّبَ مِنَ الشَّرطيَّاتِ المحضةِ أَوْ مِنَ الشَّرطيَّاتِ والحمليَّاتِ، فَلِهذا كانت أقسامُهُ الأوَّلِيَّةُ خمسةٌ . (قَوْلُهُ : الْمَحْضَةِ) المراد من كونها محضةٌ أنَّه لم ينضم إليها شَرطيَّةُ الظَّاهِرِ كَمَا هو ظاهرُ ، لَا كونها ليست شبيهة بالشَّرطيَّةِ.
والمصنِّفُ لَمَّا فرغ من الحملي ؛ شرع في الشَّرطيّ من الاقترانيّ،
(الشَّرْطِيُّ مِنَ الِاقْتِرَانِي) ينقسم إلى خمسة أقسام؛ لأنَّه :
(إِمَّا أَنْ يَتَرَكَّبَ مِنْ مُتَّصِلَتَيْن)، وهو القسم الأوَّلُ، كَقولِنَا : «إن كانَتِ الشَّمسُ طالعةٌ فالنَّهارُ موجودٌ ، وكلَّما كان النَّهارُ موجوداً فالأرضُ مضيئةٌ»، ينتج : إن كانتِ الشَّمس طالعة، فالأرضُ مضيئة». (أَوْ) من (مُنْفَصِلَتَيْن)، وهو القسمُ الثَّاني، كَقولِنَا : كلُّ عددٍ إِمَّا زوج أو فرد، وكلُّ زوج إمَّا زوج الزوج، أو زوج الفردِ،
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: إِمَّا أَنْ يَتَرَكَّبَ مِنْ مُتَّصِلَتَيْنِ قالَ الحفيد : وشرائط إنتاج هذا القياس الشَّرطي الاقتراني ما سبق من الأشكال الأربعةِ .
(قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ مِنْ مُنْفَصِلَتَيْنِ شرطُ إنتاجه إيجاب المقدمتين، وكلية إحداهما،
وصدقُ منع الخلو عنهما .
(قَوْلُهُ: إِمَّا زَوْجُ الزَّوْجِ كَالعشرين والثمانية، فالعشرين زوج، وهي منقسمة إلى عشرة وعشرة وكلّ مِنَ القسمين زوج.
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : فَشَرْطِيٌّ ) أي : أنَّ القياسَ الشَّرطيّ لا يختصُّ بِمَا تركَّبَ مِنَ الشَّرطيَّاتِ فقط، وهذا اصطلاح لا حجر فيهِ، فلا يضرُّ أَنَّهم خصَّصُوا الحمليَّ بِمَا تركَّبَ مِنَ الحمليات فقط
(قَوْلُهُ : شَرَعَ فِي الشَّرْطِيّ مِنَ الِافْتِرَانِي الأظهرُ والأخصرُ إسقاط قَوْلِهِ : (مِنَ الاقتراني).
(قَوْلُهُ : إِمَّا زَوْجُ الزَّوْجِ أَوْ زَوْجُ الْفَرْدِ لأَنَّهُ إِمَّا أَنْ ينقسم إلى مُتساويَيْنِ أو لا ؛ الثَّاني الفردُ كَالثَّلاثةِ، والأَوَّلُ إِمَّا أَنْ ينقسم إلى المنقسم بمتساويين أو لا ؛ الأَوَّلُ زوجُ الزَّوحِ كَالثَّمانيةِ والثَّاني زوج الفردِ كَالسِّتَّةِ .
Section: On the compound categorical syllogism composed of conditionals
Know that the compound categorical, as previously, divides into: categorical and hypothetical; because if it is composed of purely categoricals, it is categorical; and if it is not composed of them, but rather composed of purely conditionals, or of conditionals and categoricals, it is hypothetical.
Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: composed of conditionals) meaning: the categorical syllogism is composed of categoricals and conditionals, contrary to whoever confined it to categoricals.
Al-Attar: And a section: on the categorical syllogism... etc.
Abd al-Hakim said: Just as categoricals are innate and theoretical, likewise conditionals may be innate, like our saying: Whenever the sun is shining, then daytime exists; and may be theoretical, like our saying: Whenever the contingent exists, the Necessary Existence exists. Thus there is a need to know hypothetical categorical syllogisms, especially in geometry included in Euclid's book. And because Aristotle did not include this chapter in his teaching, some claimed there is no need for it, since knowledge of categorical categoricals suffices without mentioning them. But this is nothing, because of the clear difference between their rulings.
(His saying: composed of conditionals) i.e., alone or with categoricals, as indicated by what follows.
(His saying: and if not composed of them... etc.) is a declaration that by the hypothetical syllogism is not meant the one composed of conditionals; rather, whatever is not composed of categoricals, whether composed of pure conditionals or of conditionals and categoricals. Thus its primary divisions are five.
(His saying: pure) intended by being pure is that no apparent conditional has joined it, as is apparent, not that it is not similar to a conditional.
And when the author finished with the categorical, he began with the hypothetical of the categorical.
(The hypothetical of the categorical) divides into five divisions:
(Either it is composed of two conditionals), which is the first division, like our saying: If the sun is shining, then daytime exists; and whenever daytime exists, the earth is illuminated. It yields: If the sun is shining, then the earth is illuminated.
(Or) of (two disjunctives), which is the second division, like our saying: Every number is either even or odd; and every even is either even of even or even of odd.
Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: either composed of two conditionals) Al-Hafid said: The conditions for production of this hypothetical categorical syllogism are what preceded among the four figures.
(His saying: or from two disjunctives) Condition for its production is affirmation of both premises and universality of one of them, and the truth of the prevention of voidness of both.
(His saying: either even of even like twenty and eight, for twenty is even and it divides into ten and ten, and each of the two parts is even.
Al-Attar: (His saying: then it is hypothetical) meaning: the hypothetical syllogism is not confined to what is composed of conditionals only, and this is a convention with no restriction; so it does no harm that they confined the categorical to what is composed of categoricals only.
(His saying: he began with the hypothetical of the categorical) the more apparent and concise is to drop his saying: (of the categorical).
(His saying: either even of even or even of odd) because it either divides into two equals or not; the second is odd like three; the first either divides into divisible by two equals or not; the first is even of even like eight; the second is even of odd like six.
Source pages p. 843

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory section on the compound hypothetical syllogism. The author begins by stating that the compound categorical syllogism (al-qiyas al-iqtirani al-murakkab) divides into two types: categorical (hamli) and hypothetical (sharti). The categorical is composed purely of categorical propositions, while the hypothetical is composed either purely of conditional propositions or a mix of conditional and categorical. The commentators then clarify that some scholars mistakenly restricted the categorical syllogism to only categorical propositions, but the author includes conditionals. A need for this study is justified by examples from Euclidean geometry and kalam (such as the necessary existent). The hypothetical syllogism is then divided into five primary divisions based on whether its premises are conditionals (muttasila) or disjunctives (munfasila). The first division is when both premises are conditionals, e.g., 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists; and if daytime exists, the earth is illuminated' yields 'If the sun is shining, then the earth is illuminated.' The second division is when both premises are disjunctives, e.g., 'Every number is either even or odd; and every even is either even of even or even of odd.' The conditions for validity of these syllogisms are noted: the first follows the four figures of categorical syllogisms, while the second requires both premises affirmative and one universal, with the condition of preventing voidness (man' al-khuluww).

Key Terms
متصلة
شرطي اقتراني
إنتاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example syllogism 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists; and if daytime exists, the earth is illuminated', what is the conclusion?
A) The earth is illuminated
B) If the sun is shining, then the earth is illuminated
C) If daytime exists, then the sun is shining
D) The sun is shining

Key Takeaway
A compound hypothetical syllogism of two conditionals yields a conditional conclusion, and its validity depends on the same figures as categorical syllogisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid compound hypothetical syllogism with two conditionals on a fiqh topic. For example, concerning the validity of wudu: 'If a person sleeps, their wudu is invalid; and if their wudu is invalid, they must renew it; therefore, if a person sleeps, they must renew their wudu.' Identify the middle term.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
The Second Type: Two Disjunctives (Munfasilatayn)
Author presents the second division: a syllogism composed of two disjunctives, with an example and validity conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for validity of a compound disjunctive syllogism and apply them to an Islamic example.

Core Idea
A compound disjunctive syllogism requires both premises affirmative, one universal, and the condition of preventing voidness.

The second division of the hypothetical syllogism is composed of two disjunctive (munfasila) premises. The author gives an example from number theory: 'Every number is either even or odd; and every even is either even of even or even of odd.' We will examine the conditions for its validity and how this applies to Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premises
Both premises are exclusive disjunctions (man' al-khuluw). The first: every number is either even or odd. The second: every even is either even of even or even of odd.
Exclusivity is key; the disjuncts cannot both be true and cannot both be false.
2
Apply validity conditions
Al-Dasuqi states: both premises must be affirmative, one must be universal, and the condition of prevention of voidness (صدق منع الخلو) must hold.
Prevention of voidness means the disjuncts exhaust all possibilities.
3
Derive the conclusion
The conclusion from these two disjunctive premises would be a disjunction: 'Every number is either even of even, even of odd, or odd.'
This is a classification syllogism.
4
Check validity
Given the conditions, the syllogism is valid because both premises are affirmative and universal, and the disjuncts are exclusive and exhaustive.

فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ الِاقْتِرَانِي الْمُرَكَّبِ مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ

Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: composed of conditionals) meaning: the categorical syllogism is composed of categoricals and conditionals, contrary to whoever confined it to categoricals.

And because Aristotle did not include this chapter in his teaching, some claimed there is no need for it, since knowledge of categorical categoricals suffices without mentioning them. But this is nothing, because of the clear difference between their rulings.
(His saying: composed of conditionals) i.e., alone or with categoricals, as indicated by what follows.
(His saying: and if not composed of them... etc.) is a declaration that by the hypothetical syllogism is not meant the one composed of conditionals; rather, whatever is not composed of categoricals, whether composed of pure conditionals or of conditionals and categoricals. Thus its primary divisions are five.
(His saying: pure) intended by being pure is that no apparent conditional has joined it, as is apparent, not that it is not similar to a conditional.

(The hypothetical of the categorical) divides into five divisions:
(Either it is composed of two conditionals), which is the first division, like our saying: If the sun is shining, then daytime exists; and whenever daytime exists, the earth is illuminated. It yields: If the sun is shining, then the earth is illuminated.
