Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory section on the compound hypothetical syllogism. The author begins by stating that the compound categorical syllogism (al-qiyas al-iqtirani al-murakkab) divides into two types: categorical (hamli) and hypothetical (sharti). The categorical is composed purely of categorical propositions, while the hypothetical is composed either purely of conditional propositions or a mix of conditional and categorical. The commentators then clarify that some scholars mistakenly restricted the categorical syllogism to only categorical propositions, but the author includes conditionals. A need for this study is justified by examples from Euclidean geometry and kalam (such as the necessary existent). The hypothetical syllogism is then divided into five primary divisions based on whether its premises are conditionals (muttasila) or disjunctives (munfasila). The first division is when both premises are conditionals, e.g., 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists; and if daytime exists, the earth is illuminated' yields 'If the sun is shining, then the earth is illuminated.' The second division is when both premises are disjunctives, e.g., 'Every number is either even or odd; and every even is either even of even or even of odd.' The conditions for validity of these syllogisms are noted: the first follows the four figures of categorical syllogisms, while the second requires both premises affirmative and one universal, with the condition of preventing voidness (man' al-khuluww).

Key Takeaway

The Syllogism
intermediate

Four Types of Conditional Conjunctive Syllogism
Author enumerates four (of five) types of conditional conjunctive syllogism with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the four types of conditional conjunctive syllogism based on premise composition and construct valid Islamic examples for each.

Core Idea
Conditional conjunctive syllogisms vary by premise type: conditional, disjunctive, or categorical.

This passage from a classical logic textbook lists the subtypes of the conditional conjunctive syllogism. You will learn to recognize each type by its premise combination and verify the validity of the conclusion. Mastering these types is essential for structuring compound arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify premise types in each example
Read each example and determine whether each premise is a conditional (muttasila: 'if...then'), a disjunctive (munfasila: 'either...or'), or a categorical (hamliyya: 'every...is...').
The first example has two conditionals; the second has two disjunctives; the third has one categorical and one conditional; the fourth has one categorical and one disjunctive.
2
Count and order premises
Each type uses exactly two premises. Note the order in which they appear—the first premise introduces a relation, and the second links to the conclusion.
The first premise often contains the 'middle term' that connects to the second premise.
3
Match the combination to the type number
The author lists four types: (1) two conditionals, (2) two disjunctives, (3) categorical + conditional, (4) categorical + disjunctive. Classify each example accordingly.
A fifth type (conditional + disjunctive) is hinted but not elaborated.
4
Verify the conclusion follows
Check that the conclusion is a valid logical consequence of the premises. For type 1, the conclusion is a conditional linking the antecedent of the first premise to the consequent of the second. For other types, ensure the conclusion respects the disjunctions or categorical relations.
All examples given are valid syllogisms.

الشَّرْطِيُّ مِنَ الِاقْتِرَانِي ينقسم إلى خمسة أقسام؛ لأنَّه : إِمَّا أَنْ يَتَرَكَّبَ مِنْ مُتَّصِلَتَيْن، وهو القسم الأوَّلُ، كَقولِنَا : «إن كانَتِ الشَّمسُ طالعةٌ فالنَّهارُ موجودٌ ، وكلَّما كان النَّهارُ موجوداً فالأرضُ مضيئةٌ»، ينتج : إن كانتِ الشَّمس طالعة، فالأرضُ مضيئة. أَوْ مِنْ مُنْفَصِلَتَيْن، وهو القسمُ الثَّاني، كَقولِنَا : كلُّ عددٍ إِمَّا زوج أو فرد، وكلُّ زوج إمَّا زوج الزوج، أو زوج الفردِ، فكلُّ عددٍ إمَّا فرد، أو زوج الزوج، أو زوج الفرد. أَوْ مِنْ حَمْلِيَّةٍ وَمُتَّصِلَةٍ، وهو الثَّالث، كقولنا : كلَّما كان هذا الشَّيء إنساناً فهو حيوان، وكلُّ حيوان جسم، ينتج : كلَّما كان هذا الشَّيءُ إنساناً فهو جسم. أَوْ مِنْ حَمْلِيَّةٍ وَمُنْفَصِلَةٍ، وهو الرَّابع، كَقولنا : كلُّ عددٍ إِمَّا زوج أو فرد، وكلُّ زوج إمَّا زوج الزوج أو زوج الفرد.
The conditional from the conjunctive [syllogism] is divided into five divisions: either it is composed of two conditionals, which is the first division, like our saying: 'If the sun is rising then daytime exists, and whenever daytime exists the earth is illuminated', producing: 'If the sun is rising, the earth is illuminated'. Or of two disjunctives, which is the second division, like our saying: 'Every number is either even or odd, and every even is either even-even or even-odd, so every number is either odd or even-even or even-odd'. Or of a categorical and a conditional, which is the third, like our saying: 'Whenever this thing is a human it is an animal, and every animal is a body', producing: 'Whenever this thing is a human it is a body'. Or of a categorical and a disjunctive, which is the fourth, like our saying: 'Every number is either even or odd, and every even is either even-even or even-odd'.
Source pages p. 844

Commentary
This passage classifies the conditional conjunctive syllogism (al-qiyas al-iqtirani al-sharti) into four (apparently five) types based on the composition of its premises. The author begins by stating the general division, then provides examples for each type. The first type consists of two conditional premises (mutasilatayn) — both are conditional statements of the form 'if...then'. The example given is: 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists; and whenever daytime exists, the earth is illuminated.' The conclusion is a new conditional: 'If the sun is rising, the earth is illuminated.' This is analogous to a categorical syllogism in the first figure, where the middle term is the consequence (tali) of the first premise and the antecedent (muqaddam) of the second. The second type consists of two disjunctive premises (munfasilatayn). The example uses number classification: 'Every number is either even or odd; every even is either even-even (divisible by 4) or even-odd (even but not divisible by 4).' The conclusion lists three possibilities: odd, even-even, or even-odd. This type requires that the disjunctions be exhaustive and mutually exclusive. The third type combines one categorical premise (hamliyya) and one conditional premise (muttasila). The example: 'Whenever this thing is a human it is an animal; every animal is a body.' The conclusion is a conditional: 'Whenever this thing is a human it is a body.' Here the categorical premise functions as a universal affirmative, and the conditional premise links the subject to a predicate. The fourth type combines a categorical and a disjunctive premise. The example mirrors the second type but with one premise categorical: 'Every number is either even or odd; every even is either even-even or even-odd.' The conclusion is implicit as a disjunction. The passage notes that the fifth type is mentioned but not elaborated; it may be a combination of two conditionals of different types or a different permutation. The classification is foundational for understanding compound syllogistic forms in Islamic logic.

Key Terms

مُتَّصِلَة

Checkpoint
Which type of conditional conjunctive syllogism uses two conditional premises?
A) The first type (mutasilatayn)
B) The second type (munfasilatayn)
C) The third type (hamliyya wa muttasila)
D) The fourth type (hamliyya wa munfasila)

Key Takeaway
The four types of conditional conjunctive syllogism are defined by the combination of premise forms: conditional-conditional, disjunctive-disjunctive, categorical-conditional, and categorical-disjunctive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a type 2 conditional conjunctive syllogism using Islamic rulings on types of water (e.g., pure water, impure water). Ensure the disjunctions are exhaustive and mutually exclusive.
?
Take the valid categorical syllogism 'All intoxicants are forbidden; khamr is an intoxicant; therefore khamr is forbidden.' Convert it into a type 3 syllogism (categorical + conditional) by restating the major premise as a conditional.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Mixed Syllogisms: Categorical with Conditional (Types 3 & 4)
Author enumerates two types of mixed syllogisms with examples and commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and validate compound syllogisms that combine a categorical premise with a conjunctive or disjunctive conditional premise.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms require careful placement of the categorical premise and the part of the conditional it shares.

In this lesson, we explore two types of mixed syllogisms: those formed from a categorical and a conjunctive conditional (Type 3), and those from a categorical and a disjunctive conditional (Type 4). The passage provides examples and explains the subdivisions based on whether the categorical is minor or major and whether it shares with the antecedent or consequent. Understanding these subtypes is essential for correctly applying mixed syllogisms in Islamic theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
For Type 3, note the categorical premise and the conditional premise. For Type 4, the disjunctive conditional. In the examples: Type 3 uses 'Whenever this is a human it is an animal' (conditional) and 'Every animal is a body' (categorical). Type 4 uses 'Every number is either even or odd' (disjunctive) and 'Every even number is divisible by two' (categorical).
The conditional premise in Type 3 is conjunctive (muttasila); in Type 4 it is disjunctive (munfasila).
2
Determine the categorical's position
Is the categorical premise the minor or the major? In the Type 3 example, 'Every animal is a body' is the major premise. In Type 4, the categorical 'Every even number is divisible by two' is also major. The passage notes that the natural form is categorical as major.
If the categorical is minor, different subdivisions apply.
3
Identify the shared part
In Type 3, the categorical shares with the consequent of the conditional: 'animal' appears as the predicate of the conditional and the subject of the categorical. This is the natural form. Other possibilities include sharing with the antecedent.
Sharing can be with a complete part (antecedent or consequent) or an incomplete part.
4
Construct the conclusion
Eliminate the shared term (middle) and relate the remaining extremes. For Type 3: remove 'animal', combine 'human' and 'body' using the conditional form: 'Whenever this is a human, it is a body'. For Type 4: the disjunction splits into cases; the conclusion is a disjunction of the remaining possibilities.
The conclusion type mirrors the conditional premise: Type 3 yields a conditional; Type 4 yields a disjunction.
5
Validate the inference
Check if the conclusion follows necessarily using the rules given. The passage states the natural form (categorical major, sharing with consequent) is productive. For Type 4, the conclusion 'Every number is either odd or divisible by two' holds because the true part of the disjunction leads to further division.
The commentary explains the mechanism: the truth of one disjunct entails further subdivision.

Or from a categorical and a conditional: this is the third, as our saying: 'Whenever this thing is a human, it is an animal, and every animal is a body' yields: 'Whenever this thing is a human, it is a body.' Or from a categorical and a disjunctive: this is the fourth, as our saying: 'Every number is either even or' (his saying: 'or odd of even like ten, for it is even, and it is divided into five and five, and each of the two parts is odd). (His saying: 'or from a categorical and a conditional') and it has four divisions; because the categorical is either minor or major, and in each, the sharing is either with respect to the antecedent or the consequent, and the natural one is when the categorical is major and the condition is in the consequent. (His saying: 'So every number... etc.') because the true of the first disjunctive, which is the minor, is either evenness or oddness; if the true is oddness, it is one of the divisions of the conclusion; if it is evenness, it is confined to two divisions: even of even and even of odd, so the true is one of its two mentioned divisions in the conclusion as well, and the conclusion composed of the three divisions is necessarily true. Know that each of these two divisions is divided into three divisions; because the sharing between the conditional and the two disjunctives is either in a complete part of them, i.e., the antecedent or the consequent, or in an incomplete part of them, or in a complete part of one and incomplete of the other, and the natural of the first is the first, and of the second is the second, and he gave their examples in the commentary. (His saying: 'yields: Whenever it was... etc.') because what is true of everything that the necessary (which is animal) is true of, the necessary (which is human) is true of it, and this division has four divisions; because the categorical in it is either minor or major, and whichever it is, the sharing with it is either with the consequent of the conditional or its antecedent; except that the natural of them is when the categorical is major and the sharing is with the consequent of the conditional, as its example passed. (Or from a conditional and a disjunctive) and it is the fifth, as our saying: 'Whenever this thing is a human, it is an animal, and every animal is either white or black' yields: 'Whenever this is a human, it is either white or black.' al-Dasuqi: And in the second: the difference of its two premises in quality, and the universality of the major, etc., and likewise the number of its moods except the fourth; for its moods here are five; because the production of the last three is according to the composition of the negative, and that is not considered in conditionals. End from al-Qutb on al-Shamsiyya. (His saying: 'according to what was mentioned') is attached to 'is composed'. (His saying: 'in detail') is a circumstantial from the pronoun of 'was mentioned'. al-‘Attar: (His saying: 'yields: every number') i.e., yields after removing the middle which is 'even' due to its being repeated between the two premises: 'Every number is either odd or equally divisible'; because the equal, which is 'equally divisible' here, is an opposite of one of the two contraries, which is 'even', and it is contrary to the other contrary, which is 'odd', so from the existence of the equal to one of the two contraries follows the non-existence of the other contrary, and vice versa. And this division has three divisions; because the categoricals are either equal to the number of parts of the disjunctive, or less, or more. (His saying: 'yields: Whenever it was...') because the division of everything that the necessary (which is animal) is true of necessitates the division of the necessary (which is human). Commentary
This passage is a classification of mixed syllogisms that combine categorical propositions (ḥamliyya) with conditional propositions (sharṭiyya) — both conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfaṣila). The author enumerates three types: third, fourth, and fifth. For each type, he provides an example and then the commentators (al-Dasuqi and al-‘Attar) expand on the subdivisions and conditions for validity. The third type (categorical + conjunctive conditional) has four subdivisions based on whether the categorical is minor or major and whether sharing is with the antecedent or consequent; the natural form is categorical as major and sharing with the consequent. The fourth type (categorical + disjunctive) is explained through an example about numbers: the disjunctive minor premise states that every number is either even or odd (with further division of even into even of even and even of odd). The conclusion combines these three possibilities. The commentator notes that each division of the even further subdivides. The fifth type (conjunctive conditional + disjunctive) yields a disjunctive conclusion: if something is human it is an animal, and every animal is either white or black, so the conclusion is that if it is human it is either white or black. Al-Dasuqi adds a note about the moods of this type, mentioning that only five moods are productive in this section due to the nature of conditionals. Al-‘Attar clarifies the logical mechanism: the middle term (e.g., 'even') is eliminated, and the conclusion relates the extremes. The passage demonstrates the rigorous classification of compound syllogisms in classical Islamic logic, ensuring that all valid combinations are identified and exemplified.

Key Takeaway
Mixed syllogisms of categorical and conditional premises are classified into subtypes based on the position of the categorical and the shared part; the natural form yields a valid conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Verify the placement of categorical as major and sharing with the consequent.

Mixed Syllogisms: Conditional with Disjunctive (Type 5)
Author introduces the fifth type of mixed syllogism (conditional + disjunctive) with example and commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and validate a Type 5 mixed syllogism that combines a conjunctive conditional with a disjunctive conditional.

Core Idea
A conditional premise can combine with a disjunctive to yield a disjunctive conclusion when they share a term.

The fifth type of mixed syllogism combines a conjunctive conditional (muttasila) with a disjunctive conditional (munfasila). The passage provides a classic example: 'Whenever this thing is a human, it is an animal; every animal is either white or black; therefore whenever this is a human, it is either white or black.' Commentators al-Dasuqi and al-‘Attar elaborate on the moods and the elimination of the middle term. This type is particularly useful in arguments that connect a conditional relationship to a classification into alternatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Note the two premises: one is a conjunctive conditional (if A then B), the other is a disjunctive conditional (every B is either C or D). In the example: 'If human then animal' and 'Every animal is either white or black'.
Both premises are conditional, but the second is a universal disjunctive categorical? Actually 'every animal is either white or black' is a disjunctive proposition with a subject and predicate, but it is treated as a conditional? In classical logic, it's a universal disjunctive categorical. The text calls it 'munfasila' (disjunctive), so it's a disjunctive proposition.
2
Identify the shared term
The shared term (middle) is 'animal'. It appears as the consequent of the conditional premise and the subject of the disjunctive premise.
Al-‘Attar explains that the middle term (e.g., 'even' in the earlier example) is eliminated.
3
Eliminate the middle term
Remove 'animal' from both premises. The remaining terms are 'human' (from the conditional's antecedent) and 'white or black' (from the disjunctive's predicates).
The conclusion combines them using the conditional form of the first premise: 'if human then (white or black)'.
4
Formulate the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional with a disjunctive consequent: 'Whenever this is a human, it is either white or black'.
This is the direct result of the example; other arrangements may yield different conclusions.
5
Understand the subdivisions
Al-Dasuqi notes that this type has six subdivisions based on how the sharing occurs: with complete parts (antecedent/consequent) or incomplete parts, etc. The natural form is sharing with the consequent of the conditional.
The passage states the division has six divisions, but the details are in further commentary.

(His saying: 'or from a categorical and a conditional') and it has four divisions; because the categorical is either minor or major, and in each, the sharing is either with respect to the antecedent or the consequent, and the natural one is when the categorical is major and the condition is in the consequent. Commentary
This passage is a classification of mixed syllogisms that combine categorical propositions (ḥamliyya) with conditional propositions (sharṭiyya) — both conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfaṣila). The author enumerates three types: third, fourth, and fifth. For each type, he provides an example and then the commentators (al-Dasuqi and al-‘Attar) expand on the subdivisions and conditions for validity. The third type (categorical + conjunctive conditional) has four subdivisions based on whether the categorical is minor or major and whether sharing is with the antecedent or consequent; the natural form is categorical as major and sharing with the consequent. The fourth type (categorical + disjunctive) is explained through an example about numbers: the disjunctive minor premise states that every number is either even or odd (with further division of even into even of even and even of odd). The conclusion combines these three possibilities. The commentator notes that each division of the even further subdivides. The fifth type (conjunctive conditional + disjunctive) yields a disjunctive conclusion: if something is human it is an animal, and every animal is either white or black, so the conclusion is that if it is human it is either white or black. Al-Dasuqi adds a note about the moods of this type, mentioning that only five moods are productive in this section due to the nature of conditionals. Al-‘Attar clarifies the logical mechanism: the middle term (e.g., 'even') is eliminated, and the conclusion relates the extremes. The passage demonstrates the rigorous classification of compound syllogisms in classical Islamic logic, ensuring that all valid combinations are identified and exemplified.

The Syllogism
intermediate
6 min
Mixed Syllogisms: Combining a Conditional and a Disjunctive Premise
Explains fifth type of mixed syllogism with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct a mixed syllogism that combines a conditional premise (muttasila) and a disjunctive premise (munfasila), and determine its conclusion by eliminating the middle term.

Core Idea
A conditional and a disjunctive premise together yield a conditional conclusion with a disjunctive consequent.

This lesson presents the fifth type of mixed syllogism in Islamic logic, where one premise is conditional (e.g., 'Whenever it is human, it is animal') and the other is disjunctive (e.g., 'Every animal is either white or black'). By eliminating the middle term (the predicate common to both), we derive a new conditional that combines the antecedent of the first with the disjunction from the second. This structure appears in legal reasoning where a general conditional rule meets a classification of outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Find the conditional premise (muttasila) and the disjunctive premise (munfasila). In the example: 'Whenever this thing is a human, it is an animal' (conditional) and 'Every animal is either white or black' (disjunctive).
2
Locate the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat)
The middle term is the predicate that connects the two premises. Here it is 'animal' – it appears as the consequent in the conditional and as the subject in the disjunctive. In the number example, the middle term is 'even' (divisible into two equals).
3
Eliminate the middle term
Since the middle term bridges the premises, you remove it to form a direct link between the antecedent of the conditional and the disjunction of the disjunctive. The conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the first premise and whose consequent is the whole disjunctive proposition (with the middle term removed). So: 'If this is a human, then it is either white or black.'
The middle term is not a term in the conclusion; it is abstracted away.
4
Classify the syllogism
The text mentions three divisions based on the relation of the categoricals to the parts of the disjunctive: either equal, less, or more. This mixed syllogism can be further classified into six types when considering the sharing between antecedent and consequent (as indicated in the cut-off part).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قَوْلُهُ : ينتج : كُلُّ عَدَدٍ أي : ينتج بعد حذفِ الأوسط منهُ وَهُوَ الزَّوجُ لِكَونِهِ المكرَّرَ بينَ المقدَّمتين : كُلُّ عددٍ إِمَّا فردٌ وَإِمَّا مُنقسم بِمتساويَيْنِ؛ لأنَّ المساوي وَهُوَ المنقسمُ بِمتساويين هنا لأحد المعاندَيْنِ، وهوَ الزَّوج؛ مُعاندٌ لِلمعاندِ الآخَرِ وَهُوَ الفرد، فيلزم من وجودِ المساوي لأحدِ المعاندين عدم وجودِ المعاند الآخَرِ، وبالعكس، وهذا القسم ثلاثة أقسام؛ لأنَّ الحمليَّاتِ إما بعدد أجزاء المنفصلة أو أقلّ أوْ أكثر . (قَوْلُهُ : يَنْتُجُ : كُلَّمَا كَانَ لأَنَّ انقسامَ كُلِّ مَا يصدق عليه اللازم وهو الحيوان؛ يستلزم انقسام الملزومِ وَهُوَ الإنسانُ، وهذا القسم ستَّة أقسام؛ لأنَّ المشاركة بين
['Every time (or) from (connected and disconnected) and it is the fifth, as we say: 'Whenever this thing is a human, it is an animal, and every animal is either white or black,' it produces: 'If this is a human, then it is either white or black.' Source pages p. 846

Commentary
This passage explains the fifth type of mixed syllogism, which combines a conditional premise (muttasila) and a disjunctive premise (munfasila). The author gives an example: if we have 'Whenever this thing is a human, it is an animal' (conditional) and 'Every animal is either white or black' (disjunctive), then the conclusion is 'If this is a human, then it is either white or black.' The commentator (al-'Attar) clarifies how the middle term (the repeated term) is eliminated. In the first example, the middle term is 'animal', and the result is a disjunctive conditional. He then explains another example involving numbers: 'Every number is either odd or divisible into two equals' is a disjunctive produced from categoricals. The key point is that the disjunctive conclusion follows because the two contraries (odd and divisible into two equals) are opposite, so if one exists the other does not. The commentator divides these mixed syllogisms into three types based on the relation of the categoricals to the parts of the disjunctive. Then he mentions a second example involving 'whenever' and says that division has six types because of the sharing between the antecedent and consequent. The passage demonstrates how mixed syllogisms are reducible and classified.

Key Terms
متصلة (muttasila): A conditional proposition (qadiyya shartiyya muttasila) expressing a connection between antecedent and consequent, e.g., 'If A then B'. [Note: In later logic, muttasila sometimes refers specifically to the material conditional, but here it is the standard conditional.]
منفصلة (munfasila): A disjunctive proposition expressing mutual exclusion between two or more parts, e.g., 'Either A or B'. [Note: No significant shift; stable across the tradition.]
حد أوسط (hadd awsat): The middle term that is repeated in both premises and eliminated in the conclusion, here identified as 'animal' in the first example and 'even' in the second. [Note: In categorical syllogisms, the middle term is the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion; same definition applies here.]
معاند (mu'and): A contrary; each of the two disjuncts in a disjunctive proposition, such that they cannot both be true or both false simultaneously (here: odd and even). [Note: In disjunctive logic, mu'and is the standard term for a disjunct in a mutually exclusive disjunction.]
حملية (hamliyya): A categorical proposition with a subject and predicate, e.g., 'Every human is an animal'. [Note: No significant shift; standard term.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Every human is either white or black.
B) If this is a human then it is either white or black.
C) Every human is an animal.
D) Some animals are white.

Key Takeaway
A mixed syllogism with a conditional and a disjunctive premise yields a conditional conclusion by eliminating the common middle term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Conditional Syllogism Figures and Their Parallel to Categoricals
States that conditional syllogisms also have four figures like categoricals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure of a conditional syllogism using the position of the middle term and state the condition for the first figure.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms share the same four figures and inferential conditions as categoricals.

This passage introduces purely conditional syllogisms, which are composed of conditional premises. The author emphasizes that, just like categorical syllogisms, conditional syllogisms also have four figures. The detailed rules are omitted here because they are lengthy, but the commentator explains how the figures are determined by the position of the middle term. Understanding this structural parallel is essential for evaluating the validity of arguments that use conditional propositions, which are common in Islamic theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
In a conditional syllogism, the middle term is a shared part (e.g., the antecedent of one conditional and the consequent of another) that connects the two premises.
2
Determine the position of the middle term
The figure depends on whether the middle term is the subject or predicate in each premise. The description given is: if the middle term is the predicate (محكوم عليه) in the major and subject in the minor, it is the first figure; if subject in major and predicate in minor, it is the second; if subject in both, it is the third; if predicate in both, it is the fourth.
The passage gives a specific mapping: predicate in major → third figure? Actually it says: if the middle term is as predicate in the major and subject in the minor, that is the third figure? Wait, the text says: 'For it is either the predicate in the major or vice versa. The first is the third figure, the second is the second, the third is the first, and the fourth is the fourth.' This is a mapping of the four possibilities. We need to clarify: (1) middle term subject in minor, predicate in major → third figure? Actually standard logic: first figure: middle term subject in major, predicate in minor? Standard is: first figure: middle term is subject in major, predicate in minor; second: predicate in both; third: subject in both; fourth: predicate in major, subject in minor. The text says: the case where the middle term is predicate (محكوم عليه) in major (i.e., subject in major? Actually محكوم عليه means the thing about which something is predicated, i.e., subject. So 'predicate in major' likely means it is the subject of the major premise? This is confusing. The guided_path step should be careful; we can simplify: determine which premise the middle term appears in and whether it is the subject or predicate. The passage then assigns the figure based on a schema. For our pedagogical purpose, we can say that the figure is determined by the pattern of the middle term's position, exactly as in categoricals.
3
Apply the condition for the first figure
The commentator explicitly states that for the first figure, the minor premise must be affirmative and the major premise universal. This is identical to the condition for categorical syllogisms.
Other figures have their own conditions, which can be found in longer works.
4
Recognize the identity of figures across types
The passage emphasizes that conditional syllogisms have the same four figures as categoricals, and the conditions for productivity are the same. This means you do not need to learn a separate system for conditionals.
This structural parallel allows you to transfer your knowledge of categorical syllogisms directly to conditional ones.

فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ الِافْتِرَانِي الْمُرَكَّبِ مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ (وَ) كما أنَّ الحملي تنعقد فيه الأشكال الأربعةُ على ما ذُكِرَ مُفصَّلاً . كذلك الشَّرطي (تَنْعَقِدُ فِيْهِ الْأَشْكَالُ الأَرْبَعَةُ، وَفِي تَفْصِيْلِهَا طُوْلٌ) لا يليقُ بهذا المختصر ؛ لأنَّهُ شأن المطوّلاتِ ، فاطلبه ثمَّةَ . (قَوْلُهُ : كَذَلِكَ الشَّرْطِيُّ ... إلخ) وشرائط إنتاج هذه الأشكال كما في الحمليات من غيرِ فرقٍ حتَّى يُشترط في الأوَّل : إيجابُ الصُّغرى وَكُلِّيَّةُ الكُبرى. (قَوْلُهُ : كَذَلِكَ تَنْعَقِدُ فِيهِ الْأَشْكَالُ الْأَرْبَعَةُ أَيْ : لأَنَّهُ لا بُدَّ فيه من اشتراكِ المقدمتين في جزء يكون هو الحد الأوسط، فإنَّه إما أن يكون محكوماً عليه في الكبرى أو بالعكس، فالأوَّلُ : هو الشَّكلُ الثَّالث، والثاني : هو الثَّاني، والثَّالثُ هو الأوَّلُ، والرابع هو الرَّابع. (قَوْلُهُ : فَاطْلُبْهُ ثَمَّةَ) أي: في المطوَّلاتِ . (قَوْلُهُ : وَتَنْعَقِدُ فِيهِ الْأَشْكَالُ الْأَرْبَعَةُ راجع لجميع الأقسامِ.
Chapter: On the Categorical Syllogism Composed of Conditionals. (And) Just as the categorical syllogism has the four figures as mentioned in detail, likewise the conditional syllogism has the four figures, and in their detail there is length, not suitable for this abridgment, because it is the concern of lengthy works, so seek it there. (His saying: 'Likewise the conditional... etc.') The conditions for the production of these figures are as in categoricals without any difference, such that in the first figure the minor must be affirmative and the major universal. (His saying: 'Likewise the four figures arise in it') i.e., because in it there must be sharing of the two premises in a part that is the middle term, for it is either the predicate in the major or vice versa. The first is the third figure, the second is the second, the third is the first, and the fourth is the fourth. (His saying: 'So seek it there') i.e., in the lengthy works. (His saying: 'And the four figures arise in it' refers to all divisions.)
Source pages p. 847

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on purely conditional syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani murakkab min al-shartiyyat). The matn states that just as categorical syllogisms have four figures, so do conditional syllogisms, but the full detail is omitted because it is too long for this abridgment. The commentator (al-Dasuqi) explains that the conditions for valid inference are the same as in categoricals, e.g., in the first figure the minor must be affirmative and the major universal. He also shows how the three terms function: the predicate of the major becomes the middle term, etc., leading to the identification of the figures. Al-'Attar adds that the four figures apply to all divisions of conditionals. The key point is that conditional syllogisms are structurally analogous to categoricals and do not require a separate set of rules.

Definition of Conditional Syllogism and Its Conjunctive Type
Definition and division of qiyas istithna'i with elaboration on muttasil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of conditional syllogism and describe the structure of the conjunctive type.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogism uses a conditional major and a categorical minor.

In this passage, the author defines conditional syllogism and divides it into two types: conjunctive and disjunctive. The focus here is on the conjunctive type, which combines a conjunctive conditional premise with the affirmation of the antecedent or negation of the consequent. This structure is the foundation for constructing valid conditional arguments in Islamic logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate 'al-qiyas al-istithna'i' in the Arabic. This is the definiendum: the conditional syllogism.
2
Recognize the two divisions
The passage states it is divided into conjunctive (ittisali) and disjunctive (infisali). Note both.
3
Extract the composition of the conjunctive type
The conjunctive type is composed of a conjunctive conditional (shartiyya muttasila) as the major premise, and the affirmation of the antecedent (wad' al-muqaddam) as the minor premise. Al-Dasuqi clarifies that the minor premise is called the exceptive premise (istithna'iyya).
Alternatively, the minor can be the negation of the consequent (raf' al-tali).
4
Understand the role of the minor premise
The minor premise is a categorical proposition that either affirms the antecedent or negates the consequent. It is not a conditional itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ الِاسْتِثْنَائِي وهُوَ قِسْمَانِ: اتِّصَالِيٌّ وَانْفِصَالِيٌّ. فَالِاتِّصَالِيُّ: هُوَ مَا يَتَرَكَّبُ مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ الْمُتَّصِلَةِ وَوَضْعِ الْمُقَدَّمِ؛ أَيْ: إِثْبَاتِهِ. الدَّسُوقِيُّ (قَوْلُهُ: مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ الْمُتَّصِلَةِ) هِيَ: الْكُبْرَى. (قَوْلُهُ: وَوَضْعِ الْمُقَدَّمِ) بِالْجَرِّ عَطْفٌ عَلَى (الشَّرْطِيَّةِ الْمُتَّصِلَةِ). وَقَوْلُهُ: (وَضْعُ الْمُقَدَّمِ) هُوَ الاسْتِثْنَائِيَّةُ الْمُسَمَّاةُ عِنْدَهُمْ بِالصُّغْرَى، وَكَذَا يُقَالُ فِي قَوْلِهِ: (رَفْعِ التَّالِي)، فَالْمُرَادُ: ذَاتُ وَضْعٍ وَذَاتُ رَفْعٍ؛ أَيِ الْقَضِيَّةُ الْمُشْتَمِلَةُ عَلَى ذَلِكَ.
Chapter: On the conditional syllogism. It is two divisions: conjunctive and disjunctive. As for the conjunctive: it is what is composed of the conjunctive conditional and the affirmation of the antecedent; i.e., establishing it. Al-Dasuqi (his statement: from the conjunctive conditional) it is the major premise. (His statement: and the affirmation of the antecedent) in the genitive case is an apposition to (the conjunctive conditional). And his statement: (affirmation of the antecedent) is the exceptive [premise] called by them the minor premise. Similarly is said in his statement: (negation of the consequent). So the intended [meaning] is: a proposition containing affirmation and a proposition containing negation; i.e., the proposition that includes that.
Source pages p. 848

Commentary
This passage defines conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) and divides it into two types: conjunctive (muttasil) and disjunctive (munfasil). It then specifically explains the conjunctive type. The conjunctive conditional syllogism is composed of a conjunctive conditional premise (shartiyya muttasila) and the affirmation (wad') of the antecedent (muqaddam). Al-Dasuqi clarifies that the conjunctive conditional premise serves as the major premise (kubra), while the affirmation of the antecedent is the exceptive premise (istithna'iyya) which they call the minor premise (sughra). Similarly, in the disjunctive type, one may negate (raf') the consequent (tali) instead. Thus, the two premises consist of a conditional major and a categorical minor affirming the antecedent or negating the consequent. This is foundational for understanding how conditional syllogisms produce conclusions.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Three Conditions for a Valid Conditional Syllogism
Al-'Attar enumerates three necessary conditions for a productive conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a conditional syllogism satisfies the three necessary conditions for a valid conclusion: affirmative, necessary connection, and universality.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism yields a conclusion only if the conditional is affirmative, logically necessary, and universal in scope.

In the previous lesson, you learned the basic structure of a conditional syllogism (istithna'i). Now we turn to the conditions that make it productive. Al-'Attar, commenting on al-Razi, lists three requirements: the conditional must be affirmative (not negative), it must be implicative (luzumiyya) or contradictory ('inadiyya) rather than coincidental (ittifaqiyya), and either the conditional or the exceptive premise must be universal. These conditions ensure that the conclusion follows necessarily.

Guided Path
1
Check the affirmation of the conditional
Examine whether the conditional premise is affirmative (mujaba) or negative (saliba). If it is negative, the syllogism is 'barren' because no connection exists between the parts, so no inference is possible.
A negative conditional denies connection, so affirming or negating one part gives no information about the other.
2
Verify the type of conditional connection
For a conjunctive conditional (mutassila), determine if it is implicative (luzumiyya) – the antecedent necessarily implies the consequent. For a disjunctive conditional (munfasila), check if it is contradictory ('inadiyya) – the parts cannot be true together or cannot be false together. Avoid accidentally coincidental (ittifaqiyya) conditionals, as they lead to circular reasoning.
Coincidental conditionals lack a necessary link; using them would require knowing the truth of the parts beforehand.
3
Ensure universality of one premise
Check that either the conditional premise or the exceptive premise is universal (kulliyya). This ensures the conclusion applies to all cases, not just a particular instance.
Universality guarantees the inference is general and not limited to an accidental example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْعَطَّارُ فَصْلٌ: فِي الْقِيَاسِ الِاسْتِثْنَائِي أَيْ: فِي بَيَانِهِ وَيُشْتَرَطُ فِي إِنْتَاجِهِ أُمُورٌ؛ الْأَوَّلُ: أَنْ تَكُونَ الشَّرْطِيَّةُ مُوجَبَةً؛ إِذِ السَّالِبَةُ عَقِيمٌ؛ لِأَنَّهُ إِذْ لَمْ يَكُنْ بَيْنَ أَمْرَيْنِ اتِّصَالٌ أَوِ انْفِصَالٌ؛ لَمْ يَلْزَمْ مِنْ وُجُودِ أَحَدِهِمَا أَوْ نَقِيضِهِ وُجُودُ الْآخَرِ أَوْ عَدَمُهُ. الثَّانِي: أَنْ تَكُونَ الشَّرْطِيَّةُ لُزُومِيَّةً إِنْ كَانَتْ مُتَّصِلَةً، أَوْ عَنَادِيَّةً إِنْ كَانَتْ مُنْفَصِلَةً؛ لِأَنَّ الْعِلْمَ بِصِدْقِ الِاتِّفَاقِيَّةِ مَوْقُوفٌ عَلَى الْعِلْمِ بِصِدْقِ أَحَدِ طَرَفَيْهَا أَوْ كَذِبِهِ، فَلَوِ اسْتُفِيدَ الْعِلْمُ بِصِدْقِ أَحَدِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ أَوْ كَذِبِهِ مِنَ الِاتِّفَاقِيَّةِ؛ لَزِمَ الدَّوْرُ، قَالَهُ الْقُطْبُ الرَّازِيُّ. وَفِي شَرْحِ الْمُصَنِّفِ أَنَّهُ فِي غَايَةِ الْفَسَادِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ جَعَلَ كُلًّا مِنَ الْمَوْقُوفِ وَالْمَوْقُوفِ عَلَيْهِ الْعِلْمَ بِصِدْقِ أَحَدِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ أَوْ بِكَذِبِهِ، وَجَازَ أَنْ يَكُونَ الطَّرَفُ الْمَوْقُوفُ غَيْرَ الطَّرَفِ الْمَوْقُوفِ عَلَيْهِ، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ الدَّوْرُ. الثَّالِثُ: أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ كُلِّيَّةُ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ أَوْ كُلِّيَّةُ الِاسْتِثْنَائِيَّةِ، كَذَا وَقَعَ فِي عِبَارَةِ الرَّازِيِّ.
Al-'Attar: Chapter on the conditional syllogism, i.e., concerning its explanation. And conditions are required for its productivity: First, that the conditional be affirmative; because the negative is barren; for when there is no connection or separation between two things, it does not follow from the existence of one of them or its contradictory the existence or non-existence of the other. Second, that the conditional be implicative if it is conjunctive, or contradictory if it is disjunctive; because knowledge of the truth of an accidentally coincidental conditional depends on knowledge of the truth of one of its two parts or its falsehood, so if knowledge of the truth of one of the two parts or its falsehood were derived from the accidentally coincidental conditional, circularity would follow, said al-Qutb al-Razi. And in the author's commentary [it is stated] that this is extremely invalid; because he made both the dependent and the depended-upon the knowledge of the truth of one of the two parts or its falsehood, and it is possible that the dependent part is not the depended-upon part, so circularity does not follow. Third, that one of the two [premises] be the universality of the conditional or the universality of the exceptive [premise]. Thus it occurs in the statement of al-Razi.
Source pages p. 849

Commentary
This passage, from al-'Attar's commentary, lists three necessary conditions for a conditional syllogism to produce a valid conclusion (intaj). The first condition: the conditional premise must be affirmative (mujaba). A negative conditional is 'barren' because if there is no connection (ittisal) or separation (infisal) between two things, then affirming or negating one part gives no necessary result about the other. The second condition: the conditional must be implicative (luzumiyya) if it is conjunctive, or contradictory ('inadiyya) if it is disjunctive. An accidentally coincidental conditional (ittifaqiyya) does not guarantee a logical connection; using it would lead to circularity (dawr) because knowledge of the truth of the parts would be required before knowing the conditional itself. Al-'Attar then recounts an objection from al-Qutb al-Razi, but notes that the author (the commentator of the original text) dismisses it as utterly invalid, arguing that the circularity does not occur if the dependent part is different from the depended-upon part. The third condition: either the conditional premise or the exceptive premise must be universal (kulliyya). This ensures that the inference applies to all cases, as stated by al-Razi. These conditions refine the understanding of the conditional syllogism, emphasizing the need for genuine logical connection and generality.

Key Terms
موجبة
لزومية
عنادية
اتفاقية
دور
كلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A conditional syllogism is productive only when the conditional is affirmative, possesses a necessary connection (implicative or contradictory), and is universal in at least one premise.

The Syllogism

Connective Conditional Syllogism: Definition, Conditions, and Valid Moods
Author defines the connective conditional syllogism and its valid moods with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two valid moods of a connective conditional syllogism and verify whether a given conditional syllogism satisfies the conditions for validity.

Core Idea
The connective conditional syllogism has only two valid moods: affirming the antecedent and denying the consequent.

In Islamic logic, the exceptional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) is crucial for deriving conclusions from conditional statements. This lesson focuses on the connective type, explaining its structure, two productive moods, and the conditions that guarantee a sound inference. Understanding these principles is essential for evaluating arguments in Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure
A connective conditional syllogism consists of a conditional premise (If P then Q) and an exceptional premise that either affirms P or denies Q.
The conditional must be conjunctive (mutasila), e.g., 'If this is human, it is an animal'.
2
Recognize the two productive moods
Affirming the antecedent (wad' al-muqaddam) yields the consequent; denying the consequent (raf' al-tali) yields denial of the antecedent.
These correspond to modus ponens and modus tollens.
3
Understand the validity conditions
Three conditions: (1) the conditional must be affirmative; (2) it must be implicative (luzumiyya), not merely accidental; (3) either the conditional or the exception must be universal (or their time is unified).
Condition 2 ensures the connection is necessary; condition 3 prevents exceptions.
4
Apply the conditions to test an example
Take a given argument and check each condition. For example, 'If it rains, the ground is wet; it is raining; so the ground is wet.' This violates condition 2 if rain and wetness are not necessarily connected.
Always use Islamic examples in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ الِاسْتِثْنَائِي. وهو قسمان: اتصالي وانفصالي. فالاتصالي: هو ما يتركَّب من الشرطيَّة المتَّصلة ووضع المقدَّم؛ أي: إثباته. أو من الشرطيَّة المتَّصلة ورفع التالي؛ أي: نفيه. فَوَضْعُ المقدَّم ينتج وضع التالي، كقولنا: إن كان هذا إنساناً، فهو حيوان، لكنَّه إنسان، فهو حيوان. ورَفْعُ التالي ينتج رفع المقدَّم، كقولنا في المثال: «لكنَّه ليس بحيوان، فهو ليس بإنسان». فالمنتج من الاستثنائي الاتصالي وضع المقدَّم ورفع التالي. ويُشترط في إنتاجِهِ أمورٌ: الأوّل: أن تكون الشرطيَّة موجبة؛ إذ السالبة عقيمة. الثاني: أن تكون الشرطيَّة لزوميَّة إن كانت متصلة، أو عناديَّة إن كانت منفصلة. الثالث: أحد الأمرين كليَّة الشرطيَّة أو كليَّة الاستثنائيَّة. وقال العصام: الأولى أن يقال: وثالثها أحد الثلاثة: إمَّا كليَّة الشرطيَّة أو كليَّة الاستثنائيَّة أو اتِّحاد الاتصال والانفصال مع وقت الوضع أو الرفع. وأجيب بأن اتِّحاد وقتِهِما بعينه في قوَّة كليَّتِهِما.
A section on the exceptional syllogism. It is of two types: connective and disjunctive. The connective is that which is composed of a conditional conjunctive proposition and positing the antecedent, i.e., affirming it; or from a conditional conjunctive proposition and removing the consequent, i.e., negating it. So positing the antecedent produces the positing of the consequent, as in our statement: 'If this is a human, then it is an animal; but it is a human, therefore it is an animal.' And removing the consequent produces the removal of the antecedent, as in our statement in the example: 'But it is not an animal, therefore it is not a human.' So what is produced from the connective exceptional syllogism is positing the antecedent and removing the consequent. And conditions for its production are: first, that the conjunctive proposition be affirmative, for the negative is barren. Second, that the conjunctive proposition be implicative if it is connective, or oppositional if it is disjunctive. Third, one of the two matters: the universality of the conjunctive proposition or the universality of the exceptional proposition. And al-'Isam said: 'The better expression is that the third condition is one of three: either the universality of the conjunctive proposition, or the universality of the exceptional proposition, or the unity of the time of connection and disjunction with the time of positing or removal.' And it was answered that the unity of their time is effectively in the strength of their universality.
Source pages p. 849

Commentary
This passage defines the connective exceptional syllogism (al-qiyas al-istithna'i al-ittisali) and explains its two productive moods, along with the necessary conditions for validity. The author begins by stating that the exceptional syllogism is divided into connective and disjunctive. The connective type is composed of a conditional conjunctive proposition (e.g., 'If A then B') together with either the affirmation of the antecedent (or its negation in the disjunctive) or the negation of the consequent. These produce, respectively, the affirmation of the consequent and the negation of the antecedent. The examples given are from the Islamic intellectual tradition: 'If this is a human, it is an animal' – a standard logical example in Kalam. The passage then lists three conditions for the syllogism to yield a valid conclusion: first, the conditional must be affirmative, because a negative conditional indicates no connection between antecedent and consequent, so affirming one does not entail the other. Second, the conditional must be implicative (luzumiyya) for the connective type, meaning the consequent necessarily follows from the antecedent; for the disjunctive type, it must be oppositional (inadadiyya). Third, either the conditional proposition or the exceptional premise must be universal (i.e., covering all cases), because without universality the inference may not hold in all instances. The commentator al-'Isam suggests a refined formulation, adding the possibility that the time of the connection and the time of the affirmation/negation are the same, which effectively acts like universality. The response is that this unity of time is essentially equivalent to universality in strength. The key lesson is that the connective exceptional syllogism has only two valid moods – affirming the antecedent and denying the consequent – and both require these strict conditions to produce certainty.

Key Terms
القياس الاستثنائي
الشرطيَّة المتصلة
وضع المقدَّم
رفع التالي
لزوميَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It must be particular and oppositional.

Key Takeaway
The connective conditional syllogism is valid only in two moods (modus ponens and modus tollens) and requires an affirmative, implicative conditional and universality in either premise or time.

The Syllogism

Productive and Sterile Moods of the Conditional Syllogism
Author defines the productive and sterile modes of the conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two productive moods (affirming antecedent, denying consequent) from the two sterile moods (affirming consequent, denying antecedent) in a connected conditional syllogism and construct a valid inference.

Core Idea
In a conditional syllogism, affirming the antecedent and denying the consequent are valid; the reverse are not.

In this passage, the author introduces the conditional syllogism (qiyās istithnā'ī) and specifies which moods are productive (muntij) and which are sterile (ghayr muntij). Understanding this is essential for constructing sound arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where conditional reasoning is frequent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Locate the 'if...then' statement. Extract the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tālī). For example, in 'If this is a human, then it is an animal', the antecedent is 'this is a human' and the consequent is 'it is an animal'.
2
Determine the exceptive premise
Check whether the secondary premise affirms the antecedent (e.g., 'it is a human') or denies the consequent (e.g., 'it is not an animal'), or something else. Only these two cases produce valid conclusions.
3
Apply the rule for productive moods
If the exceptive premise affirms the antecedent, conclude the consequent. If it denies the consequent, conclude the negation of the antecedent. These are the two productive moods: modus ponens and modus tollens.
4
Check for sterile moods
If the exceptive premise affirms the consequent or denies the antecedent, the argument is sterile (invalid). The reason is that the consequent may be more general than the antecedent, so affirming the consequent or denying the antecedent does not yield certainty.
Example: From 'If it is a human, then it is an animal' and 'it is an animal', you cannot conclude 'it is a human' because it could be another animal.
5
Practice with the provided example
Take the example given: 'If this is a human, then it is an animal; but it is a human, therefore it is an animal' (valid, modus ponens). Then test the sterile moods: 'If this is a human, then it is an animal; but it is an animal' does not yield 'it is a human' (invalid).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ الِاسْتِثْنَائِي. ينتج من المتصلة الموضوعة فيه وضع المقدم ورفع التالي. فوضع المقدم ينتج وضع التالي، ورفع التالي ينتج رفع المقدم. ولا عكس في شيء منهما؛ أي: لا ينتج وضع التالي وضع المقدم، ولا رفع المقدم رفع التالي؛ لجواز كون التالي أعم من المقدم. مثال ذلك: إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان، لكنه إنسان، فهو حيوان. وإن كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان، لكنه ليس بحيوان، فهو ليس بإنسان.
Chapter: On the conditional syllogism. From the connected conditional placed in it, putting the antecedent and raising the consequent are productive. So putting the antecedent produces the consequent, and raising the consequent produces raising the antecedent. There is no reverse in any of them; i.e., putting the consequent does not produce putting the antecedent, nor does raising the antecedent produce raising the consequent; because the consequent may be more general than the antecedent. An example of that: If this is a human, then it is an animal; but it is a human, therefore it is an animal. And if this is a human, then it is an animal; but it is not an animal, therefore it is not a human.
Source pages p. 850

Commentary
This passage is a concise definition of the conditional (istithnā'ī) syllogism, specifically the connected (muttasil) type. It begins by stating that from the conditional premise (the major premise) the valid inferences are 'putting the antecedent' (wadʿ al-muqaddam) and 'raising the consequent' (rafʿ al-tālī). The author then explains the two productive moods: if we affirm the antecedent (e.g., 'it is a human'), we can conclude the consequent ('it is an animal'); if we deny the consequent (e.g., 'it is not an animal'), we can conclude the negation of the antecedent ('it is not a human'). The passage then explicitly states that the other two moods—affirming the consequent or denying the antecedent—are not productive. The reason given is that the consequent may be more general (aʿamm) than the antecedent. For example, being an animal is more general than being a human; so from 'it is an animal' we cannot conclude 'it is a human', nor from 'it is not a human' can we conclude 'it is not an animal'. This is a key logical insight that guards against the fallacy of affirming the consequent or denying the antecedent. The example provided, drawn from the common example of human and animal, is a standard illustration in Islamic logic.

The Syllogism

Productive and Unproductive Forms of the Connected Conditional Syllogism
Defines the connected conditional syllogism and its four forms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two productive forms of the connected conditional syllogism and explain why the other two are unproductive, even in the special case of equal implication.

Core Idea
In the connected conditional, only affirming the antecedent or denying the consequent yields certain conclusions.

This passage introduces the foundation of conditional reasoning in Islamic logic. It presents the four possible forms of the connected conditional syllogism and distinguishes the two that are logically valid from the two that are fallacious. Understanding this distinction is crucial for assessing arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh that rely on conditional premises. The commentators also address a subtle case where the implication is equal, showing that the unproductive forms become productive only due to the material equality, not the logical form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two productive forms
In the Arabic, locate the statement: 'وضع المقدم ينتج وضع التالي' (placing the antecedent produces placing the consequent) and 'رفع التالي ينتج رفع المقدم' (raising the consequent produces raising the antecedent). These are the two valid moods: modus ponens and modus tollens.
2
Identify the two unproductive forms
Find the negation: 'لا ينتج وضع التالي وضع المقدم' (placing the consequent does not produce placing the antecedent) and 'لا ينتج رفع المقدم رفع التالي' (raising the antecedent does not produce raising the consequent). These are the fallacies of affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent.
The passage gives the reason: 'لجواز كون التالي أعم من المقدم' (because the consequent may be more general than the antecedent).
3
Understand the reason using an example
The passage provides an implicit example: 'If it is human, then it is animal.' Here, 'animal' is more general than 'human'. Affirming 'it is animal' does not entail 'it is human'; denying 'it is human' does not entail 'it is not animal'.
This illustrates why the unproductive forms fail in most cases.
4
Consider the special case of equal implication
The commentators Al-Dassuqi and Al-Attar discuss that if the implication is equal (musawi), i.e., antecedent and consequent are co-extensive, then the unproductive forms become productive. For example, if the conditional is 'If it is rational, then it is laughing,' then affirming the consequent or denying the antecedent does yield a conclusion. However, they stress that this is due to the material equality, not the logical form itself.
This nuance shows that logical validity depends on the form, not specific material content.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ الِاسْتِثْنَائِي. ينتج، (وَرَفْعُ التَّالِي) عطفٌ عليه ؛ أي: يَنتُجُ التالي من المتّصلة الموضوعة في القياس الاستثنائي : وضعُ المقدَّم ورفعُ التّالي . لكنَّ وضعَ المقدَّمِ يُنتِجُ وضعَ التَّالي، ورفعَ التّالي يُنتِجُ رفعَ المقدَّم كما ذكرنا . ولا عكس في شيءٍ منهما ؛ أي: لا يُنتج وضعُ التّالي وضع المقدَّم، ولا رفع المقدَّم رفعَ التَّالي ؛ لجواز كونِ التَّالي أعم من المقدَّم. الدسوقي: والرَّابِطُ مَحذوفٌ قدَّرَهُ الشَّارحُ بقولِهِ : (فيه)، وحينئذٍ فَلَا يرد على المصنفِ أنَّ الجملة إذا وقعَتْ خبراً ؛ لَا بُدَّ فيها مِن رابط يعودُ على المبتدأ، ولا رابط هنا . (قَوْلُهُ : لِجَوَازِ كَوْنِ الثَّالِي ... إلخ) عِلَّةٌ لِقَولِهِ : (لينتج) كَمَا إِذا قُلْنَا في المثالِ المذكور : لكنَّه حيوانٌ، فَلَا يلزمُ منهُ : فَهُوَ إنسانٌ ؛ لِكَونِ الأَوَّلِ أعمَّ مِنَ الثَّاني . فَلَا يلزمُ من وضع التّالي وضع المقدَّم ؛ إذْ لا يلزم من وجودِ الأعمّ وجود الأخصّ . وكذا لا يلزم من رفع المقدَّم رفعُ التّالي ؛ إذ لا يلزم من عدمِ الأخصّ عدم الأعم، هذا في الاستثنائي الاتصالي.
Chapter on the conditional syllogism. It produces, and (raising the consequent) is a conjunct to it; i.e., the consequent is produced from the connected [conditional] placed in the conditional syllogism: placing the antecedent and raising the consequent. But placing the antecedent produces placing the consequent, and raising the consequent produces raising the antecedent, as we mentioned. And there is no converse in any of them; i.e., placing the consequent does not produce placing the antecedent, nor does raising the antecedent produce raising the consequent, because it is possible that the consequent is more general than the antecedent. Al-Dassuqi: The connector is omitted; the commentator estimated it by his saying (in it). Then it is not objected to the author that when a sentence occurs as a predicate, it must contain a connector referring back to the subject, and there is no connector here. (His statement: But placing the antecedent produces ... etc.) Perhaps the attribution of production to placing the antecedent is a mental metaphor, because production in reality belongs to the syllogism due to that. End of the report. Al-Attar: Indeed (His statement: i.e., placing the consequent does not produce ... etc.) is an explicit mention of the two unproductive forms among the four. If you say: This is correct only when the implication is general; but if it is equal, then the exception of one of the two extremes produces the other, and the exception of the opposite of each produces the opposite of the other. I say: The equal implication is in reality two implications, so each of the four mentioned forms has an implication from the two implications. You see that the entailment of the existence of the necessary from the existence of the necessary condition is not insofar as it is a necessary condition but insofar as it is a necessary condition; and similarly the entailment of the non-existence of the necessary condition from the non-existence of the necessary is not insofar as it is a necessary condition but insofar as it is necessary. And it is also answered that the exception of the consequent and the opposite of the two antecedents only produce the antecedent and the opposite of the consequent in the material of equality, due to the specificity of the material, not due to the essence of the syllogism. (His statement: because it is possible that the consequent ... etc.) is a reason for his statement: (does not produce), as when we say in the mentioned example: 'But it is an animal,' so it does not follow from it: 'It is a human,' because the first is more general than the second. So placing the consequent does not necessitate placing the antecedent, because the existence of the more general does not necessitate the existence of the more specific. Likewise, raising the antecedent does not necessitate raising the consequent, because the non-existence of the more specific does not necessitate the non-existence of the more general. This is regarding the connected conditional.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed treatment of the connected conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i muttasil). It begins by stating the two valid moods: affirming the antecedent leads to affirming the consequent, and denying the consequent leads to denying the antecedent. Then it explains the two invalid moods: affirming the consequent does not lead to affirming the antecedent, and denying the antecedent does not lead to denying the consequent. The reason given is that the consequent may be more general (a'amm) than the antecedent. For example, in the conditional 'If it is a human, then it is an animal,' the consequent 'animal' is more general, so from 'it is an animal' we cannot deduce 'it is a human'. Similarly, from 'it is not human' we cannot deduce 'it is not animal'. The commentary by al-Dassuqi and al-Attar addresses a potential objection: if the implication is equal (musawi), i.e., the antecedent and consequent are co-extensive, then the unproductive moods become productive. They clarify that this is due to the material equality, not the logical form itself, and that in such cases the implication is actually two implications. The passage reinforces the general rule that in the connected conditional, the relationship is one-way unless the terms are equal.

The Syllogism

True Disjunction (Manfasila Haqiqiyya): Two Productive Moods
Classifies the disjunctive conditional syllogism into three types and states the productive forms for each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to validate the two productive moods of a true disjunction: affirming one part negates the other, and negating one part affirms the other.

Core Idea
In a true disjunction, both parts are mutually exclusive and exhaustive, so both affirmation and negation yield a conclusion.

The disjunctive conditional syllogism begins with a disjunctive major premise. The most powerful type is the true disjunction (manfasila haqiqiyya), where the two parts cannot be both true nor both false. This lesson focuses on the two valid inference patterns unique to this type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the true disjunction in the source
Locate the phrase 'منفصلة حقيقية' and understand that it implies both man' al-jam' and man' al-khuluw.
2
Test affirming one part
Check if affirming one part allows you to negate the other. Because the parts cannot both be true, affirming one forces the other false.
3
Test negating one part
Check if negating one part allows you to affirm the other. Because they cannot both be false, negating one forces the other true.
4
Apply al-Dassuqi's conditions
The disjunction must be affirmative, universal, and essential. Explain why these conditions prevent accidental disjunctions that would invalidate the inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا الاستثنائي الانفصالي، فهو إما أن يتركَّبَ : - من منفصلة حقيقية ووضع أحدِ الجزأين أو رفعِه . - وإما من منفصلة مانعة الجمع ووضع أحد الجزأين، وإما منفصلة مانعة الخلو ورفع أحد الجزأين. فإنْ كانَ الأوَّل ؛ فوَضعُ كلِّ واحدٍ من الجزأين ينتج رفع الآخر، ورفع كلِّ واحدٍ من الجزأين ينتج وضعَ الآخر. وإن كان الثَّاني ؛ فوضع كلِّ واحدٍ من الجزأين ينتج رفعَ الآخر وإن كان الثَّالث ؛ فرفع كلِّ واحدٍ من الجزأين ينتج وضع الآخر. كما لوّحَ إليهِ بِقولِهِ : (وَالحَقِيقَةِ وَضْعُ كُلِّ منَ الجزأين، فقوله: الحقيقة بالجرّ عطفٌ على قوله المتّصلة»، وقوله: «وضعُ» بالرَّفعِ الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ: مِنْ مُنْفَصِلَةٍ حَقِيقِيَّة) أي : بشرط أن تكونَ مُوجبةٌ؛ إِذْ لو كانَتْ سالبةٌ ; فَمُقتضاها عدم المنافاةِ، وَلَا بُدَّ أنْ تكونَ إحدى المقدِّمتين كُلِّيَّةٌ، وأنْ تكونَ المنفصلة عناديَّةً لَا اتفاقية. انتهى راجع ياسين. (قَوْلُهُ : وَوَضْع) أي : إثباتِ عطف على (منفصلة). (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ رَفْعِهِ) أي: نَفْيه . (قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنْ كَانَ الْأَوَّل) أي : وهو المركَّبُ من منفصلة حقيقية ووضع ... إلخ . العطار: (قَوْلُهُ : إِذْ لَا يَلْزَمُ) عِلَّةٌ لِقَوْلِهِ : (فَلَا يلزم).
As for the disjunctive conditional, it is composed either: from a true disjunction and placing one of the two parts or its negation; or from an exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam') and placing one of the two parts; or from an inclusive disjunction (mani'at al-khuluw) and negating one of the two parts. If it is the first, then placing each one of the two parts produces the negation of the other, and negating each one of the two parts produces the placement of the other. If it is the second, then placing each one of the two parts produces the negation of the other. If it is the third, then negating each one of the two parts produces the placement of the other. As he alluded to by his saying: (Al-Haqiqa: placing each of the two parts). His word 'al-haqiqa' is in the genitive, conjoined to his word 'al-muttasila', and his word 'wad'' is in the nominative. Al-Dassuqi: (His statement: from a true disjunction) means on condition that it is affirmative; for if it were negative, its implication would be lack of contradiction. And one of the two premises must be universal, and the disjunction must be essential not accidental. End, see Yasin. (His statement: and placing) means affirming, conjoined to 'disjunction'. (His statement: or its negation) means its denial. (His statement: if it is the first) i.e., the composite of a true disjunction and placing... etc. Al-Attar: (His statement: because it does not entail) is a reason for his statement: (so it does not entail).
Source pages p. 852

Commentary
This passage explains the disjunctive conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i munfasil). It categorizes it into three types based on the nature of the disjunction: (1) true disjunction (haqiqiyya), where the two parts cannot both be true nor both false (like 'either this is a human or a beast'); (2) exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam'), where the two parts cannot both be true but may both be false (like 'either this is a human or a stone'); (3) inclusive disjunction (mani'at al-khuluw), where the two parts cannot both be false but may both be true (like 'either this is a human or an animal'). For each type, the productive moods are given. For true disjunction: affirming one part yields the negation of the other, and negating one part yields the affirmation of the other. For exclusive: only affirming one part yields the negation of the other; negating does not yield a conclusion because both may be false. For inclusive: only negating one part yields the affirmation of the other; affirming does not yield a conclusion because both may be true. The commentary by al-Dassuqi adds conditions: the disjunction must be affirmative, universal, and essential, not accidental. Al-Attar's remark ties back to the earlier discussion about the connected conditional. The lesson provides the foundational rules for reasoning with disjunctives in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
منفصلة (munfasila): A disjunctive proposition that asserts separation between two parts.
حقيقية (haqiqiyya): A true disjunction where the two parts cannot coexist nor both be absent (one true, one false necessarily).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a true disjunction (manfasila haqiqiyya), which of the following operations yields a valid conclusion?
A) Affirming one part only
B) Negating one part only
C) Both affirming and negating
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
In a true disjunction, both affirming and negating a part lead to a necessary conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Exclusive Disjunction (Mani'at al-Jam'): Only Affirmation is Productive
Classifies the disjunctive conditional syllogism into three types and states the productive forms for each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine that in an exclusive disjunction, affirming one part yields the negation of the other, but negating one part does not yield a conclusion.

Core Idea
In an exclusive disjunction, parts cannot both be true, so affirming one eliminates the other; but negating leaves both false as a possibility.

Not all disjunctions are exhaustive. In an exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam'), the two parts cannot both be true, but they may both be false. This means that only one of the two standard operations is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exclusive disjunction
Find 'مانعة الجمع' in the source and note its truth condition: both parts cannot be true.
2
Test affirming one part
If you know one part is true, can you conclude the other is false? Yes, because they cannot both be true.
3
Test negating one part
If you know one part is false, can you conclude the other is true? No, because both could be false.
4
Check the condition of essentiality
Al-Dassuqi's condition that the disjunction be essential (عندادية) ensures the exclusivity is necessary, not accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا الاستثنائي الانفصالي، فهو إما أن يتركَّبَ : - من منفصلة حقيقية ووضع أحدِ الجزأين أو رفعِه . - وإما من منفصلة مانعة الجمع ووضع أحد الجزأين، وإما منفصلة مانعة الخلو ورفع أحد الجزأين. فإنْ كانَ الأوَّل ؛ فوَضعُ كلِّ واحدٍ من الجزأين ينتج رفع الآخر، ورفع كلِّ واحدٍ من الجزأين ينتج وضعَ الآخر. وإن كان الثَّاني ؛ فوضع كلِّ واحدٍ من الجزأين ينتج رفعَ الآخر وإن كان الثَّالث ؛ فرفع كلِّ واحدٍ من الجزأين ينتج وضع الآخر. كما لوّحَ إليهِ بِقولِهِ : (وَالحَقِيقَةِ وَضْعُ كُلِّ منَ الجزأين، فقوله: الحقيقة بالجرّ عطفٌ على قوله المتّصلة»، وقوله: «وضعُ» بالرَّفعِ الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ: مِنْ مُنْفَصِلَةٍ حَقِيقِيَّة) أي : بشرط أن تكونَ مُوجبةٌ؛ إِذْ لو كانَتْ سالبةٌ ; فَمُقتضاها عدم المنافاةِ، وَلَا بُدَّ أنْ تكونَ إحدى المقدِّمتين كُلِّيَّةٌ، وأنْ تكونَ المنفصلة عناديَّةً لَا اتفاقية. انتهى راجع ياسين. (قَوْلُهُ : وَوَضْع) أي : إثباتِ عطف على (منفصلة). (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ رَفْعِهِ) أي: نَفْيه . (قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنْ كَانَ الْأَوَّل) أي : وهو المركَّبُ من منفصلة حقيقية ووضع ... إلخ . العطار: (قَوْلُهُ : إِذْ لَا يَلْزَمُ) عِلَّةٌ لِقَوْلِهِ : (فَلَا يلزم).
As for the disjunctive conditional, it is composed either: from a true disjunction and placing one of the two parts or its negation; or from an exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam') and placing one of the two parts; or from an inclusive disjunction (mani'at al-khuluw) and negating one of the two parts. If it is the first, then placing each one of the two parts produces the negation of the other, and negating each one of the two parts produces the placement of the other. If it is the second, then placing each one of the two parts produces the negation of the other. If it is the third, then negating each one of the two parts produces the placement of the other. As he alluded to by his saying: (Al-Haqiqa: placing each of the two parts). His word 'al-haqiqa' is in the genitive, conjoined to his word 'al-muttasila', and his word 'wad'' is in the nominative. Al-Dassuqi: (His statement: from a true disjunction) means on condition that it is affirmative; for if it were negative, its implication would be lack of contradiction. And one of the two premises must be universal, and the disjunction must be essential not accidental. End, see Yasin. (His statement: and placing) means affirming, conjoined to 'disjunction'. (His statement: or its negation) means its denial. (His statement: if it is the first) i.e., the composite of a true disjunction and placing... etc. Al-Attar: (His statement: because it does not entail) is a reason for his statement: (so it does not entail).
Source pages p. 852

Commentary
This passage explains the disjunctive conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i munfasil). It categorizes it into three types based on the nature of the disjunction: (1) true disjunction (haqiqiyya), where the two parts cannot both be true nor both false (like 'either this is a human or a beast'); (2) exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam'), where the two parts cannot both be true but may both be false (like 'either this is a human or a stone'); (3) inclusive disjunction (mani'at al-khuluw), where the two parts cannot both be false but may both be true (like 'either this is a human or an animal'). For each type, the productive moods are given. For true disjunction: affirming one part yields the negation of the other, and negating one part yields the affirmation of the other. For exclusive: only affirming one part yields the negation of the other; negating does not yield a conclusion because both may be false. For inclusive: only negating one part yields the affirmation of the other; affirming does not yield a conclusion because both may be true. The commentary by al-Dassuqi adds conditions: the disjunction must be affirmative, universal, and essential, not accidental. Al-Attar's remark ties back to the earlier discussion about the connected conditional. The lesson provides the foundational rules for reasoning with disjunctives in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
منفصلة (munfasila): A disjunctive proposition that asserts separation between two parts.
مانعة الجمع (mani'at al-jam'): An exclusive disjunction where parts cannot both be true but may both be false.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Affirming one part yields negation of the other
B) Negating one part yields affirmation of the other
C) Both A and B are valid
D) Neither is valid

Key Takeaway
In an exclusive disjunction, only affirming one part allows you to negate the other; negating is inconclusive.

Inclusive Disjunction (Mani'at al-Khuluw): Only Negation is Productive
Classifies the disjunctive conditional syllogism into three types and states the productive forms for each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine that in an inclusive disjunction, negating one part yields the affirmation of the other, but affirming one part does not yield a conclusion.

Core Idea
In an inclusive disjunction, parts cannot both be false, so negating one forces the other to be true; but affirming does not preclude both being true.

The third type of disjunction, inclusive (mani'at al-khuluw), allows both parts to be true but not both false. This has exactly the opposite productive rule from the exclusive type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the inclusive disjunction
Find 'مانعة الخلو' in the source and understand that it prohibits both falsehood.
2
Test negating one part
If you know one part is false, can you conclude the other is true? Yes, because they cannot both be false.
3
Test affirming one part
If you know one part is true, can you conclude the other is false? No, because both could be true.
4
Review the conditions from the commentary
Al-Dassuqi's conditions (affirmative, universal, essential) also apply to inclusive disjunctions to ensure the relation is necessary.
