Rules of the Connected Conditional Syllogism
Author explains inference rules for muttasil conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid and invalid inferences in a connected conditional syllogism, specifically distinguishing modus ponens from the fallacies of affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent.

Core Idea
In a connected conditional, affirming the antecedent is valid but denying it is not.

The connected conditional (al-muttasil) is the familiar 'if...then' proposition. Understanding its inference rules is critical for evaluating many arguments in Kalam and Fiqh. This lesson unpacks the valid forms and common pitfalls using the example from the sharh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
In a conditional 'If A then B', A is the antecedent and B is the consequent. For example: 'If Zayd comes, then 'Amr leaves.'
2
Recognize the valid form: modus ponens
Affirming the antecedent (positing A) allows you to conclude the consequent (B). This is valid and known as modus ponens.
The author states: 'from positing the antecedent follows positing the consequent.'
3
Recognize the invalid form: affirming the consequent
From positing the consequent (B), you cannot conclude the antecedent (A). The author explains: 'it is not necessary that from the existence of the more general follows the existence of the more specific.'
Example: 'If it rains, the ground is wet. The ground is wet, therefore it rained.' This is invalid.
4
Recognize the invalid form: denying the antecedent
From removing the antecedent (not A), you cannot conclude the removal of the consequent (not B). The author says: 'it is not necessary that from the absence of the more specific follows the absence of the more general.'
Example: 'If Zayd comes, 'Amr leaves. Zayd does not come, therefore 'Amr does not leave.' Invalid.
5
Practice with Islamic examples
Consider: 'If the world is temporally originated, it has a cause. The world is temporally originated, therefore it has a cause.' This is valid modus ponens. Now, 'It has a cause, therefore it is temporally originated' is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَلَا يلزمُ من وضع التّالي وضع المقدَّم ؛ إذْ لا يلزم من وجودِ الأعمّ وجود الأخصّ . وكذا لا يلزم من رفع المقدَّم رفعُ التّالي ؛ إذ لا يلزم من عدمِ الأخصّ عدم الأعم، هذا في الاستثنائي الاتصالي. وأمَّا الاستثنائي الانفصالي، فهو إما أن يتركَّبَ : - من منفصلة حقيقية ووضع أحدِ الجزأين أو رفعِه . - وإما من منفصلة مانعة الجمع ووضع أحد الجزأين، وإما منفصلة مانعة الخلو ورفع أحد الجزأين. فإنْ كانَ الأَوَّل ؛ فوَضعُ كلِّ واحدٍ من الجزأين ينتج رفع الآخر، ورفع كلِّ واحدٍ من الجزأين ينتج وضعَ الآخر. وإن كان الثَّاني ؛ فوضع كلِّ واحدٍ من الجزأين ينتج رفعَ الآخر وإن كان الثَّالث ؛ فرفع كلِّ واحدٍ من الجزأين ينتج وضع الآخر. كما لوّحَ إليهِ بِقولِهِ : (وَالحَقِيقَةِ وَضْعُ كُلِّ منَ الجزأين، فقوله: الحقيقة بالجرّ عطفٌ على قوله المتّصلة»، وقوله: «وضعُ» بالرَّفعِ
It is not necessary that from positing the consequent follows positing the antecedent; for it is not necessary that from the existence of the more general follows the existence of the more specific. Likewise, it is not necessary that from removing the antecedent follows removing the consequent; for it is not necessary that from the absence of the more specific follows the absence of the more general. This is in the connected exceptional [syllogism]. As for the disjunctive exceptional [syllogism], it is either composed of a true disjunction and the positing of one of the two parts or its removal; or of a disjunction that prevents conjunction (mani'at al-jam') and the positing of one of the two parts; or of a disjunction that prevents emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw) and the removal of one of the two parts. If it is the first, then the positing of each of the two parts entails the removal of the other, and the removal of each of the two parts entails the positing of the other. If it is the second, then the positing of each of the two parts entails the removal of the other. If it is the third, then the removal of each of the two parts entails the positing of the other. As he alluded to with his saying: 'and the true [disjunction] the positing of each of the two parts', so his saying 'the true' is in the genitive as an apposition to his saying 'the connected', and his saying 'the positing' is in the nominative.
Source pages p. 852

Commentary
This passage from the sharh explains the inference rules for two types of conditional syllogisms (al-qiyas al-istithna'i). First, it covers the connected conditional (muttasil), e.g., 'If Zayd comes, then 'Amr leaves.' From the positing of the antecedent (Zayd comes), we conclude the consequent (Amr leaves). But from the positing of the consequent, we cannot conclude the antecedent, just as from the existence of the more general (e.g., animal) we cannot conclude the existence of the more specific (e.g., human). Similarly, from the removal of the antecedent, we cannot conclude the removal of the consequent, because the consequent might still occur from another cause. The passage then turns to the disjunctive conditional (munfasil), which has three subtypes. The first is a true disjunction (haqiqiyya) where the parts are mutually exclusive and exhaustive, e.g., 'The number is either even or odd.' Here, positing either part removes the other, and removing either part posits the other. The second is a disjunction that prevents conjunction (mani'at al-jam') but allows emptiness, e.g., 'This is either wine or grape juice.' Positing one part removes the other, but removing one does not necessitate positing the other. The third is a disjunction that prevents emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw) but allows conjunction, e.g., 'This is either water or a liquid.' Removing one part posits the other. The passage ends with a grammatical note about the phrase 'wa al-haqiqati wad'u kulli...' indicating how the words are parsed (genitive and nominative) to convey the correct meaning.

Key Terms
المقدَّم
التّالي
القياس الاستثنائي الاتصالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does positing the consequent not necessitate positing the antecedent in a connected conditional?
A) Because the antecedent may be false when the consequent is true.
B) Because the consequent is more general than the antecedent.
C) Because the antecedent is more specific than the consequent.
D) Because the logicians have not proved it.

Key Takeaway
In a connected conditional, only modus ponens and modus tollens are valid; affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent are fallacies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the following argument: 'If the Prophet's ﷺ sunnah is a source of law, then it is binding. The Prophet's ﷺ sunnah is a source of law, therefore it is binding.' Is this valid? Explain why.
?
Construct a modus tollens argument from usul al-fiqh: 'If the contract is valid, the sale is permissible. The sale is not permissible, therefore the contract is not valid.' Identify the logical form.

The Syllogism

Rules of the Disjunctive Conditional Syllogism
Author explains inference rules for munfasil conditional syllogisms and their subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of disjunctive syllogisms (true, exclusive, exhaustive) and apply their specific inference rules to determine valid conclusions.

Core Idea
The inference rules of a disjunction depend on whether it is exclusive, exhaustive, or both.

Disjunctive syllogisms (al-munfasil) appear in the form 'either...or'. But not all 'or' statements are the same. Islamic logicians classify three types: true disjunction (exclusive and exhaustive), exclusive only, and exhaustive only. Each has distinct inference patterns. This lesson guides you through them using the sharh's explanation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of disjunction
1) Haqiqiyya (true): parts cannot be both true nor both false (exclusive and exhaustive). 2) Mani'at al-jam': parts cannot both be true, but both can be false (exclusive only). 3) Mani'at al-khuluw: parts cannot both be false, but both can be true (exhaustive only).
The passage distinguishes these based on whether the syllogism 'is composed of a true disjunction... or of a disjunction that prevents conjunction... or of a disjunction that prevents emptiness.'
2
Learn rules for true disjunction (haqiqiyya)
Positing one part entails removing the other. Removing one part entails positing the other. Both directions are valid.
Example: 'The number is either even or odd.' If it is even, it cannot be odd. If it is not even, it is odd.
3
Learn rules for exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam')
Positing one part entails removing the other. But removing one part does NOT necessitate positing the other, because both could be false.
Example: 'Either this is wine or it is grape juice.' If it is wine, it cannot be grape juice. But if it is not wine, it might not be grape juice either (e.g., vinegar).
4
Learn rules for exhaustive disjunction (mani'at al-khuluw)
Removing one part entails positing the other. But positing one part does NOT necessitate removing the other, because both could be true.
Example: 'Either this is water or it is a liquid.' If it is not water, it must be a liquid (since everything is either water or liquid). But if it is water, it is also a liquid (both true).
5
Apply to Islamic examples
In fiqh: 'Either this transaction is a valid sale or it is riba' (true disjunction). If it is a valid sale, it is not riba. If it is not a valid sale, it is riba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَلَا يلزمُ من وضع التّالي وضع المقدَّم ؛ إذْ لا يلزم من وجودِ الأعمّ وجود الأخصّ . وكذا لا يلزم من رفع المقدَّم رفعُ التّالي ؛ إذ لا يلزم من عدمِ الأخصّ عدم الأعم، هذا في الاستثنائي الاتصالي. وأمَّا الاستثنائي الانفصالي، فهو إما أن يتركَّبَ : - من منفصلة حقيقية ووضع أحدِ الجزأين أو رفعِه . - وإما من منفصلة مانعة الجمع ووضع أحد الجزأين، وإما منفصلة مانعة الخلو ورفع أحد الجزأين. فإنْ كانَ الأَوَّل ؛ فوَضعُ كلِّ واحدٍ من الجزأين ينتج رفع الآخر، ورفع كلِّ واحدٍ من الجزأين ينتج وضعَ الآخر. وإن كان الثَّاني ؛ فوضع كلِّ واحدٍ من الجزأين ينتج رفعَ الآخر وإن كان الثَّالث ؛ فرفع كلِّ واحدٍ من الجزأين ينتج وضع الآخر. كما لوّحَ إليهِ بِقولِهِ : (وَالحَقِيقَةِ وَضْعُ كُلِّ منَ الجزأين، فقوله: الحقيقة بالجرّ عطفٌ على قوله المتّصلة»، وقوله: «وضعُ» بالرَّفعِ
It is not necessary that from positing the consequent follows positing the antecedent; for it is not necessary that from the existence of the more general follows the existence of the more specific. Likewise, it is not necessary that from removing the antecedent follows removing the consequent; for it is not necessary that from the absence of the more specific follows the absence of the more general. This is in the connected exceptional [syllogism]. As for the disjunctive exceptional [syllogism], it is either composed of a true disjunction and the positing of one of the two parts or its removal; or of a disjunction that prevents conjunction (mani'at al-jam') and the positing of one of the two parts; or of a disjunction that prevents emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw) and the removal of one of the two parts. If it is the first, then the positing of each of the two parts entails the removal of the other, and the removal of each of the two parts entails the positing of the other. If it is the second, then the positing of each of the two parts entails the removal of the other. If it is the third, then the removal of each of the two parts entails the positing of the other. As he alluded to with his saying: 'and the true [disjunction] the positing of each of the two parts', so his saying 'the true' is in the genitive as an apposition to his saying 'the connected', and his saying 'the positing' is in the nominative.
Source pages p. 852

Commentary
This passage from the sharh explains the inference rules for two types of conditional syllogisms (al-qiyas al-istithna'i). First, it covers the connected conditional (muttasil), e.g., 'If Zayd comes, then 'Amr leaves.' From the positing of the antecedent (Zayd comes), we conclude the consequent (Amr leaves). But from the positing of the consequent, we cannot conclude the antecedent, just as from the existence of the more general (e.g., animal) we cannot conclude the existence of the more specific (e.g., human). Similarly, from the removal of the antecedent, we cannot conclude the removal of the consequent, because the consequent might still occur from another cause. The passage then turns to the disjunctive conditional (munfasil), which has three subtypes. The first is a true disjunction (haqiqiyya) where the parts are mutually exclusive and exhaustive, e.g., 'The number is either even or odd.' Here, positing either part removes the other, and removing either part posits the other. The second is a disjunction that prevents conjunction (mani'at al-jam') but allows emptiness, e.g., 'This is either wine or grape juice.' Positing one part removes the other, but removing one does not necessitate positing the other. The third is a disjunction that prevents emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw) but allows conjunction, e.g., 'This is either water or a liquid.' Removing one part posits the other. The passage ends with a grammatical note about the phrase 'wa al-haqiqati wad'u kulli...' indicating how the words are parsed (genitive and nominative) to convey the correct meaning.

Key Terms
المنفصلة الحقيقية
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو

Checkpoint

A) Positing one part entails positing the other.
B) Removing one part entails removing the other.
C) Positing one part entails removing the other.
D) Removing one part entails positing the other.

Key Takeaway
The validity of inferences in disjunctive syllogisms depends on whether the disjunction is true, exclusive only, or exhaustive only.

Disjunctive Syllogism: Types and Inferences
Author defines the disjunctive syllogism and its two main types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of disjunctive syllogisms (مانعة الجمع ومانعة الخلو) and determine what conclusion follows from affirming or negating a part.

Core Idea
A true disjunction flips truth: affirm one part, negate the other; negate one part, affirm the other.

In this passage, the author defines the disjunctive syllogism (القياس الاستثنائي) and explains the two types of disjunctive premises: exclusive of both (مانعة الجمع) and exclusive of neither (مانعة الخلو). Understanding which type you are dealing with tells you what conclusion follows when you affirm or negate one of the disjuncts. This is crucial for constructing valid arguments in Islamic scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disjunction
Determine whether the disjunction is a true disjunction (exclusive OR, i.e., both cannot be true and both cannot be false), exclusive of both (مانعة الجمع: both cannot be true but both may be false), or exclusive of neither (مانعة الخلو: both cannot be false but both may be true).
The passage focuses on the true disjunction, which combines both properties.
2
Determine the operation: affirmation or negation
Note whether the syllogism affirms (وضع) or negates (رفع) one of the two parts. This is the 'exception' (استثناء) step.
3
Apply the rule for that type
For a true disjunction: affirmation of one part → negation of the other; negation of one part → affirmation of the other. For مانعة الجمع alone: only affirmation implies negation; negation implies nothing. For مانعة الخلو alone: only negation implies affirmation; affirmation implies nothing.
The passage illustrates these rules with examples: as in مانعة الجمع for affirmation, and as in مانعة الخلو for negation.
4
Derive the conclusion
Based on the type and operation, write the logical conclusion. Example: From a true disjunction 'Either A or B', if A is affirmed, then B is negated.

Chapter: On the Disjunctive Syllogism. It is syntactically connected to his statement: 'the positing of the antecedent', so it falls under the category of conjoining two objects of two different operators, and the genitive is placed before the nominative, as in our saying: 'In the house is Zayd, and in the room is Amr.' The meaning is that the disjunctive syllogism produces, from the conditional conjunctive proposition placed in it, the affirmation of the antecedent and the negation of the consequent as previously mentioned. Commentary
This passage defines the disjunctive syllogism (القياس الاستثنائي) and explains the two types of disjunctive premises. The author first notes a syntactic connection to earlier discussion. Then he states that the disjunctive syllogism from a conditional conjunctive (متصل) yields affirmation of the antecedent and negation of the consequent. From a true disjunction (منفصلة حقيقية), affirmation of one part gives negation of the other, and vice versa. He distinguishes two sub-types: exclusive of both (مانعة الجمع), where only affirmation leads to negation of the other, and exclusive of neither (مانعة الخلو), where only negation leads to affirmation of the other. The key is that true disjunction is contradictory, so affirmation/negation of one implies the opposite of the other, while the other types have limited implications due to their structure.

Valid Inferences for Each Type of Disjunction
Author enumerates number of valid conclusions for each disjunction type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the number of valid conclusions for each type of disjunctive syllogism and apply this to a ruling in Islamic jurisprudence.

Core Idea
The number of valid conclusions in a disjunction depends on whether the parts are contradictories, contraries, or subcontraries.

In this passage, the author and commentator explain how many valid inferences each type of disjunction produces. True disjunction (exclusive OR) gives four conclusions; exclusive-of-both gives two by affirmation; exclusive-of-neither gives two by negation. Understanding this is crucial for correct reasoning in Islamic law where disjunctions appear, such as classifying an act as either obligatory or forbidden.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three disjunction types
The passage distinguishes three types: true disjunction (manfiṣila ḥaqīqiyya), exclusive-of-both (māniʿa al-jamʿ), and exclusive-of-neither (māniʿa al-khulū). Note their definitions: true disjunction has parts that are true contradictories (cannot both be true or both false); exclusive-of-both cannot both be true but can both be false; exclusive-of-neither cannot both be false but can both be true.
These types correspond to exclusive OR, contraries, and subcontraries in modern logic.
2
Extract the inference patterns for true disjunction
The author gives four valid conclusions: affirming one part negates the other (two ways: affirming first negates second, affirming second negates first), and negating one part affirms the other (two ways). This is because the parts are contradictories.
Use the example: 'Either this number is even or odd' – from 'it is even' we infer 'it is not odd', etc.
3
Extract the inference patterns for exclusive-of-both
Only two valid conclusions: affirming one part negates the other. Negating one part does not affirm the other because both can be false. Example: 'Either this thing is a tree or a stone' – from 'it is a tree' we infer 'it is not a stone', but from 'it is not a tree' we cannot infer 'it is a stone' (it could be something else).
4
Extract the inference patterns for exclusive-of-neither
Only two valid conclusions: negating one part affirms the other. Affirming one part does not negate the other because both can be true. Example: 'This thing is either not a stone or not a tree' – from 'it is a stone' we infer 'it is not a tree', but from 'it is a tree' we cannot infer 'it is not a stone' (it could be both? Actually this example is a bit tricky; the passage uses a double negation structure to get subcontraries. The key: when both can be true, affirming one does not rule out the other.)
The commentator explains that exclusive-of-neither is composed of a thing and something more general than its negation, so both can be true.
5
Apply the patterns to an Islamic ruling example
Consider the disjunction: 'An act is either obligatory (wājib) or forbidden (ḥarām).' This is often a true disjunction (if an act cannot be both obligatory and forbidden, and cannot be neither, but in fiqh there are categories in between). However, if we treat it as exclusive-of-both (cannot be both, but could be neither as mubāḥ), then only affirmation inferences work. Use the patterns to test validity.
This step connects the logical tool to Islamic jurisprudence.

فيكون للمنفصلة الحقيقيَّة أربعُ نتائج : اثنتان باعتبار الوضع، واثنتان باعتبار الرفع، كقولنا : «إما أن يكونَ هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً»، «لكنه زوج فليس بفرد»، «لكنَّه فرد فليس بزوج»، «لكنَّه ليس بزوج فهو فرد»، «لكنه ليس بفرد فهو زوج». ولمنفصلة المانعة الجمع نتيجتان فقط باعتبار الوضع، كَقولنا : «إمَّا أن يكون هذا الشَّيء شجراً ، أو حجراً»، «لكنَّه شجر فهو ليس بحجرٍ»، «لكنَّه حجرٌ فليس بشجر . ولمنفصلة المانعة الخلو نتيجتان أيضاً باعتبارِ الرَّفْعِ، كَقولِنَا : هذا الشَّيءُ إِمَّا ليس بحجرٍ أو ليس بشجرٍ»، «لكنَّه حجر فهو ليس بشجرٍ»، «لكنَّه شجر فهو ليس بحجرٍ». (قَوْلُ الشَّارح فيكون للمنفصلة ... إلخ) السِّرُّ في ذلك أن الحقيقة ما ركبت من الشيء ونقيضه أو المساوي لنقيضه، ومعلوم أن النقيضين لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان، فلذا كان ثبوت أحدهما ينتج رفع الآخر؛ لأنهما لا يجتمعان ورفع أحدهما ينتج ثبوت الآخر لأنهما لا يرتفعان، وأما مانعة الجمع فمركبة من الشيء ونقيضه والنقيضان لا يجتمعان وقد يرتفعان فلذا كان ثبوت أحدهما ينتج رفع الآخر لأنهما لا يجتمعان ورفع أحدهما لا ينتج ثبوت الآخر لجواز رفعهما، وأما مانعة الخلو فتجوز الجمع لتركبها من الشيء والأعم من نقيضه فلذا كان رفع أحدهما ينتج ثبوت الآخر لمنع الخلو عنهما، ولا ينتج ثبوت أحدهما رفع الآخر لجواز الجمع بينهما . . . هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
So for the true disjunction there are four results: two by way of affirmation, and two by way of negation, as in our saying: 'Either this number is even or odd', 'but it is even, so it is not odd', 'but it is odd, so it is not even', 'but it is not even, so it is odd', 'but it is not odd, so it is even'. And for the exclusive of both disjunction, there are only two results by way of affirmation, as in our saying: 'Either this thing is a tree or a stone', 'but it is a tree, so it is not a stone', 'but it is a stone, so it is not a tree'. And for the exclusive of neither disjunction, there are also two results by way of negation, as in our saying: 'This thing is either not a stone or not a tree', 'but it is a stone, so it is not a tree', 'but it is a tree, so it is not a stone'. (The commentator's statement: 'So for the disjunction...' etc.) The secret behind that is that the true disjunction is composed from a thing and its negation or what is equivalent to its negation, and it is known that two negations cannot both be true and cannot both be false. Therefore, the affirmation of one produces the negation of the other because they cannot both be true, and the negation of one produces the affirmation of the other because they cannot both be false. As for the exclusive of both, it is composed from a thing and its negation, and two negations cannot both be true but they can both be false. Therefore, the affirmation of one produces the negation of the other because they cannot both be true, and the negation of one does not produce the affirmation of the other because it is possible for both to be false. As for the exclusive of neither, it permits both to be true because it is composed from a thing and something more general than its negation. Therefore, the negation of one produces the affirmation of the other because it prevents both from being false, and the affirmation of one does not produce the negation of the other because both can be true together. . . . End of al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 854

Commentary
This passage enumerates the valid inferences for each type of disjunction. The author gives concrete examples using numbers and objects. The true disjunction (exclusive OR) yields four valid inferences: affirming one negates the other, and negating one affirms the other. The exclusive of both (مانعة الجمع) yields only two: affirming one negates the other, but negating one does not affirm the other because both can be false. The exclusive of neither (مانعة الخلو) also yields two: negating one affirms the other, but affirming one does not negate the other because both can be true. The commentary by al-Dasuqi explains the logical principle: in a true disjunction, the parts are contradictories (cannot both be true or both false), so both inference directions are valid. In exclusive of both, the parts are contraries (cannot both be true but can both be false), so only affirmation inferences work. In exclusive of neither, the parts are subcontraries (cannot both be false but can both be true), so only negation inferences work. This explains the numbers.

نتيجة (natīja)
المانعة الجمع (al-māni‘a al-jam‘)
المانعة الخلو (al-māni‘a al-khulū)
النقيض (al-naqīḍ)

Key Takeaway
The number of valid conclusions in a disjunctive syllogism is determined by whether the parts are contradictories (4), contraries (2 by affirmation), or subcontraries (2 by negation).

Valid Conclusions from Disjunctive Propositions
Author enumerates results for three types of disjunctive statements with explanatory commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the number and type of valid conclusions (natā'ij) for each of the three types of disjunctive propositions: real (ḥaqīqiyya), exclusive (māni‘a al-jam‘), and exhaustive (māni‘a al-khuluww), and justify why.

Core Idea
The structure of a disjunction determines how many valid inferences are possible: contradictory disjunctions yield four, exclusive yields two by affirmation, exhaustive yields two by negation.

Disjunctive propositions are common in Islamic legal and theological reasoning, from classifying actions as wajib or mubah to proving the temporality of the world. But not every disjunction behaves the same way: the logical relationship between the two parts determines which inferences are sound. In this lesson, we will examine the three types of disjunction and the exact conclusions that follow from affirming or negating one of their parts, as laid out by the author and clarified by al-Sharnūbī's commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disjunction
Look at the two parts: Are they true contradictories (like even/odd) – that is a real disjunction. Do they prevent both being true but allow both to be false (like tree/stone)? That is exclusive. Do they prevent both being false but allow both to be true (like not stone/not tree)? That is exhaustive.
The commentary explains that the composition (thing and its contradictory, or thing with its general) determines the type.
2
Recall the possibilities of combination and cessation
For real: neither combine nor cease (contradictories). For exclusive: do not combine, but can cease. For exhaustive: can combine, but do not cease. From these properties, deduce which inferences are valid.
Al-Sharnūbī's 'secret' (sirr) is exactly this matrix.
3
Apply the inference rules
Real: both affirmation and negation of one disjunct yield a conclusion about the other (4 results). Exclusive: only affirmation yields a negation of the other (2 results, by waḍ‘). Exhaustive: only negation yields an affirmation of the other (2 results, by raf‘).
Check the examples given: even/odd, tree/stone, not stone/not tree.
4
Test with a new example
Construct a disjunction in each type and state the valid conclusions. For instance: 'This act is either wajib or haram' – if there is no third possibility, it is real, so four valid inferences.
Be careful: many everyday disjunctions in fiqh may be ambiguous in type; this tool clarifies the conditions.

فيكون للمنفصلة الحقيقيَّة أربعُ نتائج : اثنتان باعتبار الوضع، واثنتان باعتبار الرفع، كقولنا : «إما أن يكونَ هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً»، «لكنه زوج فليس بفرد»، لكنَّه فرد فليس بزوج»، لكنَّه ليس بزوج فهو فرد»، «لكنه ليس بفرد فهو زوج . وللمنفصلة المانعة الجمع نتيجتان فقط باعتبار الوضع، كَقولنا : «إمَّا أن يكون هذا الشَّيء شجراً ، أو حجراً»، «لكنَّه شجر فهو ليس بحجرٍ»، لكنَّه حجرٌ فليس بشجر . ((وللمنفصلة المانعة الخلو نتيجتان أيضاً باعتبارِ الرَّفْعِ، كَقولِنَا : هذا الشَّيءُ إِمَّا ليس بحجرٍ أو ليس بشجرٍ»، لكنَّه حجر فهو ليس بشجرٍ ((الكنَّه شجر فهو ليس بحجرٍ . الدسوقي العطار (۱) (قَوْلُ الشَّارح فيكون للمنفصلة ... إلخ) السِّرُّ في ذلك أن الحقيقة ما ركبت من الشيء ونقيضه أو المساوي لنقيضه، ومعلوم أن النقيضين لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان، فلذا كان ثبوت أحدهما ينتج رفع الآخر؛ لأنهما لا يجتمعان ورفع أحدهما ينتج ثبوت الآخر لأنهما لا يرتفعان، وأما مانعة الجمع فمركبة من الشيء ونقيضه والنقيضان لا يجتمعان وقد يرتفعان فلذا كان ثبوت أحدهما ينتج رفع الآخر لأنهما لا يجتمعان ورفع أحدهما لا ينتج ثبوت الآخر لجواز رفعهما، وأما مانعة الخلو فتجوز الجمع لتركبها من الشيء والأعم من نقيضه فلذا كان رفع أحدهما ينتج ثبوت الآخر لمنع الخلو عنهما، ولا ينتج ثبوت أحدهما رفع الآخر لجواز الجمع بينهما . . . هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
Thus for the real disjunction there are four results: two by way of affirmation and two by way of negation, as we say: 'Either this number is even or odd', 'But it is even, so it is not odd', 'But it is odd, so it is not even', 'But it is not even, so it is odd', 'But it is not odd, so it is even'. For the exclusive disjunction (māni‘a al-jam‘) there are only two results by way of affirmation, as we say: 'Either this thing is a tree or a stone', 'But it is a tree, so it is not a stone', 'But it is a stone, so it is not a tree'. For the exhaustive disjunction (māni‘a al-khuluww) there are also two results by way of negation, as we say: 'This thing is either not a stone or not a tree', 'But it is a stone, so it is not a tree', 'But it is a tree, so it is not a stone'. Al-Dasūqī, al-‘Aṭṭār: (The commentator's statement: 'Thus for the disjunction ... etc.') The reason for this is that the real disjunction is composed of a thing and its contradictory or something equal to its contradictory, and it is known that contradictories neither combine nor cease, so affirming one necessitates negating the other because they do not combine, and negating one necessitates affirming the other because they do not cease. As for the exclusive disjunction, it is composed of a thing and its contradictory, and contradictories do not combine but may cease, so affirming one necessitates negating the other because they do not combine, but negating one does not necessitate affirming the other because it is possible for both to cease. As for the exhaustive disjunction, it allows combination because it is composed of a thing and something more general than its contradictory, so negating one necessitates affirming the other to prevent their ceasing, but affirming one does not necessitate negating the other because combination is possible. End quote from al-Sharnūbī.
Source pages p. 854

Commentary
This passage explains the number of valid conclusions (natā'ij) that follow from the three types of disjunctive statements in logic. The author first presents the results for each type with concrete examples, then al-Sharnūbī's commentary reveals the underlying logical principle. For the real disjunction (al-munfaṣila al-ḥaqīqiyya), which is composed of a term and its exact contradictory, both affirmation and negation of one term yield a conclusion: affirming one negates the other, and negating one affirms the other, resulting in four valid inferences. The example uses numbers being even or odd—these are true contradictories. For the exclusive disjunction (māni‘a al-jam‘), which prevents combination but allows both to be false, only affirmation of one term yields a conclusion (negation of the other), because negating one term does not guarantee the other due to possible 'double negation.' Thus only two results, both by way of affirmation (waḍ‘). For the exhaustive disjunction (māni‘a al-khuluww), which prevents both being false but allows both to be true, only negation of one term yields a conclusion (affirmation of the other), because affirming one term does not negate the other if they can coexist. Thus two results by way of negation (raf‘). The commentary by al-Sharnūbī clarifies the 'secret' (sirr) behind these rules: the composition of each disjunction (from a thing and its contradictory or its more general) determines whether the two extremes can combine or cease, which in turn dictates the valid inferences.

مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو

The Syllogism

Definition and Nature of Qiyās al-Khalf
Definition of qiyās al-khalf and nomenclature discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qiyās al-khalf, explain its logical mechanism, and identify the reasons for its name.

Core Idea
Qiyās al-khalf proves a claim by showing its contradictory leads to impossibility.

Having covered conjunctive and exceptional syllogisms, the text now introduces a special type of exceptional syllogism called qiyās al-khalf (indirect proof). This method is widely used in kalām and uṣūl al-fiqh to establish theological and legal conclusions. We will examine its definition, how it works, and why it is called 'khalf'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
Find the term being defined (qiyās al-khalf) and the phrase that defines it. The definition states it is a syllogism intended to prove the desired conclusion by invalidating its contradictory.
The definiens is 'what is intended to prove the desired conclusion by invalidating its contradictory'.
2
Understand the logical mechanism
The method assumes the negation of the desired conclusion, then derives an impossibility (muḥāl) from that assumption, thereby proving the original statement true. This is a proof by contradiction.
This is a two-step process: assume not-P, derive a contradiction, therefore P.
3
Compare with direct syllogism (al-qiyās al-mustaqīm)
A direct syllogism proves a conclusion straightforwardly without assuming its opposite. Qiyās al-khalf works 'from behind' by attacking the contradictory. Understand the contrast.
Direct syllogism is called mustaqīm (straight) because it goes from premises to conclusion directly.
4
Analyze the reasons for the name 'khalf'
The passage gives multiple reasons: (1) it leads to an impossibility (khalf meaning false/impossible), (2) it approaches the conclusion from behind (khalf meaning behind), (3) it deals with falsity. Identify which explanation the author supports.
Al-‘Iṣām clarifies that the syllogism itself is not false, but it produces falsity in the contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ الِاسْتِثْنَائِي [ قياسُ الخُلْفِ]: ۸۵۵ ولَما فرغ من تعريف القياس الاقتراني والاستثنائي؛ شرع في قياس الخُلْفِ المركَبِ من الاستثنائي والاقتراني، فقال: (وَقَدْ يُخَصُّ بِاسْمِ قِيَاسِ الخُلْفَ مَا يُقْصَدُ بِهِ إِثْبَاتُ الْمَطْلُوبِ بِإِبْطَالِ نَقِيضِهِ)؛ أي: القياسُ الَّذي يقصد به إثبات المطلوبِ بسبب إبطال نقيضه، مخصوص باسم قياس الخُلْف، (وَمَرْجِعُهُ)؛ أي: حاصل الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ: بِاسْمِ قِيَاسِ الْخُلْفِ إِنَّما سُمِّيَ خُلْفاً؛ لأنَّه يؤدّي إلى الخلف؛ أي: المحال على تقدير عدم حقّيَّةِ المطلوب، أو لأنَّه يأتي المطلوب من خلفه؛ أي: من ورائه؛ إذ المطلوب نقيضُ النتيجة . (قَوْلُهُ : ( بِإِبْطَالِ) مُتعلِّق بإثبات، وباؤه سببيَّة العطار (قَوْلُهُ : وَقَدْ يُخَصُّ بِاسْمِ قِيَاسِ الْخُلْفِ يعني من أقسام الاستثنائي: قياس الخلف، وهوَ القياسُ الَّذي يقصدُ فيهِ إثباتُ المطلوب بإبطال نقيضهِ، وَيُقابله القياسُ المستقيمُ ، وإِنَّما سُمِّي خلفاً ؛ لأنَّهُ يثبتُ المطلوب مِن خَلْفِهِ؛ أي: ورائِهِ؛ حيثُ يثبتُهُ مِن جانبِ نقيضِهِ، كَمَا أَنَّ مُقابِلَهُ يُسمَّى مُستقيماً؛ لأنَّه يثبتُ المطلوب من قُدَّامِهِ على وجه الاستقامةِ، وَقِيلَ : سُمِّي خلفاً ؛ أي: باطلاً لاشتماله على بيان كونِ النَّقيض باطلاً ؛ قالَهُ بعضُ الشَّارحينَ، وقال العصامُ : سُمِّيَ خلفاً لا لأنَّهُ باطل بنفسه، بل لأنَّه ينتج الباطل، أو لأنَّهُ يتمسَّكُ فيه بملاحظة الباطل واعتباره، وَسُمِّيَ ما يقابله القياس المستقيم، ولهذا قيل : الظَّاهِرُ أنَّهُ سُمِّي خلفاً؛ لأنَّهُ لَا يأتي سالكة المطلوب من قُدَّامِهِ، بَلْ مِنْ خَلْفِهِ، حيث يتمسَّكُ فيه بِنَقيضِهِ الَّذي هو الخلفُ بالنّسبة إلى القُدَّامِ، ويؤيّدُ كلامَهُمَا كلامُ المصنفِ في شَرحِ الأصل حيثُ قال : خلفاً ؛ لأنَّهُ يَؤدّي إلى الخلف أي : المحال على تقدير عدم حقّيَّةِ المطلوب، سُمِّيَ وقِيلَ : لأنَّه يأتي المطلوب مِنْ خلفِهِ ؛ أي من ورائِهِ الَّذي هو نقيضُه . (قَوْلُهُ : وَقَدْ يُخَصُ بِاسْمِ) الباء داخلة على المقصور.
Chapter: On the Exceptional Syllogism [Syllogism of Indirect Proof] 855. After finishing the definition of the conjunctive and exceptional syllogisms, he began on the syllogism of indirect proof (al-khalf), which is composed of the exceptional and conjunctive. He said: (And the name 'syllogism of indirect proof' may be specifically applied to that by which one intends to prove the desired conclusion by invalidating its contradictory). That is: the syllogism by which one intends to prove the desired conclusion by invalidating its contradictory is specially called the syllogism of indirect proof. (And its return) [al-Dasūqī: His statement: 'by the name of syllogism of indirect proof' — it is only called khalf because it leads to khalf, i.e., an impossibility on the assumption that the desired conclusion is not true, or because it comes to the desired conclusion from behind it, i.e., from its rear, since the desired conclusion is the contradictory of the result.] [al-‘Aṭṭār: His statement: 'And the name ... etc.' means that among the types of the exceptional syllogism is the khalf syllogism, which is the syllogism in which one intends to prove the desired conclusion by invalidating its contradictory. Its opposite is the direct syllogism. It is called khalf because it proves the desired conclusion from behind it, i.e., from its rear, proving it from the side of its contradictory, just as its opposite is called direct because it proves the desired conclusion from its front in a straightforward manner. And it was said: it is called khalf, i.e., false, because it involves showing the contradictory to be false. Some commentators said that. Al-‘Iṣām said: It is called khalf not because it is false in itself, but because it produces falsity, or because one adheres to considering falsity. Its opposite is called the direct syllogism. Hence it was said: Apparently it is called khalf because it does not reach the desired conclusion from its front, but from its rear, adhering to its contradictory which is the 'behind' relative to the front. The author's statement in the commentary on the original supports both: he said: 'khalf because it leads to khalf, i.e., impossibility on the assumption that the desired conclusion is not true.' It was also said: because it comes to the desired conclusion from behind it, i.e., from its rear, which is its contradictory.]
Source pages p. 855

Commentary
This passage introduces a special type of syllogism called qiyās al-khalf (syllogism of indirect proof or reductio ad absurdum). It is classified under the exceptional syllogism (al-qiyās al-istithnā'ī) but also incorporates a conjunctive (iqtirānī) element. The core definition is given: it is a syllogism intended to prove a desired conclusion (al-maṭlūb) by invalidating its contradictory (naqīḍ). The method works by assuming the negation of what you want to prove, then deriving an impossibility (muḥāl) from that assumption, which shows the assumption to be false and thus the original is true. The commentary by al-Dasūqī and al-‘Aṭṭār discusses why it is called 'khalf' (lit. 'behind' or 'contrary'). Several reasons are given: (1) because it leads to an impossibility (khalf meaning 'impossible') on the assumption that the desired conclusion is false; (2) because it comes at the desired conclusion 'from behind' by working through its contradictory, as opposed to a direct syllogism that proceeds straightforwardly; (3) because it deals with falsity (bāṭil). The passage also clarifies that 'khalf' does not mean the syllogism itself is false, but that it proves by exposing the falsity of the contradictory. The direct syllogism is called mustaqīm (straightforward). This method is common in kalām and uṣūl al-fiqh, e.g., proving the temporality of the world by assuming its eternity and showing that leads to contradictions.

القياس المستقيم
إبطال النقيض

What is Qiyas al-Khalf? Proof by Contradiction
Author defines qiyas al-khalf as proof by contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify qiyas al-khalf and explain how it proves a conclusion by invalidating its opposite.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-khalf proves truth by showing the opposite leads to absurdity.

This passage defines a special syllogism unique to Islamic logic: qiyas al-khalf, or proof by contradiction. Unlike ordinary syllogisms that directly deduce a conclusion, this method works by assuming the negation of the desired statement and deriving an impossibility. The author explains that this type of argument combines two basic syllogistic forms. Let's break down what qiyas al-khalf is and why it is a powerful tool in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined. The passage says 'wa qad yukhasṣu bi-ismi qiyās al-khalf' — it is specifically called proof by contradiction. Underline that this is a type of conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i).
The term 'khalf' means contradiction or absurdity.
2
Understand the purpose
The purpose is 'ithbāt al-maṭlūb bi-ibṭāl naqīḍihi' — proving the desired conclusion by invalidating its opposite. Notice that the method does not prove directly; it disproves the contradictory.
This is the essence of proof by contradiction.
3
Recognize the compound nature
The author says 'marjiʿuhu ilā qiyās istithnāʾiyyin wa-iftirāniyyin' — its basis goes back to a conditional and a categorical syllogism. This means qiyas al-khalf is not a simple inference but a chain of reasoning.
You will see this compound structure in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ الِاسْتِثْنَائِي. وَقَدْ يُخَصُّ بِاسْمِ قِيَاسِ الخُلْفِ مَا يُقْصَدُ بِهِ إِثْبَاتُ الْمَطْلُوبِ بِإِبْطَالِ نَقِيضِهِ. وَمَرْجِعُهُ إِلَى قِيَاسٍ اسْتِثْنَائِيٍّ وَافْتِرَانِيٍّ. كَمَا إِذَا قُلْنَا مَثَلاً: إِذَا صَدَقَ كُلُّ ج ب بِالْفِعْلِ؛ وَجَبَ أَنْ يَصْدُقَ فِي عَكْسِهِ: بَعْضُ ب ج بِالْفِعْلِ، فَهَذَا مَطْلُوبُنَا.
Section: On conditional syllogism. And it may be specifically called proof by contradiction (qiyas al-khalf) that which is intended to prove the desired conclusion by invalidating its opposite. And its basis goes back to a conditional syllogism and a categorical syllogism. For example, when we say: If 'every J is B' is actually true, then its converse 'some B is J' must actually be true, and this is our desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 855

Commentary
This passage defines a special type of syllogism called qiyas al-khalf (proof by contradiction). The author states that it is a syllogism aimed at proving a proposition (the desired conclusion) by invalidating its opposite (its negation). The term 'khalf' means contradiction or absurdity, because this method shows that assuming the opposite leads to an impossible consequence. The author then explains that this type of syllogism combines two basic forms: a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) and a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani). An example is given: to prove that 'some B is J' (the converse) is true given that 'every J is B' is true, one would set up a conditional. The reasoning is: if the converse were false, then its negation (no B is J) would be true, which together with the original premise (every J is B) leads to an absurdity (e.g., some J is not J). Thus, the converse must be true. This example shows the application of qiyas al-khalf in establishing the validity of conversion per accidens.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
The Compound Structure of Qiyas al-Khalf
Author explains compound structure with conversion example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to decompose a qiyas al-khalf into its conditional and categorical components and verify its validity.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-khalf is a conditional syllogism containing an embedded categorical syllogism.

The previous lesson introduced the definition of qiyas al-khalf. Now we examine its inner structure: it is not a single step but a compound of two syllogisms. The author says its basis goes back to a conditional (istithna'i) and a categorical (iqtirani). In this lesson, we will dissect the example given to see how these two forms work together to produce a valid proof by contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired conclusion (matlub)
In the example, the conclusion is 'some B is J' (the converse of 'every J is B'). Recognize that the proof aims to show this must be true given the premise.
The conclusion is the converse per accidens.
2
Set up the conditional (istithna'i) structure
The qiyas al-khalf begins with a conditional: 'If the opposite of the conclusion were true, then an absurdity follows.' In the example, the opposite of 'some B is J' is 'no B is J'. The conditional premise is: if 'no B is J' (together with the original premise 'every J is B') then something impossible results (e.g., some J is not J).
The conditional connects the assumption of the opposite to the absurd consequence.
3
Identify the embedded categorical (iqtirani) syllogism
The derivation of the absurdity itself is a categorical syllogism. The passage does not fully spell it out but implies it. In full: major premise: 'every J is B' (given); minor: 'no B is J' (assumed); conclusion: 'no J is J' (i.e., some J is not J) — a contradiction. This categorical syllogism is in the second figure (Camestres mood) and is valid.
The categorical part proves the absurdity (khalf).
4
Apply the conditional rule (modus tollens)
Since the conditional antecedent (assumption of the opposite) leads to a false consequent (the absurdity), the antecedent is false. Therefore, the opposite of the conclusion is false, which means the original conclusion ('some B is J') is true. This is an application of modus tollens: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.
The conditional conclusion (not P) is the desired result.
5
Verify overall validity
Check both syllogisms: the categorical must be valid and the conditional inference (modus tollens) must be correctly applied. In the example, both are valid. Thus the qiyas al-khalf is sound.
Any flaw in either component invalidates the whole.

فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ الِاسْتِثْنَائِي. وَقَدْ يُخَصُّ بِاسْمِ قِيَاسِ الخُلْفِ مَا يُقْصَدُ بِهِ إِثْبَاتُ الْمَطْلُوبِ بِإِبْطَالِ نَقِيضِهِ. وَمَرْجِعُهُ إِلَى قِيَاسٍ اسْتِثْنَائِيٍّ وَافْتِرَانِيٍّ. كَمَا إِذَا قُلْنَا مَثَلاً: إِذَا صَدَقَ كُلُّ ج ب بِالْفِعْلِ؛ وَجَبَ أَنْ يَصْدُقَ فِي عَكْسِهِ: بَعْضُ ب ج بِالْفِعْلِ، فَهَذَا مَطْلُوبُنَا.
Section: On conditional syllogism. And it may be specifically called proof by contradiction (qiyas al-khalf) that which is intended to prove the desired conclusion by invalidating its opposite. And its basis goes back to a conditional syllogism and a categorical syllogism. For example, when we say: If 'every J is B' is actually true, then its converse 'some B is J' must actually be true, and this is our desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 855

Commentary
This passage defines a special type of syllogism called qiyas al-khalf (proof by contradiction). The author states that it is a syllogism aimed at proving a proposition (the desired conclusion) by invalidating its opposite (its negation). The term 'khalf' means contradiction or absurdity, because this method shows that assuming the opposite leads to an impossible consequence. The author then explains that this type of syllogism combines two basic forms: a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) and a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani). An example is given: to prove that 'some B is J' (the converse) is true given that 'every J is B' is true, one would set up a conditional. The reasoning is: if the converse were false, then its negation (no B is J) would be true, which together with the original premise (every J is B) leads to an absurdity (e.g., some J is not J). Thus, the converse must be true. This example shows the application of qiyas al-khalf in establishing the validity of conversion per accidens.

استثنائي
اقتراني

B) Every J is B; no B is J; therefore no J is J.
C) Every B is J; no J is B; therefore no B is B.
D) Some J is B; no B is J; therefore no J is B.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-khalf is a compound syllogism where a conditional inference (modus tollens) is applied to the result of an embedded categorical syllogism that demonstrates an absurdity.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Qiyas al-Khalf (Proof by Contradiction) Explained
Explanation of qiyas al-khalf for conditional syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a proof by contradiction (qiyas al-khalf) for a conditional syllogism and identify its compound structure (categorical + conditional).

Core Idea
Proof by contradiction proves a proposition by showing its negation leads to an absurdity.

Proof by contradiction (qiyas al-khalf) is a powerful logical tool used to establish propositions indirectly. Instead of proving a claim directly, you assume its opposite and derive an impossibility, thereby confirming the original claim. In this lesson, we will unpack the method as explained by classical logicians, using the example of a conditional syllogism. You will learn to identify the original premise, the desired proposition, and the contradictory, and see how a categorical syllogism within the proof generates a self-contradiction that seals the argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components
Determine the original premise (asl) and the desired proposition (matlab). In the text, the original is 'Every J is B' (a universal affirmative). The desired is the conclusion you want to prove, whose contradictory is a universal negative: 'Nothing of B is J'.
The original premise is taken as given; the desired is the target of the proof.
2
Formulate the supposition
Assume that the desired proposition is false together with the original. That is, suppose the contradictory of the desired (naqid al-matlab) is true alongside the original. In the text, this means asserting 'Nothing of B is J' as true while keeping 'Every J is B'.
The supposition is the negation of what we want to prove.
3
Construct the categorical syllogism
Combine the original as minor premise and the contradictory as major premise to form a categorical syllogism. Minor: 'Every J is B' (original). Major: 'Nothing of B is J' (contradictory). This yields the impossible conclusion: 'Nothing of J is J' — a self-contradiction (a thing is not itself).
This syllogism is valid in the first figure (Celarent). The middle term is B.
4
Derive the conditional and conclude
The impossible conclusion is the consequent of a conditional: 'If the desired is not true with the original, then impossibility (self-contradiction) is true.' Then we add the minor premise: 'But impossibility is false (impossible cannot be true).' By modus tollens, the antecedent is false, meaning the desired is true with the original. This establishes the proof.
The overall argument is a compound: a categorical syllogism feeding into a conditional syllogism (modus tollens).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قَوْلُهُ : يَنْتُجُ : لَوْ لَمْ يَصْدُقُ ... إلخ) هذه نتيجة الاقتراني، فتجعل كُبرى للقياس الاستثنائي، ويُؤتى بعدها بِصُغراه، وهي : لكن كذا كَمَا فعلَ الشَّارِحُ . العطار
ذلك، وقد وقع اختلاف عظيمٌ فيه، والذي استقر عليه رأي الشَّيخِ أَنَّهُ مُركَّبٌ مِن اقتراني واستثنائي .
ثم: لكن التّالي باطل، فالمقدَّم مثله، وإذا بطل صِدْقُ نقيض المطلوب مع الأصلِ، ثَبَتَ صِدْقُ المطلوب مع الأصل، فهذا إثبات المطلوب بإبطال نقيضه.
Chapter: On Conditional Syllogism. Its proof is established by the syllogism of reduction (qiyas al-khalf), as follows: 'If our desired proposition is not true together with the original premise, then the contradictory of the desired proposition is true together with the original premise'; that is: 'Nothing of B is J always'. And whenever its contradictory is true with the original premise, then 'Nothing of J is J always' is true. So this is a categorical syllogism composed of two conditionals, producing: 'If our desired proposition is not true with the original premise, then Nothing of J is J always is true.' Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'with the original premise') that is: 'Every J is B'; so the original is made the minor premise and the contradictory of the desired the major premise. (His statement: 'the contradictory of the desired') and its contradictory is a universal permanent absolute negative (his statement: 'that is: nothing of B is J' i.e., of animal with man, and this is an explanation of the contradictory. (His statement: 'Nothing of J is J is true') i.e., of man with man, and it is better to say: due to the realization of impossibility, and it is true by negating a thing from itself resulting from combining it with the original as major, or by the conjunction of two contradictories. (His statement: 'produces: if it is not true... etc.') This is the result of the categorical, so it is made the major premise of the conditional syllogism, and then its minor premise is brought, which is: 'But it is such' as the commentator did. (His statement: 'if it is not true... etc.') Another's expression: if the desired is not realized, its contradictory is realized, and if this contradictory is realized, then impossibility is realized, which is the falsehood of what was assumed true, so it results: if the desired is not realized, then impossibility is realized; then the conditional syllogism is appended to it, which is: 'But impossibility is not realized, so the contradictory of the desired is not realized, so the desired is realized.' (His statement: 'with the original premise') that is: so the original is made the minor premise and the contradictory of the desired the major, producing what was mentioned by his statement: 'Nothing of J is J.' Al-Attar: That, and a great disagreement occurred about it, and what the Shaykh's opinion settled upon is that it is composed of a categorical and a conditional. Then: 'But the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise.' And if the truth of the contradictory of the desired with the original is invalidated, the truth of the desired with the original is established. So this is the proof of the desired by nullifying its contradictory.
Source pages p. 857

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the method of proof by contradiction (qiyas al-khalf) used to establish conditional syllogisms. The author begins by stating that conditional syllogisms can be proven through reductio. The method: suppose the desired proposition (the conclusion we want) is not true together with the original premise (the given assumption). Then, under that supposition, the contradictory of the desired becomes true with the original. The original premise here is 'Every J is B' (a universal affirmative). The contradictory is a universal negative: 'Nothing of B is J'. From these two (original as minor, contradictory as major), a categorical syllogism in the first figure yields 'Nothing of J is J always' – a self-contradictory impossible statement (since it says a thing is not itself). This impossibility shows that the supposition (that the desired is false) is impossible, so the desired must be true. The commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies each term: 'original' means the given premise; 'contradictory of the desired' is a universal permanent negation; the result shows impossibility either by self-negation or conjunction of contradictories. The categorical syllogism's conclusion becomes the major premise of a conditional syllogism, whose minor is 'but the consequent is false' (i.e., impossibility is not realized), leading to the denial of the antecedent (the desired's falsity), thus proving the desired. Al-Attar notes a significant disagreement about the nature of this proof, but the Shaykh's settled view is that it is composed of both a categorical and a conditional syllogism. The passage concludes that by falsifying the contradictory of the desired with the original, the desired itself is proven true. This method is fundamental in Islamic logic for indirect proof, especially in kalam arguments for the existence of God and in fiqh reasoning.

قياس الخلف
الأصل
نقيض المطلوب

Key Takeaway
Proof by contradiction (qiyas al-khalf) establishes a proposition by showing that its negation leads to an impossible self-contradiction.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Proof by Contradiction in Conditional Syllogisms
Author describes proof by contradiction; commentators explain terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a proof by contradiction using a conditional syllogism and identify the antecedent and consequent in Islamic theological arguments.

Core Idea
Proof by contradiction establishes a proposition by showing its opposite leads to a false consequent.

This lesson unpacks a classical logical method: proving a desired proposition by falsifying its contradictory. The author shows how, given an original premise, assuming the contradictory of the desired proposition leads to a false consequent, thereby establishing the desired proposition. The commentators al-Dasuqi and al-Attar clarify the technical terms used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired proposition (al-maṭlūb)
Find the proposition you want to prove. In the passage, it is the proposition to be established together with the original premise.
The 'original' is an accepted truth that both sides agree upon.
2
Assume its contradictory (naqīḍ al-maṭlūb)
Temporarily assume the opposite of the desired proposition, combined with the original. This becomes the antecedent of the conditional.
This assumption is the 'muqaddam' (antecedent) in the exceptional syllogism.
3
Derive a consequent (tālī) from the assumption
Using the original premise and the contradictory assumption, validly infer a conclusion. This conclusion becomes the consequent of the conditional syllogism.
The consequent is the 'tālī' and is the result of the syllogism.
4
Show the consequent is false
Demonstrate that the derived consequent contradicts known truths or leads to an absurdity. The passage states 'the consequent is false.'
Falsity can be from scripture, consensus, or self-evident reason.
5
Conclude the antecedent is false, thus the desired proposition true
In a valid conditional, if the consequent is false, the antecedent must be false. Therefore, the contradictory assumption is false, so the desired proposition is true.
This is modus tollens: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن التّالي باطل، فالمقدَّم مثله، وإذا بطل صِدْقُ نقيض المطلوب مع الأصلِ، ثَبَتَ صِدْقُ المطلوب مع الأصل، فهذا إثبات المطلوب بإبطال نقيضه . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : لَكِن التَّالِي بَاطِلٌ أَيْ : التّالي من النتيجة؛ لأنَّ النَّتيجةَ جُعِلَتْ كُبرى للقياس الاستثنائي، وإنْ لمْ يُعِدها الشَّارحُ فيه، فكانَ الأولى لِلشَّارحِ أَنْ يُعيدها فيه (قَوْلُهُ : فَالْمُقَدَّمُ مِثْلُهُ المقدَّمُ هو عدمُ صدقِ مَطلوبِنَا معَ الأصل. العطار
But the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. And when the truth of the contradictory of the desired proposition with the original is falsified, the truth of the desired proposition with the original is established. So this is establishing the desired proposition by disproving its opposite. al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'But the consequent is false') i.e., the consequent from the conclusion; because the conclusion was made the major premise in the exceptional syllogism, even if the commentator did not repeat it therein, so it would have been better for the commentator to repeat it therein. (His statement: 'So the antecedent is likewise false') the antecedent is the non-truth of our desired proposition with the original. al-Attar.
Source pages p. 858

Commentary
This passage explains the logical method of proving a proposition by contradiction. The main text describes the process: we have a desired proposition together with an original premise. To prove the desired proposition true when combined with the original, we assume its contradictory. If that assumption yields a false consequent in a conditional syllogism, then the antecedent (the assumption) is also false, thereby establishing the desired proposition. The commentators clarify terminology: al-Dasuqi notes that the 'consequent' refers to the conclusion of the syllogism, which is made the major premise in an exceptional (conditional) syllogism. The 'antecedent' is the non-truth of our desired proposition with the original. Al-Attar's remark is brief.

Materials and Form of Syllogisms & Definition of Burhani
Author introduces materials and defines burhani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the form and material of a syllogism, and identify the defining characteristic of a demonstrative (burhani) syllogism.

Core Idea
A syllogism's material is the certainty of its premises; its form is the arrangement.

In this passage, the author shifts from discussing the forms of syllogisms to their materials. He defines the demonstrative syllogism as one composed of certain premises. We will explore what 'materials' means and why they matter for avoiding error.

Guided Path
1
Locate the shift in focus
Notice the phrase 'لما فرغ من صور الأقيسة؛ شرع في موادها' – this indicates a transition from form to material.
This transition shows that both form and material are essential for a complete understanding of syllogisms.
2
Identify what 'materials' means
The commentary states that materials are 'القضايا التي تتركب منها' – the propositions that compose the syllogisms. This is distinct from the form (mood and figure).
3
Extract the definition of burhani
The matn says: 'ما يتألف من اليقينيات' – a demonstrative syllogism is composed of certainties.
This definition sets burhani apart from other types.
4
Understand the definition of certainty
Certainty (اليقين) is defined as 'اعتقاد الشيء بأنه كذا'. al-Dasuqi glosses it as 'مطلق الإدراك' (absolute perception).
This broadens the concept to include perception beyond mere belief.
5
Recognise the importance of material
Error can arise from the material even if the form is correct. Knowing the epistemic status of premises is crucial for sound reasoning.
The five arts are distinguished precisely by the material.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في مواد الأقيسة ولما فرغ من صور الأقيسة؛ شرع في موادها، فقال: القياس البرهاني] القياس إما برهاني، وهو: ما يتألف من اليقينيات. واليقين: اعتقاد الشيء بأنه كذا، الدسوقي (قوله: واليقين) المراد به مطلق الإدراك . العطار فصل: في مواد الأقبسة (قوله: شرع في موادها وهي القضايا التي تتركب منها؛ لكونها لا بد من معرفتها حتى لا يتطرق الغلط من جهة المادة، وتتميز الصناعات الخمس بعضها عن بعض، فالنظر ههنا في القضايا من حيث ذاتها، مع قطع النظر عن تركيبها بهيئة مخصوصة، والبحث عن اشتراط الشرائط في الصغرى والكبرى بحسب الكمية والكيفية والجهة؛ ليس نظراً في مواد الأقيسة؛ لكونها مختصة بهيئة مخصوصة. (قوله: القياس إما برهاني ... إلخ) بيان للصناعات الخمس، وهي أقسام للدليل باعتبار مادته، كما أن الأقسام السابقة أقسام له باعتبار صورته، وهي البرهان والجدل والخطابة والمغالطة والشعر، ووجه الحصر أن القياس يفيد إما تصديقاً أو تأثراً؛ أعني: التخييل، والتصديق إما جازم أو غير جازم، والجازم إما أن تعتبر حقيته أو لا، والمعتبر حقيته إما أن يكون حقا في الواقع أو لا، فالمفيد للتصديق الجازم الحق؛ هو البرهان، وللتصديق الجازم غير الحق؛ هو السفسطة، والتصديق الجازم الذي لا يعتبر فيه كونه حقا أو غير حق، بل يعتبر فيه عموم الاعتراف؛ هو الجدل إن تحقق عموم الاعتراف، وإلا؛ فهو الشعب، وهو مع
Chapter: On the materials of syllogisms. When he finished with the forms of syllogisms, he began with their materials, saying: The syllogism is either demonstrative (burhani), and it is what is composed of certainties (yaqiniyyat). And certainty (yaqin) is: belief in something as being such. al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'and certainty'—what is meant by it is absolute perception. al-Attar: Chapter: On the materials of syllogisms. His statement: 'he began with their materials'—they are the propositions of which they are composed, because it is necessary to know them so that error does not creep in from the aspect of the material, and the five arts are distinguished from each other. So the consideration here is of propositions in themselves, without regard to their composition in a specific form. And the investigation into the stipulation of conditions in the minor and major premises with respect to quantity, quality, and modality is not a consideration of the materials of syllogisms, because they are specific to a particular form. His statement: 'The syllogism is either demonstrative... etc.' is an explanation of the five arts, which are divisions of the proof (dalil) according to its material, just as the previous divisions were divisions of it according to its form. They are: demonstration (burhan), dialectic (jadal), rhetoric (khataba), sophistry (mushalata), and poetry (shi'r). The justification for the enumeration is that the syllogism yields either assent (tasdiq) or impression (ta'aththur), i.e., imagination (takhayyul). Assent is either certain (jazim) or non-certain. The certain is either considered with regard to its truth or not. What is considered with regard to its truth is either true in reality or not. What yields certain true assent is demonstration. What yields certain false assent is sophistry. The certain assent in which it is not considered whether it is true or false, but rather general acceptance is considered, is dialectic if general acceptance is realized, otherwise it is trickery (shu'b), and it is with...
Source pages p. 868

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on the 'Materials of Syllogisms' chapter serves both as a definitional and classificatory section. It begins by explaining the shift from the previous discussion of the forms of syllogisms (صور الأقيسة) to their materials (موادها). The commentator clarifies that 'materials' refers to the propositions (قضايا) that compose the syllogisms, and this distinction is crucial for avoiding error that could arise from the content of the premises, not just their form. The five arts (الصناعات الخمس) are then introduced as divisions of proofs based on their material, as opposed to the earlier divisions based on form. The text first defines the demonstrative syllogism (برهاني) as that composed of certainties (يقينيات), and defines certainty (يقين) as a firm belief. A marginal note from al-Dasuqi expands this definition, stating that 'yer' here means absolute perception (مطلق الإدراك). Then, the commentary from al-Attar explains the rationale behind the five arts: syllogisms either produce assent (تصديق) or imaginative impression (تخييل). Assent is either certain or not; certain assent is either considered with regard to its truth or not; if truth is considered, it either corresponds to reality (demonstration) or not (sophistry); if general acceptance is considered, it is dialectic if widespread, otherwise it is trickery (شعب). This classification underscores that the material of the premises determines the art, not the syllogistic form alone. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the logical structure is clear.

Key Terms
مواد الأقيسة
برهاني
اليقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the term 'materials' (مواد) refer to in the context of syllogisms?
A) The subject matter of the conclusion
B) The propositions that compose the syllogism
C) The mood and figure
D) The terms (hudud)

Key Takeaway
The materials of a syllogism are its premises, and a demonstrative syllogism must be composed of certain premises.

Is the material certain? Which art does it belong to?

The Syllogism

The Five Arts Classification
Author classifies the five arts by material.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a syllogism into one of the five arts based on the epistemic status of its premises and the type of assent it produces.

Core Idea
The five arts are distinguished by the material of the syllogism, not its form.

The passage then explains the five arts (الصناعات الخمس) as divisions of proof according to material. We will trace the logical classification that leads from assent vs. imagination to the five arts.

Guided Path
1
Note the basis of classification
The five arts are divisions of the proof (دليل) by material, just as previous divisions were by form.
Material here means the type of premises.
2
First split: assent or imagination
The syllogism yields either assent (تصديق) or impression (تأثر), i.e., imagination (تخييل). Poetry falls under imagination.
This is the root of the classification tree.
3
If assent, is it certain?
Assent is either certain (جازم) or non-certain. Non-certain assent leads to rhetoric (خطابة) – but the passage doesn't elaborate; the classification continues only for certain assent.
Certainty is key.
4
For certain assent: truth considered or not?
If truth is considered, it is either true in reality (برهان) or false (سفسطة). If truth is not considered but general acceptance is, it is dialectic (جدل) if widely accepted, otherwise trickery (شعب).
The material of premises determines which art: certain premises for burhan, accepted for jadal, etc.
5
Apply the classification to an example
Take the syllogism: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' If the premises are certain (e.g., from rational necessity), it is demonstrative. If the major premise is merely accepted by common sense, it is dialectical.
This shows how the same form can belong to different arts based on material.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في مواد الأقيسة ولما فرغ من صور الأقيسة؛ شرع في موادها، فقال: القياس البرهاني] القياس إما برهاني، وهو: ما يتألف من اليقينيات. واليقين: اعتقاد الشيء بأنه كذا، الدسوقي (قوله: واليقين) المراد به مطلق الإدراك . العطار فصل: في مواد الأقبسة (قوله: شرع في موادها وهي القضايا التي تتركب منها؛ لكونها لا بد من معرفتها حتى لا يتطرق الغلط من جهة المادة، وتتميز الصناعات الخمس بعضها عن بعض، فالنظر ههنا في القضايا من حيث ذاتها، مع قطع النظر عن تركيبها بهيئة مخصوصة، والبحث عن اشتراط الشرائط في الصغرى والكبرى بحسب الكمية والكيفية والجهة؛ ليس نظراً في مواد الأقيسة؛ لكونها مختصة بهيئة مخصوصة. (قوله: القياس إما برهاني ... إلخ) بيان للصناعات الخمس، وهي أقسام للدليل باعتبار مادته، كما أن الأقسام السابقة أقسام له باعتبار صورته، وهي البرهان والجدل والخطابة والمغالطة والشعر، ووجه الحصر أن القياس يفيد إما تصديقاً أو تأثراً؛ أعني: التخييل، والتصديق إما جازم أو غير جازم، والجازم إما أن تعتبر حقيته أو لا، والمعتبر حقيته إما أن يكون حقا في الواقع أو لا، فالمفيد للتصديق الجازم الحق؛ هو البرهان، وللتصديق الجازم غير الحق؛ هو السفسطة، والتصديق الجازم الذي لا يعتبر فيه كونه حقا أو غير حق، بل يعتبر فيه عموم الاعتراف؛ هو الجدل إن تحقق عموم الاعتراف، وإلا؛ فهو الشعب، وهو مع

Refuting Infinite Regress in Demonstration
Commentator refutes the claim that demonstration requires an infinite regress of premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why infinite regress does not invalidate demonstration and identify the commentator's key point.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is avoided by self-evident premises.

Some argue that demonstration requires an infinite chain of certain premises. This passage refutes that by showing that certain self-evident premises exist, ending the regress.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims that if demonstration requires all premises to be certain, then each premise needs a proof, leading to infinite regress.
Find the phrase 'وإلا لتسلسل' in the Arabic.
2
Find the commentator's refutation
The commentator denies that infinite regress is necessary. He points out that there are self-evident premises that are certain by themselves.
Look for 'بل ههنا مقدمات يقينية بنفسها'.
3
Understand the role of self-evident premises
These premises are foundational; they do not require proof. Demonstration ends with them, stopping the regress.
This is a key concept in Islamic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما هو مستعمل في العلوم ليس برهاناً، وإلا لتسلسل، فإن مقدماته يجب أن تكون يقينية؛ إذ لا يقين من غير اليقينيات، وهكذا ويتسلسل. ومنهم من اعترف بالبرهان، وقال: يستدل على مقدماته بالدور بأن يحصل المطلوب من مقدماته، ويحصل تلك المقدمات بمقدمات أخر، ثم يرجع ويثبت تلك المقدمات بأصل المطلوب فلا تسلسل، ويلزمه المصادرة على المطلوب الأول والتقدم على نفسه؛ لأن موقوف الموقوف موقوف. وهذا كله إنما نشأ من ظنهم الكاسد أن اليقين لا يحصل إلا من البرهان، بل ههنا مقدمات يقينية بنفسها ينتهي إليها البرهان، نعم؛ العلم اليقيني بالنظريات لا يحصل إلا بالبرهان.
And what is used in the sciences is not a demonstration, otherwise there would be infinite regress; for its premises must be certain, since there is no certainty from non-certainties, and so on ad infinitum. And among them is one who admitted demonstration and said: one infers its premises by circularity: that the desired conclusion is obtained from its premises, and those premises are obtained from other premises, then one returns and proves those premises by the original desired conclusion, so there is no infinite regress. But this entails begging the first question and priority over oneself, because what is dependent on what is dependent is dependent. All this arose from their corrupt assumption that certainty is only obtained from demonstration; rather, there are here self-evident certain premises to which demonstration ends. Yes, certain knowledge of theoretical matters is only obtained by demonstration.
Source pages p. 871

Commentary
This passage critiques a common skeptical argument against demonstration. The objector claims that if we require all premises to be certain, then we need a proof for every premise, leading to infinite regress. Some attempted to solve this by circular reasoning (proof of premises by the conclusion), but the commentator points out this simply begs the question. The core problem is the false belief that certainty can only come from demonstration. In reality, there are self-evident premises (al-awwaliyat) that are certain by themselves and serve as foundations for demonstration. Thus theoretical knowledge does require demonstration, but it ultimately rests on these foundational certainties. This passage guards against the misunderstanding that all knowledge requires proof or that infinite regress is inevitable.

Key Terms
تسلسل
برهان
مقدمات يقينية بنفسها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is infinite regress not a problem for demonstration?
A) Because we can use circular reasoning to avoid it.
B) Because there are self-evident premises that are certain in themselves.
C) Because demonstration does not require certainty.
D) Because the regress ends with empirical observation.

Key Takeaway
Infinite regress in demonstration is avoided by the existence of self-evident certain premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Fallacy of Circular Reasoning (Dawr) in Demonstration
Commentator refutes circular reasoning as a solution to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify circular reasoning in arguments about premises and explain why it is fallacious.

Core Idea
Circular reasoning (dawr) does not solve regress; it begs the question.

Some attempted to avoid infinite regress by using circular reasoning: proving premises by the conclusion and vice versa. The commentator reveals why this is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the circular reasoning attempt
The passage describes someone who says premises are proven by other premises, and then those premises are proven by the original conclusion, creating a circle.
Find 'بالدور' in the Arabic.
2
Understand the commentator's objection
The commentator points out that this circular reasoning simply begs the question (المصادرة على المطلوب) and implies priority over oneself (التقدم على نفسه).
'موقوف الموقوف موقوف' means the dependent of the dependent is still dependent.
3
Apply to another example
In Fiqh, circular reasoning occurs if you prove the authority of the Prophet by the Quran and the Quran by the Prophet without external evidence.
This is a common fallacy in legal theory.

Source Text

Self-Evident Premises as Foundations of Certainty
Commentator offers self-evident premises as the solution to the regress problem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the role of self-evident premises in demonstration and provide an example from Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Self-evident premises are the bedrock of demonstration, providing certainty without proof.

The commentator asserts that there are premises certain by themselves. This lesson explores what they are and how they function in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the commentator's positive claim
The commentator says: 'بل ههنا مقدمات يقينية بنفسها ينتهي إليها البرهان' (Rather, there are here self-evident certain premises to which demonstration ends).
This is the solution to the regress problem.
2
Understand what makes premises self-evident
Self-evident premises (al-awwaliyat) are such that once you understand the terms, you immediately assent to them. Examples: 'The whole is greater than its part', 'The same thing cannot be both true and false'.
These are foundational in any science.
3
Apply to a theological argument
In Kalam, the premise 'The world is temporally originated' is argued through self-evident observations of change. This premise then serves in proving God's existence.
Thus, the argument does not regress because it starts with self-evident truths.

The Syllogism
