Observations: Sense and Reason
Author defines observations as propositions judged by sense perception, with rational verification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-mushahadat and explain why sense perception alone is insufficient for certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Sense perception alone is not enough; observations are verified by both sense and reason.

The passage defines the second category of certain propositions: observations. These are not merely sense impressions; they require rational scrutiny to exclude illusions. Commentators clarify that sense alone yields only particular judgments, and reason must confirm to avoid mistakes like taking a mirage for water. This refines our understanding of what counts as reliable sensory knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and initial definition
Find the Arabic term 'المشاهدات' and its initial characterization as 'المحسوسات' i.e., propositions judged by sense. Note the equation with sensory things.
2
Analyze Sharh al-Mawaqif's refinement
Notice that sense alone yields only a particular judgment (e.g., 'this fire is hot'), not a universal. This shows the limitation of raw sense.
Particular judgments are insufficient for universal knowledge.
3
Extract the condition from Sullam al-Ulum
Read the statement that not every sensory assent is an observation; it requires the mind's judgment and acceptance. Reason must verify.
This condition excludes sensory errors.
4
Apply the condition to the counterexample
The mirage example (قولنا للسراب إنه ماء) is a sensory error. Without reason, it would be counted as an observation. Confirm that it is excluded.
5
Formulate the proper definition
Synthesize: al-mushahadat are propositions obtained by sense and accepted by reason. This distinguishes true observations from illusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمشاهدات: وهي المحسوسات؛ أي: القضايا التي يحكم فيها بالحس. قال في شرح المواقف: اعلم أن الحس لا يفيد إلا حكما جزئيا كقولنا: هذه النار حارة. وقال شارح سلم العلوم: ثم ليس كل تصديق يحصل بالحواس من المشاهدات، بل لا بد فيها من حكم العقل أيضا وقبوله، وإلا لكان قولنا للسراب إنه ماء من المشاهدات، وكذا سائر أغلاط الوهم والحس.
And observations: they are sensory things; i.e., propositions about which one judges by sense. He said in Sharh al-Mawaqif: Know that sense does not yield except a particular judgment like our statement: This fire is hot. And the commentator of Sullam al-Ulum said: Then not every assent obtained by the senses is among the observations; rather, it requires also the judgment of the mind and its acceptance, otherwise our statement about a mirage that it is water would be among the observations, and likewise all the other errors of illusion and sense.
Source pages p. 872

Commentary
This passage defines the second category: observations (al-mushahadat), which are propositions based on sense perception. The author equates them with 'sensory things' (al-mahsusat). The commentary from Sharh al-Mawaqif clarifies that sense perception alone yields only a particular judgment (e.g., 'This fire is hot'), not a universal. Furthermore, the commentator from Sullam al-Ulum notes that not every perceptual belief qualifies as an observation; reason must also verify to avoid errors like mistaking a mirage for water. Thus the cognitive operation involves both sense and intellect, preventing the inclusion of fallacious sensory impressions.

Checkpoint
Why is the statement 'That mirage is water' not considered a true observation (mushahada)?
A) Because it is a universal judgment.
B) Because it is rejected by the intellect.
C) Because it is based only on sense without rational verification.
D) Because it is an example of a primary.

Key Takeaway
Observations require both sense perception and rational judgment to count as certain knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Evaluate the statement 'The sun is eclipsed' as a potential mushahada. What rational verification would be needed to confirm it as a true observation?
?
In hadith criticism, a narrator claims to have seen a miraculous event. Using the definition of mushahadat, explain why reason must also assess the report for consistency with other established knowledge.
